Microbiology

Complete edition · all 15 chapters · by Dr. Vidya Aggrawal · Nightingale College
Microbiology · Chapter 1

The Microbial World & Why It Matters to Nursing

You are outnumbered. For every human cell you carry, there is roughly one bacterial cell living on or inside you — and the vast majority are harmless, even helpful. Microbiology is the study of that invisible world: the bacteria, viruses, fungi, and parasites that share your body and, occasionally, make your patients sick. This chapter introduces the cast of characters, explains the difference between a microbe that lives with you and one that harms you, tells the story of how we figured out that germs cause disease, and shows you how those germs are named. Everything else in this course — and much of what you will do at the bedside to keep patients safe — grows out of these first ideas.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Define microbiology and identify the major groups of microorganisms.
  • Distinguish cellular from acellular microbes, and prokaryotic from eukaryotic cells.
  • Explain the human microbiome and contrast normal flora, opportunistic pathogens, and true pathogens.
  • Distinguish colonization, infection, and disease, and explain why infection prevention is central to nursing.
  • Summarize the germ theory of disease and the contributions of Semmelweis, Pasteur, and Koch.
  • State Koch's postulates and what they are for.
  • Correctly write and read a microbe's binomial name and place organisms in the three-domain system.

Section 1Meet the microbes

What counts as a microbe

A microorganism (or microbe) is simply a living thing too small to see with the naked eye — you need a microscope. That single size rule sweeps together a wildly diverse group of organisms that are otherwise unrelated. Learning microbiology begins with sorting that group into a handful of families, because the family a microbe belongs to tells you almost everything that matters clinically: how it is built, how it grows, what disease it causes, and — crucially — which drugs will kill it.

The main groups

Microbes split first into cellular organisms (made of one or more true cells) and acellular agents (not cells at all). Among the cellular microbes, the deepest divide is between prokaryotes — small, simple cells with no nucleus — and eukaryotes, larger cells that keep their DNA in a membrane-bound nucleus, just like your own cells do.

GroupCell typeKey featuresExample disease
BacteriaProkaryoteSingle-celled, no nucleus; enormous variety; many are our normal floraStrep throat, UTIs, TB
ArchaeaProkaryoteProkaryotes of extreme environments; none are known to cause human disease—
FungiEukaryoteYeasts (single-celled) and molds (filamentous)Thrush (Candida), ringworm
ProtozoaEukaryoteSingle-celled animals; often motile; many are parasitesMalaria, giardiasis
HelminthsEukaryote (animal)Parasitic worms — not micro, but their eggs/larvae are, and they're studied herePinworm, tapeworm
VirusesAcellularGenetic material in a protein coat; must hijack a host cell to replicateInfluenza, COVID-19, HIV

Keep the prokaryote–eukaryote line firmly in mind, because it is the reason many drugs are safe to give: an antibiotic that attacks a structure found only in bacterial (prokaryotic) cells — a cell wall, a bacterial ribosome — can wipe out the infection while leaving your patient's eukaryotic cells untouched. That principle, called selective toxicity, is the whole reason antimicrobial therapy is possible.

Antibiotics do nothing to a virus. Antibiotics target features of bacterial cells. A virus is acellular — it has none of those targets — so antibiotics are useless against the flu or a common cold. Prescribing them anyway doesn't help the patient and it breeds resistance, which is exactly why antibiotic stewardship (Chapter 9) is a nursing concern.

Tap through the six groups of microbes below.

Interactive Meet the microbes Open full ↑
Key points
  • A microbe is any organism too small to see unaided.
  • Cellular microbes are bacteria, archaea, fungi, protozoa (plus helminths by tradition); viruses are acellular.
  • Prokaryotes (bacteria, archaea) have no nucleus; eukaryotes (fungi, protozoa) do.
  • Selective toxicity — hitting a microbial-only target — is why antimicrobial drugs can cure without poisoning the patient.

Section 2Microbes on and in us

The microbiome: your resident microbes

You are not sterile, and you were never meant to be. Trillions of microbes — collectively the microbiota, and together with their genes the microbiome — colonize your skin, mouth, gut, and other surfaces from the moment you are born. These normal flora are not freeloaders. They earn their keep: gut bacteria synthesize vitamin K and some B vitamins, they crowd out invaders by occupying space and consuming nutrients (competitive exclusion), and they help train the developing immune system to tell friend from foe.

Flora come in two flavors. Resident flora live with you long-term and re-establish themselves if disturbed. Transient flora are just passing through — picked up from a doorknob or a patient's bedrail — and this is precisely the population that hand hygiene is designed to remove before it moves from one patient to the next.

When a good microbe goes bad

Many of the organisms that cause healthcare infections are not exotic invaders at all — they are the patient's own normal flora, turned dangerous by opportunity. An opportunistic pathogen causes no trouble in its usual home but causes disease when it is moved somewhere new, when the normal flora are wiped out, or when the host's defenses fall. Escherichia coli is harmless in the colon but causes a urinary tract infection if it reaches the bladder; Candida overgrows into thrush when antibiotics clear its bacterial competitors; and Clostridioides difficile blooms into severe colitis after antibiotics flatten the gut's normal community.

What normal flora doWhy it matters
Make vitamin K & some B vitaminsNutrition; K is needed for clotting
Competitive exclusion of pathogensThey physically and chemically crowd out invaders
Train the immune systemEarly exposure calibrates immune responses
Maintain low pH / hostile nichese.g., vaginal lactobacilli keep pH low, blocking pathogens
Antibiotics can cause infection. Broad-spectrum antibiotics don't just kill the target — they clear-cut the microbiome, opening the door for C. difficile and Candida. That is why new-onset diarrhea in a patient on antibiotics is a red flag, and why we don't reach for antibiotics lightly.

Explore where your normal flora live.

Interactive Microbiome map Open full ↑
Key points
  • The microbiome is the community of microbes that normally live on and in the body.
  • Normal flora are mostly beneficial — nutrients, competitive exclusion, immune training.
  • Opportunistic pathogens are normal flora that cause disease when displaced, unopposed, or the host is weakened.
  • Removing transient flora is the entire point of hand hygiene.

Section 3When microbes cause disease

Colonization, infection, disease

These three words are often used loosely in conversation, but in the clinical world they mean different things, and confusing them leads to over-treatment. Colonization means a microbe is simply present and multiplying on a body surface without causing harm — the patient has no symptoms. Infection means a microbe has invaded and the body is now responding to it. Disease is the step further, when that infection produces recognizable signs and symptoms and impairs function.

The distinction is intensely practical. A wound swab growing bacteria in a patient with no redness, warmth, or fever usually reflects colonization, not infection — treating it with antibiotics does harm without benefit. A pathogen, meanwhile, is any microbe capable of causing disease, and its virulence is the degree of harm it can do (we unpack the machinery of virulence in Chapter 10).

StateMicrobe present?Host responding?Symptoms?
ColonizationYes (multiplying)NoNo
InfectionYes (invading)YesNot necessarily
DiseaseYesYesYes — signs & symptoms

Why this is a nursing problem

Infections are among the most common and most preventable threats a hospitalized patient faces. Healthcare-associated infections (HAIs) — catheter-associated UTIs, central-line bloodstream infections, surgical-site infections, ventilator-associated pneumonia — affect roughly one in every thirty-one hospital patients on any given day. The nurse is the person at the bedside most often, which makes the nurse the single most important line of defense: every hand wash, every sterile field, every catheter removed a day earlier prevents an infection that would otherwise have happened.

The most powerful drug in the building is soap. No antibiotic, no isolation gown, and no technology prevents more infections than clean hands between patients. Everything in the coming chapters — how microbes spread, how we kill them, how the immune system fights — is in service of that bedside habit.

Step through the three clinical states below.

Interactive Colonization to disease Open full ↑
Key points
  • Colonization (present, no harm) ≠ infection (invading, host responding) ≠ disease (symptoms & impairment).
  • A pathogen can cause disease; virulence is how much harm it does.
  • HAIs are common and largely preventable, and the nurse is the front line of prevention.

Section 4How we found out microbes cause disease

From bad air to germ theory

For most of history, disease was blamed on miasma — bad air — or on spontaneous generation, the idea that life simply arose from non-living matter. The germ theory of disease, the recognition that specific microbes cause specific diseases, is only about 150 years old, and it is the intellectual foundation of everything nurses do to prevent infection.

Three people worth knowing

Ignaz Semmelweis, a Viennese obstetrician in the 1840s, noticed that mothers died of childbed fever far more often on the ward staffed by doctors (who came straight from autopsies) than on the ward staffed by midwives. He ordered handwashing with a chlorine solution, and the death rate plummeted. He could not explain why it worked — germ theory did not yet exist — and his colleagues ridiculed and rejected him. He is now remembered as the father of hand hygiene.

Louis Pasteur disproved spontaneous generation with his elegant swan-neck flask experiments, showing that broth stayed sterile until microbes from the air reached it. He gave us pasteurization and helped establish that microbes drive fermentation and spoilage. Robert Koch then supplied the rigor: a systematic way to prove that one particular microbe causes one particular disease, now called Koch's postulates.

Koch's postulateIn plain English
1The microbe is found in every case of the disease, and absent in healthy hosts
2It can be isolated from a sick host and grown in pure culture
3The cultured microbe causes the same disease when introduced into a healthy host
4The same microbe can be re-isolated from that newly diseased host
Semmelweis was right and still ignored. The tragedy of Semmelweis — correct, evidence in hand, and dismissed for decades — is a permanent reminder that good data only saves lives if people act on it. Modern hand-hygiene campaigns exist because the lesson had to be learned twice.

Walk through Koch's four-step proof below.

Interactive Koch's postulates Open full ↑
Key points
  • Germ theory — specific microbes cause specific diseases — is the basis of all infection control.
  • Semmelweis = hand hygiene; Pasteur = disproved spontaneous generation, pasteurization; Koch = postulates linking microbe to disease.
  • Koch's postulates are the logical test for proving a given microbe causes a given disease.

Section 5Naming and classifying microbes

The three domains

Every living organism belongs to one of three great domains, a scheme worked out by Carl Woese from differences in ribosomal RNA. Two domains, Bacteria and Archaea, are prokaryotes. The third, Eukarya, holds everything with a nucleus — protozoa, fungi, plants, animals, and you. Notice where the microbes land: bacteria are their own domain, archaea are a second, and the microbial eukaryotes (fungi and protozoa) sit alongside us in the third. Viruses, being acellular, are not placed on this tree of life at all.

Binomial names, and how to read them

Every organism gets a two-part Latin name — binomial nomenclature, a system from Carl Linnaeus. The first word is the genus (capitalized), the second is the species (lowercase), and the whole name is italicized: Staphylococcus aureus, Escherichia coli, Clostridioides difficile. After the full name is written once, it is abbreviated to the genus initial: S. aureus, E. coli, C. difficile (or the familiar "C. diff"). Getting this right matters at the bedside, because a chart, a culture result, and a handoff report all assume you can read these names fluently.

DomainCell typeMembersHuman disease?
BacteriaProkaryoteTrue bacteriaYes — most bacterial pathogens
ArchaeaProkaryoteExtremophiles & othersNo known pathogens
EukaryaEukaryoteProtozoa, fungi, plants, animalsYes — fungi, protozoa, helminths
Read a microbe's name and you already know something. "Staphylococcus" tells you it is a cluster-forming coccus (round) bacterium before you have read another word. Genus names are packed with clues about shape, arrangement, staining, or discoverer — a habit worth building now.

Decode a few real microbe names below.

Interactive Name that microbe Open full ↑
Key points
  • All life falls into three domains: Bacteria, Archaea (both prokaryotic), and Eukarya.
  • Viruses are acellular and are not on the tree of life.
  • Binomial names are Genus species, italicized, abbreviated to G. species after first use.

The chapter in one breath

  • Microbes are organisms too small to see: bacteria, archaea, fungi, protozoa, helminths, and (acellular) viruses.
  • The prokaryote/eukaryote divide underlies selective toxicity — why antimicrobials can cure without harming the patient — and antibiotics do nothing to viruses.
  • The microbiome of normal flora is mostly beneficial; opportunistic pathogens are normal flora that harm when displaced or unopposed.
  • Colonization ≠ infection ≠ disease; a pathogen causes disease and virulence is how much harm it does.
  • HAIs are common and preventable, and the nurse's hand hygiene is the most powerful infection-control tool there is.
  • Germ theory (Semmelweis, Pasteur, Koch) grounds all infection control; Koch's postulates prove a microbe causes a disease.
  • Life sorts into three domains; microbes are named with italic binomial names abbreviated after first use.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Acellular
Not composed of cells; describes viruses, viroids, and prions.
Archaea
A domain of prokaryotes distinct from bacteria; none are known to cause human disease.
Bacteria
A domain of single-celled prokaryotes; the source of most bacterial infections and much of our normal flora.
Binomial nomenclature
The two-part naming system (italic Genus species) used for every organism.
Colonization
The presence and multiplication of a microbe on a body surface without harm or symptoms.
Domain
The highest level of classification: Bacteria, Archaea, or Eukarya.
Disease
An infection that produces recognizable signs and symptoms and impairs function.
Eukaryote
A cell (or organism) whose DNA is enclosed in a membrane-bound nucleus; includes fungi and protozoa.
Germ theory of disease
The principle that specific microorganisms cause specific diseases.
Healthcare-associated infection (HAI)
An infection a patient acquires while receiving care, e.g., CAUTI, CLABSI, or surgical-site infection.
Infection
Invasion of body tissues by a microbe and the host's response to it.
Koch's postulates
Four criteria used to establish that a particular microbe causes a particular disease.
Microbiome
The community of microbes normally living on and in the body, together with their genes.
Microorganism (microbe)
Any living organism too small to see without a microscope.
Normal flora (microbiota)
The resident microbes that normally inhabit a healthy body, mostly beneficial.
Opportunistic pathogen
A normally harmless microbe that causes disease when displaced, unopposed, or the host is weakened.
Pathogen
A microbe capable of causing disease.
Prokaryote
A cell (or organism) with no membrane-bound nucleus; bacteria and archaea.
Selective toxicity
Harming a microbe while sparing the host by targeting a structure the microbe has and the host lacks.
Virulence
The degree of harm (pathogenicity) a microbe is capable of causing.

Check yourself

1. A microbiology student groups organisms into "cellular" and "acellular." Which major group is acellular, and why does that classification have direct consequences for treatment?
Viruses are acellular. Because a virus is not a cell, it lacks the bacterial structures (cell wall, bacterial ribosomes) that antibiotics attack — so antibiotics are useless against viral illnesses like the flu, and prescribing them only drives resistance.
2. Explain "selective toxicity" and why it is the reason antimicrobial therapy is possible.
Selective toxicity is harming the microbe while sparing the patient by targeting a structure the microbe has but human cells don't. Because bacteria are prokaryotes with unique features (a cell wall, distinct ribosomes), a drug can destroy them without poisoning the patient's eukaryotic cells.
3. Give two ways the normal gut flora actively protect a healthy person.
Any two: synthesize vitamin K and some B vitamins; competitively exclude pathogens by occupying space and consuming nutrients; help train/calibrate the immune system. This is why wiping out the flora with antibiotics can backfire.
4. A patient on broad-spectrum antibiotics develops new watery diarrhea. Explain the mechanism in terms of the microbiome, and name the classic culprit.
The antibiotics killed off the normal gut flora, removing competitive exclusion and letting an opportunist overgrow — classically Clostridioides difficile (C. diff). New-onset diarrhea on antibiotics is a red flag for exactly this.
5. Distinguish colonization, infection, and disease. Why does confusing them lead to over-treatment?
Colonization = microbe present, no host response, no symptoms; infection = microbe invading and host responding; disease = infection with signs, symptoms, and impaired function. A colonized wound or a positive swab in an asymptomatic patient is often not an infection, so treating it with antibiotics causes harm (side effects, resistance) without benefit.
6. Define pathogen and virulence, and state how they differ.
A pathogen is a microbe capable of causing disease; virulence is the degree of harm it can cause. Two pathogens can both cause disease while differing greatly in virulence.
7. What is an opportunistic pathogen? Give a specific example with its mechanism.
A normally harmless microbe that causes disease when moved, unopposed, or the host is weakened. Example: E. coli is harmless in the colon but causes a UTI when it reaches the bladder; or Candida causes thrush when antibiotics remove its bacterial competitors.
8. Why is the bedside nurse described as the single most important line of defense against healthcare-associated infections?
The nurse has the most frequent, closest contact with the patient, so nursing actions — hand hygiene, sterile technique, timely device removal — prevent the most transmission. Hand hygiene between patients removes the transient flora that spread HAIs.
9. What did Ignaz Semmelweis discover, and why is his story still told in infection-control training?
He showed that handwashing with a chlorine solution dramatically cut deaths from childbed fever — the birth of hand hygiene. His correct, evidence-backed finding was ridiculed and ignored for decades, a lasting lesson that data only saves lives when people act on it.
10. State the purpose of Koch's postulates and summarize the first postulate.
They provide a logical test to prove that a specific microbe causes a specific disease. The first postulate: the microbe is present in every case of the disease and absent from healthy individuals.
11. Name the three domains of life, say which are prokaryotic, and explain why viruses appear on none of them.
Bacteria, Archaea (both prokaryotic), and Eukarya (eukaryotic). Viruses are acellular — not living cells — so they are not placed on the cellular tree of life.
12. Rewrite this correctly and explain each rule: "staphylococcus Aureus," first mention in a note.
Staphylococcus aureus — genus capitalized, species lowercase, whole name italicized; after first use it becomes S. aureus. Binomial names are always Genus species, italic, with the genus abbreviated to its initial once the full name has appeared.
Microbiology · Chapter 2

Microscopy, Staining & the Microbiology Lab

The organisms you spent Chapter 1 meeting share one inconvenient trait: not one of them can be seen with the naked eye. Every practical thing microbiology does — identifying the bug in a wound, choosing the right antibiotic, confirming that a sterilizer worked — begins with a way to make the invisible visible. This chapter is about that toolkit: the units we use to measure things a thousand times smaller than a grain of salt, the microscope that magnifies them, the dyes that give them color and reveal their secrets, and the lab workflow that turns a swab from your patient into a culture-and-sensitivity report you can act on. Master this chapter and a lab result stops being a mystery and starts being a set of instructions.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • State the metric units used to measure microbes and place bacteria, viruses, and human cells on the size scale.
  • Explain why the unaided eye cannot resolve a bacterium.
  • Name the major parts of a compound light microscope and describe what each does.
  • Distinguish magnification from resolution, and explain the role of the oil-immersion lens.
  • Explain why cells must be stained, and contrast simple, differential, and negative stains.
  • Walk through the four steps of the Gram stain and explain what makes a cell Gram-positive or Gram-negative.
  • Trace a specimen from collection through culture, colony, identification, and sensitivity testing.
  • Describe the nurse's role in specimen collection and in reading a culture-and-sensitivity report.

Section 1Why microbes are invisible

The metric ladder

Microbiology runs on the metric system, and the whole subject makes more sense once the units are second nature. Start with the meter and divide by a thousand at each step: a millimeter (mm) is a thousandth of a meter, a micrometer (µm, sometimes called a micron) is a thousandth of a millimeter, and a nanometer (nm) is a thousandth of a micrometer. Each rung down is 1000 times smaller than the one above it. You already live at the millimeter scale — the smallest lines on a ruler are about a millimeter apart. Microbes live one or two rungs below that, in a world your eye was never built to enter.

How big is a microbe, really?

A useful benchmark is the red blood cell, roughly 7 µm across. Most bacteria are smaller still — a typical rod is about 0.5 µm wide and 2–3 µm long, and a round coccus is around 1 µm. Yeasts and protozoa, being eukaryotes, run larger, from several to tens of micrometers. Viruses are in a different league altogether, measured in nanometers: most fall between 20 and 300 nm, so hundreds of them could line up across a single bacterium. This is why viruses were invisible even to early microscopists and why we needed the electron microscope to see them at all.

UnitSizeWhat lives here
Millimeter (mm)1/1000 of a meterThe smallest thing the naked eye resolves (about 0.1 mm)
Micrometer (µm)1/1000 of a mmHuman cells (~10–100 µm), red blood cells (~7 µm), bacteria (~1–3 µm)
Nanometer (nm)1/1000 of a µmViruses (~20–300 nm), large molecules, ribosomes

Why your eye can't see them

The human eye can separate two points only if they are roughly 0.1 mm (100 µm) apart — anything closer blurs into a single dot. A bacterium at 1 µm is about a hundred times below that limit, so it simply cannot register, no matter how good your eyesight or how bright the light. This is not a matter of things being faint or far away; it is a hard physical limit called resolution, and defeating it is the entire job of the microscope you will meet in the next section.

Scale explains why cultures take time. You cannot see a single bacterium, but you can see the pile of a billion of them that grows overnight into a visible colony. That jump from invisible to visible is why a wound culture doesn't come back in five minutes — the lab is waiting for the organism to multiply into something the eye, and the tests, can work with.

Interactive Drag the zoom slider from millimeters to nanometers to see why a virus, a bacterium, and a human cell are all invisible. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Metric ladder: 1 mm = 1000 µm, and 1 µm = 1000 nm — each step is 1000× smaller.
  • Most bacteria are ~1–3 µm; viruses are ~20–300 nm; a red blood cell (~7 µm) is a handy benchmark.
  • The naked eye resolves only to about 0.1 mm — roughly 100× too coarse to see a bacterium.
  • Because microbes are invisible individually, the lab grows them into visible colonies before it can work with them.

Section 2The light microscope

The parts and what they do

The workhorse of every clinical lab is the compound light microscope — "compound" because it uses two lens systems in series to build up its magnifying power. Light starts at the illuminator in the base and travels upward. The condenser gathers and focuses that light onto the specimen, and the iris diaphragm adjusts how much passes through, which controls contrast. The specimen sits on the stage. Above it, the objective lenses — mounted on a rotating nosepiece — do the first stage of magnifying, and the ocular (eyepiece) you look through does the second. The coarse and fine focus knobs raise and lower the stage until the image is sharp.

Magnification: stacking two lenses

Total magnification is simply the ocular multiplied by the objective. Oculars are almost always 10×. Objectives come in a standard set: scanning (4×), low power (10×), high-dry (40×), and oil-immersion (100×). So with the 10× ocular, the oil-immersion objective gives 10 × 100 = 1000× total magnification — enough to see bacterial shape and arrangement clearly. You almost always find your target on a low-power objective first, center it, then rotate up to higher power.

ObjectiveObjective powerWith 10× ocularTypical use
Scanning4×40×Find the specimen, survey the slide
Low power10×100×Larger cells, general orientation
High-dry40×400×Fungi, cells, parasites
Oil immersion100×1000×Bacteria — Gram stains, cell arrangement

Resolution is what really matters

Beginners chase magnification, but the property that decides whether an image is useful is resolution — the smallest distance between two points that still lets you see them as separate. Magnifying a blurry image just gives you a bigger blur (lab staff call this empty magnification). The resolution of a light microscope is capped near 0.2 µm by the wavelength of visible light itself — you cannot resolve two things closer than about half a wavelength apart. That 0.2 µm limit is good enough to see bacteria but not viruses, which is exactly why viruses need an electron microscope. To squeeze the best resolution out of the 100× lens, we add a drop of immersion oil between the slide and the objective. Oil has the same light-bending (refractive) index as glass, so it keeps light rays from scattering away as they leave the slide, funneling more of them into the lens and sharpening the image.

Never skip the oil, and never drag a dry lens through it. The 100× objective is designed to work in oil; used dry, the image is dim and unresolved. And the 40× high-dry lens should never be swung into a pool of oil — it isn't sealed for it. It's a small habit, but it's the difference between reading a Gram stain and squinting at mush.

Interactive Tap the microscope's parts, then dial the objective to watch magnification and resolution work Open full ↑
Key points
  • The compound light microscope uses two lenses; light travels illuminator → condenser → specimen → objective → ocular.
  • Total magnification = ocular × objective; the 10× ocular with the 100× oil lens gives 1000×.
  • Resolution (~0.2 µm for light) — not magnification — determines how much real detail you see.
  • Immersion oil matches the refractive index of glass, capturing more light rays and improving resolution at 100×.

Section 3Why we stain

The contrast problem

Living cells are mostly water and nearly transparent, so under an ordinary light microscope they are almost invisible against a bright background — there just isn't enough contrast to make out shape or structure. Staining solves this by coating the cell in colored dye. Before you can stain a bacterial slide, you make a smear: spread a thin film of the sample on a slide, let it air-dry, then pass it through a flame or warm it to heat-fix it. Fixing does three jobs — it kills the organisms, glues them to the glass so they don't rinse off, and preserves their shape. Most bacterial dyes are basic (positively charged) dyes — methylene blue, crystal violet, safranin — because the bacterial cell surface carries a negative charge that attracts them.

Simple stains

A simple stain uses a single dye to add color and contrast. It tells you a cell's size, shape, and arrangement — whether the organism is a round coccus or a rod-shaped bacillus, and whether it forms clusters, chains, or pairs — but nothing more. It's fast and it's the right tool when all you need is to confirm that organisms are present and see their basic morphology.

Differential and special stains

A differential stain uses more than one reagent to divide bacteria into groups based on how they react. The two you must know are the Gram stain (the entire next section) and the acid-fast stain, which singles out the waxy-walled organisms like Mycobacterium tuberculosis that a Gram stain misses. Special stains go after one specific structure — capsule stains, endospore stains, flagella stains. Many of these use negative staining, in which an acidic dye like India ink colors the background and leaves the cell unstained and glowing against it. Negative staining skips heat-fixing, so it doesn't distort delicate cells or shrink a capsule, which is why it's the go-to for capsules and for fragile organisms.

Stain typeReagentsWhat it tells youExample
SimpleOne dyeSize, shape, arrangementMethylene blue
DifferentialTwo or more reagentsSorts cells into groupsGram stain, acid-fast stain
SpecialStructure-specificReveals one featureCapsule, endospore, flagella
NegativeAcidic dye (stains background)Shape without distortion; capsulesIndia ink, nigrosin
A stain is a lab shortcut, but the smear kills the bug. Heat-fixing destroys the organism, so a stained slide is never a live culture — it's a snapshot. That's why the lab keeps the plate growing separately: the Gram stain gives a fast preliminary read while the living culture goes on to identification and sensitivity testing.

Interactive Compare an unstained smear, a simple stain, and a differential Gram stain to see why contrast is needed Open full ↑
Key points
  • Cells are nearly transparent; staining adds the contrast needed to see shape and structure.
  • A smear is air-dried and heat-fixed — killing the organism, fixing it to the slide, and preserving shape.
  • Simple stains (one dye) show morphology; differential stains (multiple reagents) sort cells into groups.
  • Negative staining colors the background and avoids heat, so it's ideal for capsules and fragile cells.

Section 4The Gram stain

What makes a cell Gram-positive or Gram-negative

The Gram stain, devised by Hans Christian Gram in 1884, is the single most important stain in clinical microbiology, and it works because of a real difference in the bacterial cell wall. Gram-positive bacteria have a thick outer layer of peptidoglycan, a mesh-like polymer that acts like a sponge. Gram-negative bacteria have only a thin peptidoglycan layer, wrapped by an extra outer membrane. That structural difference is what the four reagents exploit: the thick wall traps the first dye, and the thin one lets it wash out.

The four steps

The sequence is always the same, and every step has a job. First, the primary stain crystal violet floods every cell purple. Second, Gram's iodine acts as a mordant — it binds the crystal violet into a large complex too big to slip out of a thick wall easily. Third comes the decolorizer, alcohol or an alcohol-acetone mix, and this is the step that actually separates the two groups: it dehydrates and tightens the thick Gram-positive wall so the dye complex stays locked in, but it dissolves the Gram-negative outer membrane and washes the dye straight out, leaving those cells colorless. Fourth, the pink counterstain safranin re-colors the now-colorless Gram-negative cells; the Gram-positive cells are already saturated with purple and don't change. The end result: Gram-positive = purple, Gram-negative = pink/red.

StepReagentRoleGram-positiveGram-negative
1Crystal violetPrimary stainPurplePurple
2Gram's iodineMordant (traps dye)PurplePurple
3Alcohol / acetoneDecolorizer (the key step)Stays purpleBecomes colorless
4SafraninCounterstainStays purpleTurns pink/red

Reading the result at the bedside

A Gram stain can be read in under an hour, long before a culture matures, so it is the first real clue to guide empiric antibiotics while the lab waits 24–48 hours for growth. The report pairs the Gram reaction with the shape and arrangement, and each combination narrows the field: Gram-positive cocci in clusters point to Staphylococcus; Gram-positive cocci in chains point to Streptococcus; Gram-negative rods suggest gut organisms like E. coli; Gram-negative diplococci raise Neisseria. That structural difference also has treatment consequences — the Gram-negative outer membrane blocks some antibiotics and carries endotoxin (Chapter 15), which is why Gram-negative bloodstream infections can be so dangerous.

The decolorizer is where Gram stains go wrong. Leave the alcohol on too long and even a Gram-positive cell surrenders its purple and reads as a false Gram-negative; rinse too briefly and Gram-negatives cling to the dye and read falsely positive. When a stain result doesn't fit the clinical picture, over- or under-decolorization is the usual culprit — another reason the living culture, not the stain alone, gets the final word.

Interactive Step through the four Gram-stain reagents and watch Gram-positive cells finish purple while Gram-negative cells finish pink Open full ↑
Key points
  • The Gram stain sorts bacteria by cell wall: thick peptidoglycan (Gram-positive) vs. thin peptidoglycan plus an outer membrane (Gram-negative).
  • Four steps: crystal violet → iodine mordant → alcohol decolorizer → safranin counterstain.
  • Decolorization is the make-or-break step that distinguishes the two groups.
  • Result: Gram-positive = purple, Gram-negative = pink; read in under an hour, it guides early antibiotic choice.

Section 5From specimen to answer

Collecting and culturing the specimen

Every lab result is only as good as the specimen it started from, and specimen collection is squarely a nursing responsibility. The rules are simple but unforgiving: sample the right site (the infection, not the surrounding skin), collect before antibiotics whenever possible, use the correct container and transport medium, and label it accurately at the bedside. Back in the lab, the sample is spread onto a culture medium — usually a nutrient agar plate (a solid jelly) or a liquid broth. Some media are chosen deliberately: selective media suppress unwanted organisms, and differential media (like blood agar or MacConkey) make different species look visibly different, giving the lab an early hint about what's growing.

The colony: an invisible bug made visible

To isolate a single organism, the lab uses a streak plate, dragging the sample thinner and thinner across the agar until individual cells are separated. Each isolated cell, left overnight in a warm incubator, divides again and again into a visible mound of millions of identical clones — a colony. Because every cell in a colony descends from one parent, a colony is a pure sample to work from. Its morphology — size, shape, color, texture, and whether it destroys the blood in blood agar (hemolysis) — is itself a first clue to the organism's identity. From there, biochemical panels, automated analyzers, or rapid MALDI-TOF mass spectrometry nail down the species.

Sensitivity testing and the antibiogram

Knowing the organism's name is only half the answer; the clinician also needs to know which antibiotics will kill it. That is sensitivity (or susceptibility) testing. In the classic Kirby-Bauer disk-diffusion method, small paper disks soaked with different antibiotics are placed on a lawn of the organism; where a drug works, it diffuses out and clears a zone of inhibition, and a larger zone generally means the organism is more susceptible to that drug (each zone is measured against standardized breakpoints, not sized up against the neighboring disk). Other methods measure the minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC) — the lowest drug concentration that stops growth — using broth microdilution or a gradient Etest strip. Results come back reported as S, I, or R (susceptible, intermediate, resistant). This is the report that lets the team de-escalate: start broad while waiting, then narrow to the most targeted, effective drug once the sensitivities return.

StageTypical timingWhat you learn
Gram stain< 1 hourGram reaction, shape, arrangement — guides empiric therapy
Colony growth18–48 hoursPreliminary identity from colony morphology
Identification & sensitivity48–72 hoursSpecies and the S/I/R susceptibility report
Cultures before antibiotics — always. Once a dose is on board, the organism may stop growing in the bottle and the culture can come back falsely negative, robbing the team of the ID and sensitivities that would have targeted therapy. Blood cultures in particular should be drawn before the first antibiotic dose. Getting the culture first is one of the highest-value things a nurse does in a suspected sepsis workup.

Interactive Step through the lab pathway — culture, colony, ID, sensitivity — and tap each antibiotic disk to read S/I/R Open full ↑
Key points
  • Good results start with good collection: right site, before antibiotics, correct container, accurate label.
  • A streak plate isolates single cells; each grows into a pure colony whose morphology hints at identity.
  • Sensitivity testing (Kirby-Bauer zones, or an MIC) reports each drug as S/I/R.
  • The culture-and-sensitivity report lets the team de-escalate from broad empiric therapy to a targeted drug.

The chapter in one breath

  • Microbes are measured in micrometers (bacteria, ~1–3 µm) and nanometers (viruses, ~20–300 nm); the naked eye stops at ~0.1 mm, so microbes are invisible individually.
  • The compound light microscope stacks two lenses; total magnification is ocular × objective, reaching 1000× with the oil lens.
  • Resolution, not magnification, sets useful detail; light microscopy bottoms out near 0.2 µm, and immersion oil sharpens the 100× image.
  • Cells are transparent, so we make a heat-fixed smear and stain it; simple stains show shape, differential stains sort cells into groups, and negative stains color the background.
  • The Gram stain's four steps (crystal violet, iodine, alcohol, safranin) exploit the cell wall: Gram-positive = purple, Gram-negative = pink, with decolorization as the key step.
  • A specimen becomes an answer through culture, a pure colony, species identification, and sensitivity testing reported as S/I/R.
  • Nurses own the front end (proper collection, cultures before antibiotics) and use the culture-and-sensitivity report to advocate for the right drug.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Acid-fast stain
A differential stain that identifies organisms with waxy cell walls, such as Mycobacterium tuberculosis, which a Gram stain does not detect well.
Agar
A solid gel derived from seaweed, used as the base of culture plates on which microbes are grown.
Antibiogram
The report of an organism's antibiotic susceptibilities, listing each drug as susceptible, intermediate, or resistant.
Colony
A visible mound of millions of genetically identical cells descended from a single original cell.
Compound light microscope
A microscope that uses two lens systems (ocular and objective) in series to magnify a specimen.
Counterstain
The second-color dye in a differential stain (safranin in the Gram stain) that colors cells the primary stain has left colorless.
Culture
The growth of microorganisms on or in a nutrient medium in the lab.
Decolorizer
The alcohol/acetone step of the Gram stain that removes dye from Gram-negative cells while Gram-positive cells retain it.
Differential stain
A staining procedure using two or more reagents to distinguish between groups of bacteria (e.g., Gram stain).
Fixation
Heat- or chemical-treating a smear to kill the organisms, adhere them to the slide, and preserve their shape before staining.
Gram stain
The key differential stain that sorts bacteria into Gram-positive (purple) and Gram-negative (pink) based on cell-wall structure.
Magnification
How much larger an image appears than the real object; ocular power multiplied by objective power.
Micrometer (µm)
One thousandth of a millimeter; the unit used to measure most bacteria and eukaryotic cells.
Minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC)
The lowest concentration of an antibiotic that prevents visible growth of an organism.
Mordant
A substance (Gram's iodine) that binds a dye more firmly to the cell, trapping it in the wall.
Negative staining
Staining the background with an acidic dye so cells appear clear; avoids heat and preserves capsules and fragile cells.
Objective lens
The lens nearest the specimen (4×, 10×, 40×, 100×) that performs the first stage of magnification.
Oil immersion
Placing immersion oil between the slide and the 100× lens; the oil's refractive index matches glass, improving resolution.
Peptidoglycan
The mesh-like polymer of the bacterial cell wall; thick in Gram-positive cells, thin in Gram-negative cells.
Resolution
The smallest distance between two points at which they can still be seen as separate; ~0.2 µm for a light microscope.
Simple stain
A stain using a single dye to reveal a cell's size, shape, and arrangement.
Zone of inhibition
The clear ring around an antibiotic disk in disk-diffusion testing where the drug has stopped bacterial growth.

Check yourself

1. Put these in order from largest to smallest and give an approximate size for each: a virus, a bacterium, a red blood cell.
Red blood cell (~7 µm) > bacterium (~1–3 µm) > virus (~20–300 nm). Bacteria are measured in micrometers, viruses in nanometers — hundreds of viruses could line up across one bacterium.
2. Why can't the naked eye see an individual bacterium, and how does the lab get around this without a microscope on every plate?
The eye resolves only to about 0.1 mm (100 µm), roughly 100× too coarse for a 1-µm bacterium. The lab grows the invisible single cell into a visible colony of billions of clones, which the eye and the tests can work with.
3. A student sets a microscope to 400× total magnification. Which ocular and objective are in use?
A 10× ocular with the 40× high-dry objective (10 × 40 = 400×). Total magnification is always ocular power multiplied by objective power.
4. Explain the difference between magnification and resolution, and why "empty magnification" is a problem.
Magnification makes the image bigger; resolution is the smallest gap at which two points still look separate. Magnifying beyond the resolution limit (~0.2 µm for light) just enlarges a blur — empty magnification — adding size but no new detail.
5. What does immersion oil do, and why is it used only with the 100× objective?
Oil has the same refractive index as glass, so it keeps light rays from scattering away and funnels more of them into the lens, improving resolution. The 100× lens is built for oil; the 40× high-dry lens isn't sealed for it and shouldn't be dragged through oil.
6. Why must bacteria be stained to be studied, and what three things does heat-fixing accomplish?
Cells are nearly transparent and lack contrast; dye makes them visible. Heat-fixing kills the organisms, adheres them to the slide, and preserves their shape — which also means a stained slide is never a live culture.
7. Contrast a simple stain with a differential stain, and give an example of each.
A simple stain uses one dye and shows only size, shape, and arrangement (e.g., methylene blue); a differential stain uses multiple reagents to sort cells into groups (e.g., the Gram or acid-fast stain). Differential stains carry clinical grouping information that a simple stain can't.
8. When would you choose a negative stain over a positive (simple) stain?
When you need to preserve delicate structures — classically to demonstrate a capsule, or to view fragile cells — because negative staining colors the background and skips heat-fixing, avoiding distortion. India ink and nigrosin are typical negative stains.
9. List the four Gram-stain reagents in order and state the result for a Gram-negative cell after each.
Crystal violet (purple) → iodine mordant (purple) → alcohol decolorizer (colorless) → safranin counterstain (pink/red). Gram-negative cells lose the crystal violet at decolorization because alcohol dissolves their outer membrane and thin wall, then take up the pink counterstain.
10. Which step of the Gram stain most often produces a wrong result, and how does the error occur in each direction?
Decolorization is the critical, error-prone step. Over-decolorizing washes purple out of a true Gram-positive so it reads falsely Gram-negative; under-decolorizing leaves dye in a Gram-negative so it reads falsely Gram-positive.
11. A wound Gram stain reports "Gram-positive cocci in clusters." What organism does that suggest, and why is this result useful before the culture is finished?
Gram-positive cocci in clusters classically suggest Staphylococcus. The Gram stain is read in under an hour, so it guides empiric antibiotic choice during the 24–48 hours the culture needs to grow.
12. A patient with suspected sepsis is about to receive antibiotics. Why should blood cultures be drawn first, and what do disk-diffusion zones of inhibition tell the team?
Antibiotics can suppress growth in the bottle and cause a falsely negative culture, so cultures must be drawn before the first dose. In disk diffusion, a clear zone of inhibition around a drug disk means that antibiotic stops the organism — a larger zone generally reflects greater susceptibility to that drug — letting the team de-escalate to targeted therapy.
Microbiology · Chapter 3

Prokaryotic vs Eukaryotic Cells

Almost every microbe that concerns a nurse is a single cell — and that cell is built one of two ways. It is either a prokaryote, a small cell with its DNA loose in the cytoplasm, or a eukaryote, a larger cell that locks its DNA inside a nucleus, exactly like your own cells do. Bacteria are prokaryotes; fungi and protozoa are eukaryotes; your patient is made of eukaryotic cells. That single architectural split is not just trivia — it is the reason an antibiotic can flood a patient's bloodstream and destroy the bacteria while leaving the patient unharmed. This chapter takes a bacterial cell apart piece by piece, from the wall that gives it shape to the spore that lets it survive a decade on a bedrail, and shows you why each structure matters at the bedside.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Contrast prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells by size, nucleus, organelles, and ribosomes.
  • Explain how the prokaryote–eukaryote divide creates selective toxicity for antimicrobial drugs.
  • Describe the bacterial cell envelope — plasma membrane, cytoplasm, and nucleoid — and what each part does.
  • Explain the structure of peptidoglycan and contrast Gram-positive and Gram-negative cell walls.
  • Connect the cell wall and endotoxin (LPS) to how antibiotics work and to Gram-negative sepsis.
  • Describe the roles of the capsule, flagella, pili, and fimbriae in disease and gene transfer.
  • Explain how endospores form, why they resist heat and disinfectants, and what that means for infection control.

Section 1Two kinds of cell

The single deepest divide in biology

Of all the ways you could sort living cells, one line runs deeper than any other: does the cell keep its DNA inside a membrane-bound nucleus, or not? Cells that do not are prokaryotes (from Greek roots meaning "before the nut," the "nut" being the nucleus). Cells that do are eukaryotes ("true nut"). This is not a small stylistic difference — it comes with a whole package of other features, and knowing which side of the line a microbe sits on tells you how it is built, how fast it grows, and which drugs can touch it.

Prokaryotes are the bacteria and the archaea. They are small — typically 0.2 to 2 µm across — and simple, with their single circular chromosome floating loose in the cytoplasm. Eukaryotes — the fungi, protozoa, plants, animals, and every cell in your patient's body — are usually ten to a hundred times larger across and far more elaborate inside. When you say a patient has a "bacterial" infection versus a "fungal" one, you are really naming which kind of cell you are fighting.

What prokaryotes lack — and what they have

The headline is what prokaryotes are missing. They have no nucleus, and no other membrane-bound organelles either — no mitochondria, no endoplasmic reticulum, no Golgi apparatus. All of that chemistry happens either in the open cytoplasm or against the inner face of the plasma membrane. But "simple" does not mean "defenseless." Prokaryotes make up for the lack of internal compartments with tough external gear: most wear a rigid cell wall, many add a slimy capsule, and many carry flagella to swim and pili to stick — the structures the rest of this chapter is about.

One difference is easy to overlook but medically enormous: the ribosome. Both cell types build proteins on ribosomes, but the machines are not the same size. Prokaryotes use a 70S ribosome; eukaryotes use a larger 80S ribosome. That size gap is a gift to medicine, because a drug can be designed to jam the 70S machine while ignoring the 80S one.

Why the difference is a drug target

Here is the payoff. Because bacterial cells have structures your cells simply do not have — a peptidoglycan wall, a 70S ribosome, unique enzymes — a drug can attack those targets and destroy the bacterium while leaving the patient's eukaryotic cells alone. This principle is called selective toxicity, and it is the entire reason antibacterial therapy is possible. Penicillin blocks cell-wall building, which human cells never do. Many antibiotics latch onto the 70S ribosome, which human cells do not have. The wider the gap between microbe and host, the safer the drug.

FeatureProkaryote (bacteria)Eukaryote (fungi, protozoa, human)
NucleusAbsent — DNA free in nucleoidPresent — DNA in membrane-bound nucleus
Typical size0.2–2 µm10–100 µm
Membrane-bound organellesNoneMitochondria, ER, Golgi, etc.
DNA shapeUsually one circular chromosomeMultiple linear chromosomes
Ribosomes70S80S (cytoplasmic)
Cell wallUsually peptidoglycanFungi: chitin; protozoa: none; human: none
ReproductionBinary fissionMitosis / meiosis
“Antibiotic” means antibacterial. Because antibiotics exploit prokaryote-only targets, they do nothing to a fungus or a virus. A patient with oral thrush (Candida, a eukaryote) needs an antifungal, not amoxicillin — and giving an antibiotic will make thrush worse by clearing the competing bacteria. Match the drug class to the cell type.
Interactive Switch between a prokaryote and eukaryote and tap each structure to compare the two cell blueprints side by side Open full ↑
Key points
  • The deepest split among cells is prokaryote (no nucleus) vs eukaryote (nucleus + organelles).
  • Bacteria and archaea are prokaryotes; fungi, protozoa, and human cells are eukaryotes.
  • Prokaryotes are small, lack organelles, and use a 70S ribosome; eukaryotes use an 80S ribosome.
  • Prokaryote-only structures give antibiotics their selective toxicity — the reason they cure without poisoning the patient.

Section 2The bacterial cell envelope

The plasma membrane: the real boundary

Working from the outside in, the wall gives a bacterium its shape, but the plasma membrane is the layer that actually keeps the cell alive. It is a phospholipid bilayer — two sheets of fatty molecules with their water-loving heads facing out and their water-fearing tails tucked inside — peppered with proteins. It is selectively permeable, meaning it decides what crosses: nutrients in, wastes out, ions carefully balanced. Because prokaryotes have no mitochondria, the membrane also does a job your cells hand off to organelles: it runs the electron transport chain and generates the cell's energy right there in the membrane.

That makes the membrane a tempting drug target, but a risky one — bacterial and human membranes are chemically similar, so drugs that punch holes in membranes tend to be toxic. The polymyxins (colistin) do exactly this and are held in reserve as last-resort agents against highly resistant Gram-negative bacteria precisely because they can also injure the patient's kidneys.

Cytoplasm, ribosomes, and inclusions

Fill the membrane and you have the cytoplasm — a crowded, water-based gel where nearly all of the cell's chemistry happens. Suspended in it are millions of 70S ribosomes, the protein factories introduced in Section 1 and the target of whole families of antibiotics (aminoglycosides, tetracyclines, macrolides). Many bacteria also stockpile spare nutrients in inclusions — little granules of stored carbon, phosphate, or sulfur they can draw on when times are lean.

The nucleoid and plasmids

A prokaryote's DNA is not gone — it is just not gift-wrapped in a nucleus. The single, circular chromosome coils into a dense region called the nucleoid, which has no surrounding membrane, so the DNA sits in direct contact with the cytoplasm. This is one reason bacteria can grow so fast: transcription and translation happen in the same space, at the same time.

Many bacteria carry extra, much smaller loops of DNA called plasmids, separate from the chromosome. Plasmids are not usually needed for basic survival, but they often carry the genes that matter most to nurses: antibiotic-resistance genes, toxin genes, and the machinery to pass those genes to other bacteria. A plasmid can hop from one bacterium to another, which is how resistance spreads through a ward faster than any single organism could reproduce.

StructureWhat it isWhy it matters clinically
Plasma membranePhospholipid bilayer with proteinsControls transport; makes energy; polymyxin target
CytoplasmWater-based gel filling the cellSite of metabolism and growth
Ribosomes (70S)Protein-building machinesTarget of many antibiotics (selective toxicity)
NucleoidRegion holding the circular chromosomeNo membrane → fast, coupled gene expression
PlasmidSmall extra loop of DNACarries and spreads resistance & toxin genes
Resistance travels on plasmids. When a resistant organism shares a plasmid, it can hand antibiotic resistance to a completely different species overnight — no reproduction required. That is why one resistant patient is an infection-control event, not just a single case, and why isolation precautions exist to stop the sharing.
Interactive Tap the four parts of a bacterial cell — membrane, cytoplasm, nucleoid, ribosomes — to label and explore each one. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The plasma membrane is a selectively permeable phospholipid bilayer that also generates the cell's energy.
  • The cytoplasm holds 70S ribosomes — the target of many antibiotics.
  • The circular chromosome coils into a membrane-free nucleoid.
  • Plasmids are small DNA loops that carry resistance and toxin genes and can move between bacteria.

Section 3The cell wall

Peptidoglycan: the mesh that holds shape

Outside the membrane, most bacteria wear a rigid cell wall made of peptidoglycan (also called murein) — a material found nowhere in the human body. Picture a chain-link fence wrapped completely around the cell: long sugar chains running in parallel, cross-linked to each other by short chains of amino acids. That cross-linking turns a floppy bag into a rigid mesh strong enough to resist the cell's internal osmotic pressure, which would otherwise make it swell and burst. The wall is what gives a bacterium its shape — rod, sphere, or spiral — and its very existence, absent from human cells, is why it is such a clean drug target.

This is exactly where the beta-lactam antibiotics work. Penicillins, cephalosporins, and carbapenems block the enzymes that stitch the peptidoglycan cross-links. The bacterium keeps trying to grow, the wall can no longer hold, and the cell ruptures. Because human cells have no wall to protect, these drugs are among the safest antibiotics we have.

Gram-positive vs Gram-negative walls

Not all walls are built alike, and the difference is the single most useful early clue in a microbiology lab. The Gram stain sorts bacteria into two great camps by how their walls hold a purple dye:

Gram-positive bacteria have a thick peptidoglycan layer on the outside. It traps the crystal-violet dye and the cell stays purple. Examples: Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, Enterococcus, Clostridioides.

Gram-negative bacteria have only a thin peptidoglycan layer, but they add a second, outer membrane on top of it. That outer membrane lets the purple dye wash out during decolorizing, so the cell picks up the pink counterstain and appears red/pink. Examples: Escherichia coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas, Neisseria.

Why the Gram stain still runs the show

The Gram result is not just a color — it predicts behavior. That extra outer membrane makes Gram-negative bacteria harder to kill, because it blocks many drugs from ever reaching the wall. And the outer membrane carries a molecule that matters enormously to nurses: lipopolysaccharide (LPS), better known as endotoxin. When Gram-negative bacteria die and break apart, LPS is released into the blood and triggers a violent immune response — fever, plummeting blood pressure, and, at the extreme, septic shock. This is why a Gram-negative bloodstream infection can spiral so fast.

FeatureGram-positiveGram-negative
PeptidoglycanThick, on the surfaceThin, between two membranes
Outer membraneNoYes — carries LPS
Gram stain colorPurple (retains crystal violet)Pink/red (takes counterstain)
Endotoxin (LPS)NoYes — drives septic shock
ExamplesStaph, Strep, Enterococcus, C. diffE. coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas
Gram-negative and crashing? Think endotoxin. Aggressively treating a Gram-negative infection can kill many bacteria at once and dump a load of LPS into the bloodstream — the patient may spike a fever and drop their pressure. Knowing the organism is Gram-negative tells you to watch closely for the signs of sepsis, not just wait for the culture.
Interactive Flip between the Gram-positive and Gram-negative envelope and tap each layer of the cell wall Open full ↑
Key points
  • The wall is made of peptidoglycan, a human-free target — where beta-lactam antibiotics act.
  • Gram-positive = thick peptidoglycan, stains purple; Gram-negative = thin wall + outer membrane, stains pink.
  • The Gram-negative outer membrane blocks many drugs and carries LPS (endotoxin).
  • Released endotoxin can drive fever, hypotension, and septic shock.

Section 4External structures

The capsule: a slippery shield

Some bacteria wrap themselves in a thick, sticky outer layer called a capsule (a looser version is called a slime layer). It is not needed to live, but where it matters, it matters a great deal. A capsule is a major virulence factor: it is slippery enough that the patient's immune cells struggle to grab and swallow the bacterium, a trick called resisting phagocytosis. This is why the most dangerous strains of Streptococcus pneumoniae, Haemophilus influenzae, and Neisseria meningitidis are the encapsulated ones. Reassuringly, the capsule is also what several key vaccines are aimed at — teach the immune system to recognize the capsule, and it can clear the organism before it causes meningitis or pneumonia.

Flagella: swimming and chemotaxis

Flagella are long, whip-like tails that rotate like a propeller to push a bacterium through liquid. They are not random wanderers — bacteria steer toward nutrients and away from toxins in a directed movement called chemotaxis. Motility can be a virulence trait: it helps organisms like E. coli and Pseudomonas climb a urinary catheter or reach and colonize new tissue. The number and position of flagella (one end, both ends, or all over) is also a useful clue to a bacterium's identity.

Pili and fimbriae: sticking and swapping genes

Two shorter appendages round out the outside of the cell, and the naming trips students up, so hold them apart:

Fimbriae are numerous, short, hair-like fibers that a bacterium uses to attach to surfaces and to host cells. Attachment is the essential first step of nearly every infection — a microbe that cannot stick gets flushed away. Fimbriae are exactly how E. coli grips the lining of the bladder to start a urinary tract infection, and how bacteria anchor into the slimy communities called biofilms on catheters and implants.

Pili (specifically the sex pilus, or conjugation pilus) are usually longer and far fewer — often just one or two. Their job is different: a pilus builds a bridge to another bacterium and pulls it close so the two can swap DNA in a process called conjugation. This is one of the main ways antibiotic-resistance plasmids move from cell to cell.

StructureMain jobClinical relevance
CapsuleSticky outer coatResists phagocytosis; vaccine target
FlagellaSwimming (chemotaxis)Motility helps reach and colonize tissue
FimbriaeAttachment (many, short)Adhesion to host cells; biofilms; UTIs
Pili (sex pilus)DNA transfer (few, long)Conjugation spreads resistance genes
No stick, no infection. Fimbriae let bacteria grip a urinary catheter and climb into the bladder — which is why the simplest, most effective way to prevent a catheter-associated UTI is to remove the catheter as soon as it is no longer needed. Every extra day of a line is another day of surface for bacteria to hold onto.
Interactive Toggle a bacterium's capsule, flagella, and pili to see the survival power each one adds Open full ↑
Key points
  • The capsule resists phagocytosis (a virulence factor) and is a target of several vaccines.
  • Flagella provide directed movement (chemotaxis) that can help bacteria colonize tissue.
  • Fimbriae (many, short) mediate attachment — the first step of infection and of biofilms.
  • Pili (few, long) transfer DNA by conjugation, spreading resistance genes.

Section 5Endospores

What an endospore is

A handful of Gram-positive bacteria — most importantly the genera Bacillus and Clostridioides/Clostridium — can do something no other microbes can: when conditions turn hostile, they seal a copy of their DNA inside an armored survival capsule called an endospore. The spore is not a way to reproduce — one cell makes one spore — it is a way to wait out disaster. Inside its tough coat, the spore is metabolically dormant: no growth, no chemistry, no vulnerability. It can persist for years, even decades, in soil, on surfaces, or on hospital equipment.

How a spore forms and wakes

The normal, actively growing bacterium is called the vegetative cell. When it senses starvation or other stress, it begins sporulation: it copies its chromosome, walls off a small portion of cytoplasm around that DNA, and wraps it in layer after protective layer — a dense core stripped of most of its water, a thick cortex, and a tough protein coat. The rest of the original cell then disintegrates, releasing the finished endospore.

The spore stays dormant until conditions improve. When nutrients return, it germinates — the coat cracks, water rushes back in, metabolism restarts, and it grows back into an ordinary, dividing vegetative cell. Crucially, the vegetative cell is the form that causes active disease and that ordinary antibiotics and disinfectants can kill; the spore is the form that survives to start the cycle again.

Why spores defeat routine disinfection

The endospore's low water content and layered armor make it extraordinarily resistant — to heat, drying, radiation, and most chemical disinfectants, including the alcohol-based hand rubs that work so well against almost everything else. This is not an academic point; it changes bedside practice. Two organisms make it real:

Clostridioides difficile spreads by spores that survive on bedrails, commodes, and hands. Alcohol gel does not kill them — so for a patient with C. diff, the rule is soap-and-water handwashing (which physically washes spores away) plus a sporicidal cleaner such as diluted bleach for surfaces. Clostridium tetani and Clostridium botulinum spores lurk in soil and improperly canned food. And because spores can shrug off boiling, true sterilization of instruments requires an autoclave — pressurized steam at 121°C — not just hot water.

OrganismDiseaseWhy the spore matters
Clostridioides difficileAntibiotic-associated colitisSpores survive alcohol gel → soap & water + bleach
Clostridium tetaniTetanusSoil spores enter wounds
Clostridium botulinumBotulismSpores survive in improperly canned food
Bacillus anthracisAnthraxDurable spores; bioterror concern
For C. diff, skip the gel. Alcohol hand rub is your default for almost every patient — but it does not kill C. diff spores. In a C. diff room, wash with soap and water (the friction rinses spores off) and clean surfaces with a sporicidal agent. Matching the method to the organism is the whole game.
Interactive Step through sporulation and tap each armor layer to see why spores resist heat and chemicals Open full ↑
Key points
  • An endospore is a dormant, armored survival form — not a method of reproduction.
  • Made mainly by Gram-positive Bacillus and Clostridioides/Clostridium; the growing form is the vegetative cell.
  • Sporulation packages DNA in protective layers under stress; germination revives it when conditions improve.
  • Spores resist heat, drying, and disinfectants — hence soap-and-water for C. diff and autoclaving for sterilization.

The chapter in one breath

  • Cells are prokaryotic (no nucleus — bacteria) or eukaryotic (nucleus + organelles — fungi, protozoa, human).
  • Prokaryote-only structures — peptidoglycan wall, 70S ribosome — give antibiotics their selective toxicity.
  • The cell envelope is the plasma membrane (transport + energy), cytoplasm with 70S ribosomes, and the DNA-holding nucleoid; plasmids carry and spread resistance.
  • The wall is peptidoglycan, the target of beta-lactams; Gram-positive = thick wall/purple, Gram-negative = thin wall + outer membrane/pink.
  • The Gram-negative outer membrane carries LPS (endotoxin), a driver of septic shock.
  • External structures — capsule (resists phagocytosis), flagella (motility), fimbriae (attachment), pili (gene transfer) — are key virulence and resistance-spreading tools.
  • Endospores let Bacillus and Clostridioides survive heat and disinfectants — the reason C. diff needs soap-and-water and instruments need autoclaving.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Capsule
A thick, sticky outer layer around some bacteria that resists phagocytosis; a virulence factor and vaccine target.
Cell envelope
The layers enclosing a bacterium — the plasma membrane, the cell wall, and any outer membrane.
Cell wall
The rigid peptidoglycan layer outside the membrane that gives a bacterium its shape and resists osmotic bursting.
Chemotaxis
Directed movement by flagella toward nutrients or away from harmful chemicals.
Conjugation
Transfer of DNA (often a plasmid) between bacteria through a sex pilus.
Endospore
A dormant, highly resistant survival form made by some Gram-positive bacteria; not a means of reproduction.
Endotoxin (LPS)
Lipopolysaccharide in the Gram-negative outer membrane that triggers fever, hypotension, and septic shock when released.
Eukaryote
A cell whose DNA is enclosed in a membrane-bound nucleus and that contains organelles; fungi, protozoa, and human cells.
Fimbriae
Numerous short hair-like appendages used to attach to surfaces and host cells; enable biofilms and adhesion.
Flagella
Long whip-like appendages that rotate to propel a bacterium through liquid.
Gram-negative
Bacteria with a thin peptidoglycan wall plus an outer membrane; stain pink and carry endotoxin.
Gram-positive
Bacteria with a thick peptidoglycan wall and no outer membrane; retain crystal violet and stain purple.
Nucleoid
The membrane-free region of a prokaryote where the circular chromosome is coiled.
Peptidoglycan
The mesh of cross-linked sugars and amino acids that makes up the bacterial cell wall; the target of beta-lactam antibiotics.
Pili (sex pilus)
Long, few appendages that bridge two bacteria to transfer DNA by conjugation.
Plasma membrane
The selectively permeable phospholipid bilayer that controls transport and generates energy in prokaryotes.
Plasmid
A small circular loop of DNA separate from the chromosome that often carries resistance or toxin genes.
Prokaryote
A cell with no membrane-bound nucleus or organelles; bacteria and archaea.
Ribosome (70S / 80S)
The protein-building machine; prokaryotes use the smaller 70S, eukaryotes the larger 80S — a key antibiotic target.
Selective toxicity
Harming a microbe while sparing the host by targeting a structure the microbe has and the host lacks.
Sporulation
The process by which a stressed vegetative cell forms an endospore; reversed by germination.
Vegetative cell
The active, growing, dividing form of a bacterium — the form that causes disease and is killed by drugs and disinfectants.

Check yourself

1. A patient's chart says the infection is caused by a eukaryotic microbe. What two large groups does that point to, and why does it change your drug choice?
Fungi and protozoa are the eukaryotic microbes. Because they are eukaryotes (like human cells), antibacterial antibiotics won't touch them — a fungal infection needs an antifungal, not an antibiotic.
2. Explain selective toxicity and name two prokaryote-only structures that make it possible.
Selective toxicity is harming the microbe while sparing the patient by attacking a structure the microbe has but human cells don't. Two examples: the peptidoglycan cell wall and the 70S ribosome — both absent from human cells.
3. Where do prokaryotes carry out energy production, given that they have no mitochondria?
On the plasma membrane. The electron transport chain runs in the membrane itself, doing the job that mitochondria perform in eukaryotic cells.
4. What is a plasmid, and why is it a major infection-control concern?
A small loop of DNA separate from the chromosome, often carrying resistance or toxin genes. Plasmids can transfer between bacteria (even different species), spreading antibiotic resistance without any reproduction — so one resistant patient can seed a whole ward.
5. Peptidoglycan is described as an ideal antibiotic target. Which drug class exploits it, and why is that class so safe?
The beta-lactams (penicillins, cephalosporins, carbapenems) block cell-wall building. Human cells have no cell wall, so the drug has no equivalent target in the patient — making beta-lactams among the safest antibiotics.
6. On a Gram stain, one organism is purple and another is pink. Describe the wall structure behind each color.
Purple = Gram-positive, with a thick surface peptidoglycan layer that traps crystal violet. Pink = Gram-negative, with a thin peptidoglycan layer plus an outer membrane that lets the dye wash out, so it takes the pink counterstain.
7. A patient with a Gram-negative bloodstream infection suddenly spikes a fever and becomes hypotensive. What molecule likely explains this, and where does it come from?
Endotoxin (LPS) from the Gram-negative outer membrane. When the bacteria die and break apart, LPS is released into the blood and drives fever, dropping blood pressure, and potentially septic shock.
8. Distinguish fimbriae from pili by number, length, and function.
Fimbriae are many and short and are used for attachment to surfaces and host cells; pili (the sex pilus) are few and long and are used to transfer DNA by conjugation. Fimbriae start infections and biofilms; pili spread resistance genes.
9. Why is the capsule considered a virulence factor, and how do we turn it against the microbe?
The capsule's slippery coat helps the bacterium resist phagocytosis, so encapsulated strains cause worse disease (e.g., pneumococcus, meningococcus). Several vaccines target the capsule so the immune system can recognize and clear the organism.
10. Explain the difference between a vegetative cell and an endospore. Which one causes active disease?
The vegetative cell is the active, growing, dividing form — and the one that causes active disease and is killed by drugs and disinfectants. The endospore is a dormant, armored survival form that waits out harsh conditions. A spore germinates back into a vegetative cell when conditions improve.
11. Why must you use soap and water — not alcohol hand rub — when caring for a patient with C. difficile?
C. diff spreads by endospores, and alcohol does not kill spores. Soap-and-water handwashing physically washes the spores off the hands, and surfaces need a sporicidal agent such as diluted bleach.
12. Why does true sterilization of instruments require an autoclave rather than boiling water?
Endospores can survive boiling. An autoclave uses pressurized steam at 121°C, hot enough to destroy even resistant spores — the standard for sterilizing surgical instruments.
Microbiology · Chapter 4

Bacterial Diversity & Classification

There are more kinds of bacteria than there are kinds of everything else alive, and only a small fraction ever make a person sick — but that fraction is the fraction you will meet at the bedside. The trick to keeping the clinically important organisms straight is not memorizing a list; it is learning the handful of features microbiologists use to sort bacteria into groups. Shape, staining, oxygen needs, and a few special quirks together place almost any pathogen into a recognizable box, and the box tells you how it spreads, what illness it causes, and which drugs will work. This chapter walks through those sorting features one at a time and hangs the most important pathogens on them, so that a culture result or a Gram-stain report starts to read like a sentence you already understand.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Name the three basic bacterial shapes and the common arrangements, and read the clues hidden in a genus name.
  • Explain what the Gram stain reveals and why gram-positive and gram-negative cell walls respond differently.
  • Identify the major gram-positive pathogens — Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, and Clostridium/Clostridioides — and the diseases they cause.
  • Identify the major gram-negative pathogens — E. coli, Salmonella, and Pseudomonas — and explain why endotoxin makes them dangerous.
  • Describe the special bacteria — mycobacteria, spirochetes, and mycoplasmas — that break the usual staining and cell-wall rules.
  • Classify bacteria by their oxygen requirements and explain why this changes how specimens are collected and where infections form.
  • List the other axes — temperature, pH, salt, intracellular lifestyle — used to describe and identify bacteria.
  • Connect each classification feature to a concrete nursing action at the bedside.

Section 1Shapes & arrangements

Three basic shapes

Under the microscope, the overwhelming majority of bacteria take one of three shapes, and the shape is the very first thing a lab reports because it narrows the field so quickly. A coccus (plural cocci) is round or spherical, like a tiny ball. A bacillus (plural bacilli) is a rod, shaped like a stick or a grain of rice. A spirillum (plural spirilla) is spiral or curved, coiled like a corkscrew or bent like a comma. Between these three there are in-between forms worth knowing: a coccobacillus is a short, plump rod that can be mistaken for a coccus, and a vibrio is a short comma-shaped curve — the shape that gives Vibrio cholerae its name.

Shape is not just description. It is built into the name of nearly every organism you will chart, so the moment you learn to hear it, the vocabulary of microbiology starts teaching itself. Staphylococcus and Streptococcus are spheres; Bacillus and Lactobacillus are rods; Spirochetes are coils. Reading the shape out of a genus name is a habit worth building on day one.

Arrangements: how the cells hang together

When a bacterium divides, the two daughter cells may drift apart or may stay stuck together, and the pattern they form is a second identifying clue — especially for cocci. The prefixes are logical once you learn them. Diplo- means the cells sit in pairs (diplococci, as in Neisseria). Strepto- means they form chains, like a string of beads (streptococci). Staphylo- means they clump in irregular grape-like clusters (staphylococci). Cocci can also form flat squares of four (tetrads) or cubical packets of eight (sarcinae). Rods more often line up end-to-end in short chains or side-by-side like a fence (a palisade), the arrangement typical of Corynebacterium.

What you seeNameClassic organism
Round, in grape-like clustersStaphylococciStaphylococcus aureus
Round, in chainsStreptococciStreptococcus pyogenes
Round, in pairsDiplococciNeisseria, S. pneumoniae
Rod-shapedBacillusE. coli, Clostridium
Comma / curved rodVibrioVibrio cholerae
Spiral / corkscrewSpirocheteTreponema pallidum
A Gram stain result is a head start, not the finish line. When the lab calls to say a blood culture shows "gram-positive cocci in clusters," the team is already thinking Staphylococcus and choosing empiric coverage — hours before the organism is formally named. Shape plus arrangement plus stain color is the fastest actionable information microbiology produces, which is why the preliminary Gram report matters so much to timing antibiotics.

Interactive Build a bacterium: pick a shape and an arrangement to watch cocci, bacilli, and spirilla earn their names. Open full ↑
Key points
  • The three basic shapes are coccus (sphere), bacillus (rod), and spirillum (spiral); vibrios and coccobacilli are in-between forms.
  • Arrangement prefixes — diplo- (pairs), strepto- (chains), staphylo- (clusters) — add a second layer of identification, especially for cocci.
  • Shape and arrangement are usually built into the genus name, so the name itself carries clues.
  • The preliminary Gram-stain report (shape + arrangement + color) guides empiric therapy before the final identification arrives.

Section 2Gram-positive pathogens

What "gram-positive" means
