Having the instructions is not the same as carrying them out. To use a gene, the cell runs the central dogma: DNA → RNA → protein. First comes transcription, where an enzyme called RNA polymerase reads a gene and writes a working copy of it as a strand of messenger RNA (mRNA). Then comes translation, where a ribosome reads the mRNA three bases at a time — each three-base codon specifying one amino acid — and links amino acids into a protein. The finished protein folds up and goes to work as an enzyme, a structural part, or a pump. Change the DNA and you change the protein; change the protein and you change what the microbe can do.

StepInput → outputKey enzyme/machineAntibiotic that blocks it
ReplicationDNA → two identical DNAsDNA polymerase, DNA gyraseFluoroquinolones (block gyrase)
TranscriptionDNA → mRNARNA polymeraseRifampin (blocks RNA polymerase)
TranslationmRNA → proteinRibosomeAminoglycosides, tetracyclines, macrolides
Half the antibiotic classes you will give are attacks on the central dogma. Rifampin jams RNA polymerase, fluoroquinolones jam the enzyme that unwinds DNA for replication, and a whole family of drugs — aminoglycosides, tetracyclines, macrolides — jam the bacterial ribosome so no protein gets made. They can do this safely because the bacterial versions of these machines differ from your patient's (selective toxicity, from Chapter 1). Knowing which step a drug hits is the fastest way to remember how it works.
Interactive Step through replication, transcription, and translation to watch a bacterial gene become a protein — and see where antibiotics jam each step. Open full ↑
Key points
  • DNA stores instructions as base pairs (A–T, G–C); a gene codes for one product, and the whole set is the genome.
  • Replication copies DNA so each daughter cell gets a full genome; bacteria do this fast.
  • The central dogma is DNA → (transcription) → mRNA → (translation) → protein.
  • Many antibiotics work by blocking replication, transcription, or translation in bacteria specifically.

Section 2Mutation

What a mutation is

A mutation is any change in the DNA sequence. Most mutations are copying errors: DNA polymerase is remarkably accurate, but it is not perfect, and when a bacterium divides billions of times, rare mistakes are guaranteed. A mutation may do nothing, may harm the microbe, or — occasionally — may hand it a brand-new ability. Whether a mutation matters depends on how it changes the protein a gene builds, which is why the type of mutation is worth knowing.

Types of mutation

The simplest change is a point mutation, where a single base is swapped for another. Its consequences vary. A silent mutation changes a codon but still codes for the same amino acid, so the protein is unchanged. A missense mutation changes one amino acid, which may subtly or seriously alter the protein — this is the classic route to a drug target that no longer binds its drug. A nonsense mutation turns an amino-acid codon into a "stop" signal, cutting the protein short and usually breaking it.

More disruptive is the frameshift mutation, where a base is inserted or deleted. Because the ribosome reads mRNA in fixed groups of three, adding or removing one base shifts the entire reading frame downstream, garbling every codon after the change. Frameshifts almost always destroy the protein completely.

Mutagens

Mutations that happen on their own during replication are spontaneous. Their rate rises sharply in the presence of a mutagen — an agent that damages DNA or causes copying errors. Chemical mutagens include certain reactive molecules and base analogs that slip into DNA in place of a normal base. Radiation is a powerful physical mutagen: ultraviolet (UV) light fuses adjacent thymine bases into thymine dimers that distort the helix, while X-rays and gamma rays break strands outright. This is also why UV light and ionizing radiation are used to sterilize — the same DNA damage that causes mutations, at high enough doses, simply kills the cell.

MutationWhat changesEffect on protein
Silent (point)One base; codon still means the same amino acidNone
Missense (point)One base; codon now means a different amino acidOne amino acid changed — may alter function (e.g., a drug target)
Nonsense (point)One base; codon becomes a "stop"Protein cut short — usually nonfunctional
FrameshiftBase inserted or deletedReading frame shifts — protein usually destroyed
Why we treat TB with three or four drugs at once. In a large population of Mycobacterium tuberculosis, a spontaneous mutation making a bug resistant to one drug is essentially inevitable. But the odds that the same cell randomly carries resistance mutations to three different drugs at once are vanishingly small. Combination therapy works precisely because it out-runs spontaneous mutation — and it collapses the moment a patient takes only one of the drugs, which is why adherence teaching is a clinical, not a clerical, task.
Interactive Tap the four point-mutation types and watch one DNA base rewrite the codon and protein Open full ↑
Key points
  • A mutation is any change in the DNA sequence; most arise as rare replication errors.
  • Point mutations swap one base: silent (no change), missense (one amino acid changed), nonsense (premature stop).
  • Frameshift mutations (insertion/deletion) shift the reading frame and usually wreck the protein.
  • Mutagens — chemicals, UV, X-rays — raise the mutation rate; spontaneous mutation is the raw material of resistance.

Section 3Horizontal gene transfer

Sideways, not just downward

Mutation is slow and random. If it were the only way bacteria gained new genes, resistance would spread far more gradually than it does. But bacteria have a second, much faster trick: they trade genes sideways. Vertical gene transfer is the ordinary inheritance of genes from a parent cell to its daughters. Horizontal gene transfer (HGT) is the movement of genetic material between separate cells — even between different species — that are not parent and offspring. HGT is why a resistance gene that arose in one organism can turn up months later in an entirely unrelated one. There are three routes.

Transformation, transduction, conjugation

Transformation is the uptake of naked DNA from the environment. When a bacterium dies and breaks apart, it spills its DNA; a nearby "competent" cell can pull those fragments across its membrane and stitch them into its own genome. If the fragment carried a resistance gene, the recipient is now resistant — without ever meeting the original owner alive.

Transduction uses a bacterial virus (a bacteriophage) as an accidental delivery truck. When a phage infects a bacterium and assembles new virus particles, it sometimes packages a piece of the host's DNA by mistake. When that phage goes on to infect another bacterium, it injects the stray host gene along with itself, and the new host may keep it.

Conjugation is the most efficient of the three and the one that matters most for resistance. Two bacteria connect through a bridge called a sex pilus, and a copy of a plasmid — often an entire packet of resistance genes — is passed from donor to recipient. It is the closest thing bacteria have to direct sharing, and it can move genes between species that would otherwise never mix.

MechanismHow DNA movesVehicleClinical relevance
TransformationCell takes up free DNA from a dead microbeNaked environmental DNASpreads genes released by lysed bacteria
TransductionA virus carries bacterial DNA to a new cellBacteriophageMoves genes, including some toxins & resistance
ConjugationDirect cell-to-cell transfer through a pilusSex pilus + plasmidMajor driver of multidrug-resistance spread
Resistance does not need to evolve in the bug that infects your patient. Through conjugation, a resistance plasmid that developed in a harmless gut bacterium can be handed to a dangerous pathogen in the same patient — sometimes within a single hospital stay. That is why an outbreak of one resistant organism on a unit is a warning about the whole unit's flora, not just about one strain.
Interactive Tap transformation, transduction, and conjugation — then add DNase to see which route it blocks Open full ↑
Key points
  • Vertical transfer is parent-to-daughter inheritance; horizontal gene transfer (HGT) moves genes between separate cells, even across species.
  • Transformation = uptake of free DNA; transduction = delivery by a bacteriophage; conjugation = direct transfer via a pilus.
  • Conjugation is the biggest driver of multidrug-resistance spread because it moves whole plasmids of genes at once.
  • HGT lets resistance jump between unrelated organisms far faster than mutation alone.

Section 4Plasmids & resistance genes

Little rings of optional DNA

Besides its main chromosome, a bacterium often carries one or more plasmids — small, circular loops of DNA that sit apart from the chromosome (they are extrachromosomal). Plasmids are copied by the cell and passed to daughter cells, but they are not strictly required for survival. Instead they carry "bonus" genes that come in handy under stress: genes for using an unusual nutrient, for producing a toxin, or — the ones that keep infection-control nurses up at night — genes for surviving antibiotics.

R plasmids: resistance to go

A plasmid that carries one or more antibiotic-resistance genes is called a resistance plasmid, or R plasmid. R plasmids are dangerous for two reasons. First, a single plasmid can carry resistance genes to several different antibiotics at once, so acquiring it in one step makes a bacterium multidrug-resistant (MDR). Second, R plasmids often carry their own conjugation machinery, so they are built to copy themselves into neighboring cells. Put those two facts together and you have a mobile, self-spreading package of multidrug resistance — a major reason resistant organisms like VRE and the multidrug-resistant gram-negatives are behind so many hospital outbreaks. (Not every resistant organism works this way: in MRSA, for instance, the key methicillin-resistance gene sits on the chromosome, not a plasmid.)

Jumping genes that build the package

How does a plasmid come to hold so many resistance genes? Often through transposons — nicknamed "jumping genes" — short DNA segments that can cut or copy themselves from one location and paste into another, including from a chromosome onto a plasmid. Related elements called integrons can capture resistance gene "cassettes" and line them up together. Over time these shuffling elements assemble multidrug-resistance plasmids gene by gene, ready to be shared by conjugation.

FeatureChromosomePlasmid
SizeLarge; the main genomeSmall loop of extra DNA
Essential?Yes — carries the genes for survivalNo — carries optional/"situational" genes
Copied & inherited?Yes, with each divisionYes, and can also be shared by conjugation
Typical cargoCore metabolism, structureResistance, toxins, special nutrients
One plasmid can defeat several drugs at once. When a culture returns resistant to three or four unrelated antibiotics, the usual explanation is not three or four separate mutations — it is a single R plasmid carrying all of them, likely picked up by conjugation. This is why a new MDR organism on a unit spreads by contact, and why gowns, gloves, and dedicated equipment (contact precautions) are the countermeasure, not a stronger single drug.
Interactive Tap the genes around the plasmid ring to see how one R-plasmid copies itself, spreads, and defeats several antibiotics at once Open full ↑
Key points
  • Plasmids are small, circular, extrachromosomal loops of DNA that carry optional but useful genes.
  • R plasmids carry antibiotic-resistance genes, often to several drugs at once (multidrug resistance).
  • R plasmids frequently carry conjugation machinery, so they copy themselves into neighboring cells.
  • Transposons and integrons shuffle resistance genes onto plasmids, building the multidrug package.

Section 5How resistance spreads

Selective pressure: the drug does the choosing

Antibiotics do not create resistance — they select for it. Picture a population of bacteria in which, by chance, a few cells already carry a resistance gene (from mutation or from a plasmid). Give an antibiotic and the susceptible cells die, but the resistant few survive and multiply, quickly repopulating the site with an all-resistant community. This is selective pressure: the drug clears the field for exactly the organisms it cannot kill. Every dose of antibiotic anywhere — appropriate or not — nudges the microbial world a little further toward resistance, which is why unnecessary antibiotic use is not a harmless "just in case."

Where the spreading happens

Resistant organisms spread the same way any microbe spreads — mostly by contact, and in healthcare that means hands, equipment, and surfaces moving flora from patient to patient. Several forces accelerate it: overprescribing and prescribing antibiotics for viral illnesses; patients stopping a course early, which kills the weak and leaves the tough survivors behind; heavy antibiotic use in agriculture, which seeds resistant organisms into the food supply and environment; and international travel, which carries resistant strains around the globe in days. The genetics from the earlier sections is what makes each of these routes so potent: once a resistance gene exists on a mobile plasmid, contact spread and conjugation do the rest.

The nurse at the center of it

Slowing resistance is not mainly a laboratory problem — it is a bedside one, and the nurse sits at the center. Hand hygiene and contact precautions stop resistant organisms from moving between patients. Antimicrobial stewardship — questioning whether an antibiotic is truly needed, pushing for cultures before starting drugs, narrowing broad-spectrum therapy once the organism is known, and stopping antibiotics that are no longer indicated — reduces the selective pressure in the first place. And patient teaching that explains why to finish the full course, why leftover antibiotics should never be shared, and why a cold will not respond to antibiotics turns the patient into a partner rather than a driver of the problem.

Driver of spreadWhat happensNursing countermeasure
Contact transmissionResistant flora ride hands, equipment, surfaces between patientsHand hygiene; contact precautions; dedicated equipment
OverprescribingAntibiotics given when not needed (e.g., for viruses) select for resistanceStewardship; culture first; question the indication
Incomplete coursesStopping early leaves the toughest survivors to repopulateTeach adherence; explain the "why"
Broad-spectrum overuseWide kill flattens flora and selects widely for resistanceDe-escalate to a narrow agent once the organism is known
The most effective antibiotic-resistance drug is still soap. No new antibiotic slows resistance the way clean hands between patients do, because hand hygiene removes the resistant organisms before they are ever exposed to a drug that could select for them. Stewardship reduces the pressure; hand hygiene blocks the spread. Together they are the two levers a nurse actually controls — and they matter more than any single prescription.
Interactive Step through conjugation to watch a donor pump a copy of its resistance plasmid into a recipient — turning a susceptible cell into a new resistant donor Open full ↑
Key points
  • Selective pressure: antibiotics do not create resistance, they select for the few cells that already have it, then those cells take over.
  • Resistance spreads mainly by contact, amplified by overprescribing, incomplete courses, agricultural use, and travel.
  • The genetics of Sections 1–4 (mutation, HGT, R plasmids) is what makes each route so fast.
  • Hand hygiene, contact precautions, stewardship, and patient teaching are the nurse's levers against resistance.

The chapter in one breath

  • DNA stores a microbe's instructions as base pairs; a gene is expressed through the central dogma — DNA replicated, transcribed to mRNA, translated to protein.
  • Several antibiotic classes work by blocking replication, transcription, or translation in bacteria specifically.
  • A mutation changes the DNA sequence; point mutations (silent, missense, nonsense) swap one base, frameshifts shift the reading frame, and mutagens raise the rate.
  • Spontaneous mutation seeds resistance, which is why serious infections like TB are treated with multiple drugs at once.
  • Horizontal gene transfer — transformation, transduction, conjugation — moves genes sideways between cells, even across species.
  • R plasmids are mobile loops of DNA carrying resistance to several drugs at once, spread efficiently by conjugation.
  • Selective pressure from antibiotic use plus contact spread drives resistance through healthcare and the community; hand hygiene, stewardship, and teaching are the nurse's countermeasures.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Central dogma
The flow of genetic information in a cell: DNA → RNA → protein (replication, transcription, translation).
Codon
A group of three mRNA bases that specifies one amino acid during translation.
Conjugation
Horizontal gene transfer by direct cell-to-cell contact through a sex pilus, often moving a plasmid.
DNA (deoxyribonucleic acid)
The double-stranded molecule that stores genetic instructions as paired bases (A–T, G–C).
Frameshift mutation
A mutation from inserting or deleting a base, which shifts the reading frame and usually destroys the protein.
Gene
A stretch of DNA that codes for one product, usually a protein.
Genome
The complete set of an organism's genetic material.
Horizontal gene transfer (HGT)
Movement of genetic material between separate cells (not parent to offspring), even across species.
Missense mutation
A point mutation that changes one amino acid in a protein; may alter its function.
Mutagen
A physical or chemical agent (e.g., UV light, X-rays, certain chemicals) that raises the mutation rate.
Mutation
Any change in the DNA sequence.
Nonsense mutation
A point mutation that turns an amino-acid codon into a stop codon, cutting the protein short.
Plasmid
A small, circular, extrachromosomal loop of DNA that carries optional genes and can be shared between cells.
Point mutation
A mutation that swaps a single base; may be silent, missense, or nonsense.
R plasmid (resistance plasmid)
A plasmid carrying one or more antibiotic-resistance genes, often to several drugs at once.
Replication
The copying of DNA before cell division, producing two identical double helices.
Selective pressure
The way antibiotic use favors the survival and multiplication of already-resistant cells.
Transcription
Copying a gene from DNA into messenger RNA by RNA polymerase.
Transduction
Horizontal gene transfer in which a bacteriophage carries bacterial DNA to a new cell.
Transformation
Horizontal gene transfer in which a cell takes up free DNA released by a dead microbe.
Translation
Building a protein from an mRNA sequence at the ribosome, one codon at a time.
Transposon
A "jumping gene" — a DNA segment that can move between locations, including onto plasmids.

Check yourself

1. State the base-pairing rule for DNA and explain why it matters for replication.
A pairs with T, and G pairs with C. Because pairing is fixed, each strand is a perfect template for rebuilding its partner, so an unzipped double helix can be copied faithfully into two identical molecules.
2. Put the central dogma in order and name the product of each step.
DNA → (transcription) → mRNA → (translation) → protein. Transcription copies a gene into messenger RNA; translation reads that mRNA at the ribosome to build a protein. (Replication separately copies DNA to DNA.)
3. Rifampin, fluoroquinolones, and tetracyclines each block a different step of the central dogma. Match each to its step.
Fluoroquinolones block replication (DNA gyrase); rifampin blocks transcription (RNA polymerase); tetracyclines block translation (the ribosome). All three are safe because the bacterial machines differ from the patient's — selective toxicity.
4. Define a mutation and explain why fast bacterial replication guarantees that rare mutations occur.
A mutation is any change in the DNA sequence. DNA polymerase is accurate but not perfect, so when a population divides billions of times, even a very low error rate produces many mutant cells — some of which may be resistant.
5. Distinguish silent, missense, and nonsense point mutations by their effect on the protein.
Silent = no change to the amino acid (protein unchanged); missense = one amino acid changed (may alter function); nonsense = a premature stop codon (protein cut short, usually broken). Missense changes are a common route to a drug target that no longer binds its drug.
6. Why does a frameshift mutation usually cause more damage than a point mutation?
Because the ribosome reads mRNA in fixed groups of three, inserting or deleting a base shifts the reading frame and garbles every codon after it. A point mutation changes at most one codon; a frameshift scrambles the entire rest of the protein.
7. Name two mutagens and explain why UV light is used both to cause mutations in the lab and to sterilize surfaces.
Any two: UV light, X-rays/gamma rays, certain chemicals/base analogs. UV fuses adjacent thymines into thymine dimers that distort DNA; a little of this causes mutations, while a large dose does so much DNA damage that the cell dies — which is what sterilization relies on.
8. Why is serious tuberculosis treated with three or four antibiotics at once instead of one?
A spontaneous mutation resistant to any single drug is nearly inevitable in a large population, but the chance of one cell being resistant to three drugs at once is vanishingly small. Combination therapy out-runs spontaneous mutation — and fails if the patient takes only one of the drugs.
9. Compare vertical and horizontal gene transfer, and name the three mechanisms of HGT.
Vertical = parent-to-daughter inheritance; horizontal (HGT) = gene movement between separate cells, even across species. The three HGT mechanisms are transformation (uptake of free DNA), transduction (delivery by a bacteriophage), and conjugation (direct transfer through a pilus).
10. Which HGT mechanism is the biggest driver of multidrug-resistance spread, and why?
Conjugation. It transfers a whole plasmid — often an entire packet of resistance genes — directly from one cell to another through a pilus, and can do so between different species, so many resistances move in a single step.
11. What is an R plasmid, and why is acquiring one especially dangerous?
An R (resistance) plasmid is a small circular loop of extrachromosomal DNA carrying antibiotic-resistance genes. A single R plasmid can carry resistance to several drugs at once (making a cell multidrug-resistant in one step) and often carries conjugation machinery, so it copies itself into neighboring cells.
12. Explain "selective pressure" and name two things a nurse controls that slow the spread of resistance.
Antibiotics do not create resistance; they kill susceptible cells and let the already-resistant few survive and take over — that is selective pressure. A nurse slows it through hand hygiene and contact precautions (blocking spread) and through stewardship and patient teaching, including adherence to the full course (reducing pressure).
Microbiology · Chapter 8

Controlling Microbes — Sterilization, Disinfection & Asepsis

Every time you clean your hands, wipe down a bedrail, open a sterile dressing kit, or hang an IV, you are practicing microbial control — the deliberate work of killing microbes or holding their numbers down so they cannot harm your patient. The trouble is that the words for this work are used loosely in everyday speech and precisely in the clinical world, and the gap between "clean," "disinfected," and "sterile" is exactly where infections slip through. This chapter sorts out that vocabulary, walks through the physical and chemical tools we use to kill microbes, explains why killing follows a predictable curve rather than an on/off switch, and finishes at the bedside with the two kinds of asepsis every nurse practices by hand, many times a shift. Master this, and infection prevention stops being a list of rules and becomes something you understand.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Define and rank sterilization, disinfection, antisepsis, and sanitization, and use the -cidal vs -static suffixes correctly.
  • Compare the major physical methods of control — moist and dry heat, the autoclave, radiation, and filtration — and match each to what it can and cannot sterilize.
  • Explain how the autoclave achieves sterilization and how its success is verified.
  • Describe the major families of chemical disinfectants and antiseptics and how they kill microbes.
  • Interpret the microbial death curve, the meaning of D-value, and why sterility is defined statistically.
  • List the factors that make microbial killing faster or slower, and rank microbes by their resistance to control.
  • Distinguish medical (clean) from surgical (sterile) asepsis and apply the rules of a sterile field.

Section 1The vocabulary of control

A ladder, not a light switch

The single most important idea in this chapter is that "getting rid of germs" is not one thing — it is a ladder of increasingly complete removal, and each rung has a precise name and a precise job. At the top sits sterilization: the complete destruction or removal of all microbial life, including the toughest of all targets, bacterial endospores. A sterile object has nothing living on it, full stop. One step down is disinfection, which destroys most pathogens on a non-living surface but does not reliably kill endospores. Because it is not absolute, disinfection comes in levels — high, intermediate, and low — depending on how much it kills. The distinction matters because much of what we do at the bedside is disinfection, not sterilization, and knowing the difference tells you which tasks are "good enough" and which demand the sterile item from central supply.

On skin versus on surfaces

The next two terms are constantly confused because they do similar work in different places. Antisepsis is the removal of microbes from living tissue — skin, a mucous membrane, a surgical site. The chemical used is an antiseptic, and because it goes on a living patient it must be gentle enough not to injure tissue. Disinfection, by contrast, happens on inanimate objects, and the chemical is a disinfectant, which can be far harsher because no living cells have to survive it. This is why you scrub a patient's skin with chlorhexidine or an alcohol swab (antiseptics) but wipe the counter with a bleach or quaternary-ammonium solution (disinfectants) — and why pouring a surface disinfectant into a wound is a genuine mistake, not a shortcut. A close cousin is degerming, the mechanical scrubbing away of microbes (the alcohol wipe before an injection does this), and sanitization, which simply lowers microbial counts to a "safe" public-health level rather than eliminating them — the standard for restaurant dishwashers and drinking glasses, not for anything that enters a patient.

Reading the suffixes

Microbiology labels its weapons with two suffixes you must be able to read on sight. An agent ending in -cidal (bactericidal, virucidal, fungicidal, sporicidal) kills the target outright. An agent ending in -static (bacteriostatic, fungistatic) only stops it from multiplying — the microbes are still alive, held in check, and will resume growing if the agent is removed or the patient's immune defenses fail. That difference is not academic: a bacteriostatic drug depends on a working immune system to finish the job, which is why the distinction resurfaces when we discuss antimicrobial drugs and immunocompromised patients.

TermWhat it doesKills endospores?Used on
SterilizationDestroys or removes all microbial lifeYesInstruments, IV fluids, implants
DisinfectionDestroys most pathogens (high/intermediate/low level)Not reliablyInanimate surfaces & equipment
AntisepsisReduces microbes on living tissueNoSkin, mucous membranes, wounds
DegermingMechanically removes microbesNoSkin (e.g., pre-injection swab)
SanitizationLowers counts to a "safe" public levelNoDishes, food surfaces, floors
"Clean" is not "sterile," and knowing which you need prevents harm. An instrument that touches only intact skin can be disinfected; one that enters sterile tissue or the bloodstream must be sterilized. Reaching for a merely-clean item where a sterile one is required — or, just as wasteful, demanding sterile where clean is enough — both come from blurring these words. Pin them down now and every later infection-control decision gets easier.

Interactive Slide across the control spectrum from sanitization to sterilization and watch which microbes survive Open full ↑
Key points
  • Sterilization alone removes all life including endospores; disinfection (surfaces) and antisepsis (living tissue) do not.
  • Disinfectant = harsh chemical for objects; antiseptic = gentle chemical for skin and tissue.
  • Sanitization only reduces counts to a safe level; degerming is mechanical removal.
  • -cidal kills; -static merely halts multiplication and relies on host defenses.

Section 2Physical methods — heat, the autoclave, radiation & filtration

Heat: the oldest and most reliable tool

Heat kills by denaturing proteins and enzymes and by disrupting membranes, and it comes in two forms with very different power. Moist heat — steam and boiling water — is far more effective than dry heat because water conducts energy efficiently and drives protein coagulation quickly (think of how fast an egg white sets in boiling water versus a dry oven). Dry heat, delivered by a hot-air oven or an open flame, works too but needs much higher temperatures and longer times to accomplish the same kill. Two classic benchmarks describe heat's effect: the thermal death point (the lowest temperature that kills all microbes in a suspension in ten minutes) and the thermal death time (the time needed to kill them at a given temperature). Note one important limit: ordinary boiling at 100°C does not sterilize — it kills vegetative cells and most viruses but leaves hardy endospores alive, which is precisely the problem the autoclave was invented to solve. A gentler cousin, pasteurization, uses brief, mild heat not to sterilize but to lower the count of pathogens and spoilage organisms in milk and other liquids while preserving flavor.

The autoclave: steam under pressure

The autoclave is the single most important sterilizing device in health care, and it solves the boiling-water problem with a simple trick: raising the pressure raises the boiling point, so steam can be heated well past 100°C. A standard cycle holds saturated steam at 121°C under about 15 pounds per square inch (psi) of pressure for roughly 15–20 minutes — conditions that reliably destroy even endospores. The active ingredient is the moist heat of the steam, not the pressure itself; the pressure is only there to make the hotter steam possible. Because a load only counts as sterile if the steam actually reached every surface at temperature for long enough, autoclaving is always verified, never assumed. Chemical indicators — the striped autoclave tape and pouch dots that change color — confirm a load was exposed to heat, but the gold standard is a biological indicator: a sealed vial of Geobacillus stearothermophilus endospores run through the cycle and then incubated. If those famously heat-resistant spores are dead, everything else in the load is too.

Radiation and filtration: when heat would ruin the product

Plenty of medical items — plastic syringes, IV tubing, sutures, some fluids — would melt, warp, or degrade in an autoclave, so we sterilize them another way. Radiation splits into two types. Ionizing radiation (gamma rays, X-rays, electron beams) carries enough energy to smash DNA and generate destructive free radicals; it penetrates deeply, sterilizes in the cold, and is used industrially for prepackaged disposables and some foods. Nonionizing radiation, chiefly ultraviolet (UV) light, is weaker: it fuses adjacent thymine bases into dimers that block DNA replication, but it barely penetrates — it cannot pass through glass, plastic, or even a layer of dust — so its use is limited to disinfecting surfaces and air in biosafety cabinets and empty rooms (and it is a genuine hazard to the eyes and skin of anyone in the room). Finally, filtration physically removes microbes rather than killing them, by straining a fluid or gas through pores too small for cells to pass. A membrane filter with 0.2 µm pores sterilizes heat-sensitive liquids such as certain antibiotics, vitamins, and IV additives, and HEPA filters do the same for air, keeping operating rooms and protective-isolation rooms clean.

MethodHow it kills / removesSterilizes?Typical use
Autoclave (moist heat)Steam at 121°C, 15 psi denatures proteinsYes (spores too)Surgical instruments, glassware, media
Dry heat / flameOxidation & denaturation at high tempYes (long/hot)Glassware, inoculating loops
Boiling (100°C)Denatures proteinsNo — spores surviveHome disinfection of vegetative cells
Ionizing radiationBreaks DNA, forms free radicalsYesDisposable plastics, sutures, foods
UV (nonionizing)Thymine dimers block replicationSurface onlyAir & surfaces in cabinets/rooms
Membrane filtrationPhysically strains out cells (0.2 µm)Yes (fluids)Heat-labile fluids; HEPA for air
A cycle is not sterile until the biological indicator says so. Autoclave tape only proves an item saw heat — it turns dark even if the cycle failed to hold temperature long enough. The spore vial is what actually certifies a load, which is why sterile-processing departments run and log them routinely. If you ever wonder whether "it looked processed" is good enough, remember that the tape and the truth are two different things.

Interactive Tap heat, the autoclave, radiation, and filtration to see how each physical method controls microbes Open full ↑
Key points
  • Moist heat beats dry heat; ordinary boiling does not sterilize because endospores survive.
  • The autoclave (121°C, ~15 psi, ~15 min) sterilizes with pressurized steam and is verified by a Geobacillus spore biological indicator.
  • Ionizing radiation sterilizes heat-sensitive disposables; UV only disinfects surfaces and air and does not penetrate.
  • Filtration (0.2 µm membranes, HEPA) removes microbes from heat-labile fluids and from air.

Section 3Chemical methods — disinfectants & antiseptics

How chemicals kill

Chemical control agents work by a handful of mechanisms, and most fall into two big buckets: they denature proteins (unfolding the enzymes a microbe needs to live) or they disrupt the plasma membrane (dissolving its lipids so the cell leaks and dies). Some go further and damage nucleic acids or oxidize cell components. Whether a given chemical is called a disinfectant or an antiseptic depends on where it is used, not on the molecule itself — several agents, such as iodine and the quats, serve as both at different concentrations. Two practical properties decide whether a chemical is useful in the hospital: its use-dilution (the concentration at which it actually works, since more concentrated is not always better — 70% alcohol kills better than 100% because it needs water to denature protein) and its behavior in the presence of organic matter like blood and pus, which can neutralize many agents on contact.

The chemical families you will meet

You do not need to memorize every compound, but you should recognize the major families and where each shines. Alcohols (isopropyl and ethanol, best at 60–90%) denature proteins and dissolve membranes; they are fast, cheap skin antiseptics and surface wipes, but they evaporate quickly and do not kill spores. Chlorhexidine is a gentle, long-lasting skin antiseptic that binds to the skin and keeps working for hours — the reason it is used for surgical scrubs and central-line site care. Iodophors (iodine complexed with a carrier, e.g., povidone-iodine/Betadine) are broad-spectrum skin preps for surgery and injections. Halogens also include chlorine (household bleach, sodium hypochlorite), a powerful, cheap disinfectant that is one of the few agents that reliably kills Clostridioides difficile spores on surfaces. Hydrogen peroxide and other peroxygens oxidize cell components and are useful for surfaces and some equipment. Quaternary ammonium compounds (quats) are low-toxicity detergent-like disinfectants for everyday surface cleaning (though weak against some resistant organisms). Finally, high-level chemical agents such as glutaraldehyde, ortho-phthalaldehyde (OPA), and the gas ethylene oxide can achieve sterilization of delicate, heat-sensitive equipment like endoscopes — but they are toxic and demand strict handling.

Matching the agent to the risk

Because no single chemical does everything, hospitals classify items by how much microbial killing they need, a scheme known as the Spaulding classification. Critical items enter sterile tissue or the bloodstream (surgical instruments, catheters, implants) and must be sterilized. Semicritical items touch mucous membranes but not sterile tissue (endoscopes, respiratory equipment) and require at least high-level disinfection. Noncritical items touch only intact skin (blood-pressure cuffs, bedrails, stethoscopes) and need only low- or intermediate-level disinfection. This one framework quietly governs a huge amount of bedside and central-supply practice: it is the reason a scope gets a different reprocessing protocol than a bedrail.

Agent familyMechanismKills spores?Typical use
Alcohols (60–90%)Denature protein, dissolve membraneNoSkin antiseptic, surface wipes, hand rub
ChlorhexidineDisrupts membrane; persists on skinNoSurgical scrub, line-site antisepsis
Iodophors (povidone-iodine)Oxidize / halogenate cell componentsNo (routine use)Pre-op skin prep, injection sites
Chlorine (bleach)Strong oxidizerYes (surfaces)Surface disinfection, C. diff rooms
Hydrogen peroxideOxidizes cell componentsYes (high conc.)Surfaces, wounds, room fogging
QuatsDetergent-like membrane disruptionNoRoutine surface cleaning
Glutaraldehyde / OPA / ethylene oxideAlkylate proteins & DNAYesHeat-sensitive scopes & equipment
Alcohol hand rub is excellent — except against spores. Alcohol-based hand sanitizer is faster and gentler than soap for most everyday hand hygiene, but it does not kill C. difficile or norovirus. When caring for a patient with C. diff, you must wash with soap and water, because the mechanical action physically flushes the spores away that alcohol cannot kill. Reaching for the wall-mounted foam here is a quiet, common error with real consequences.

Interactive Tap each chemical germicide to see if it is an antiseptic or disinfectant and how far up the microbial resistance ladder its level can kill Open full ↑
Key points
  • Chemical agents mostly denature proteins or disrupt membranes; the same molecule can be a disinfectant on surfaces and an antiseptic on skin.
  • Alcohols and chlorhexidine are workhorse skin antiseptics; bleach handles spores on surfaces; glutaraldehyde/OPA/ethylene oxide sterilize heat-sensitive equipment.
  • Use-dilution matters — 70% alcohol out-kills 100% because it needs water.
  • The Spaulding classification (critical / semicritical / noncritical) matches the level of killing to how the item is used.

Section 4The microbial death curve & what changes the odds

Death is logarithmic, not all-at-once

It is tempting to imagine that a disinfectant kills every microbe the instant it is applied, but that is not how killing works. Under a lethal agent, a microbial population dies logarithmically — a constant percentage dies in each equal unit of time, not a constant number. If a chemical kills 90% of the cells in the first minute, it kills 90% of the survivors in the next minute, and 90% of those in the third, and so on. Plotted on a log scale, this makes a straight downward line: the microbial death curve. The practical consequence is profound: because you are always killing a fraction and never the last stubborn cell instantly, you can drive the number vanishingly low but you can never mathematically reach absolute zero in finite time. That is why sterility is defined statistically, as a probability — a sterility assurance level of one survivor in a million (10−6) — rather than as a literal guarantee of nothing alive.

The D-value

The steepness of that line is captured by a single number, the decimal reduction time or D-value: the time required, at a given temperature or agent concentration, to kill 90% of the population — that is, to drop it by one power of ten (one "log"). A microbe or a process with a short D-value is killed quickly; a long D-value means a tough target or a weak method. The D-value is the bridge between the death curve and real protocols: if you know a starting count and the D-value, you can calculate how long a treatment must run to reach your target survival level, which is exactly how sterilization cycles and canning times are set.

What speeds up or slows down the kill

The same agent can kill in seconds or fail entirely depending on conditions, and a nurse who knows the variables can spot when a control measure is being undermined. Killing goes faster with higher temperature, higher concentration or intensity, and longer contact time — the last of which is why every disinfectant label lists a wet contact time that the surface must stay visibly wet to work. Killing goes slower, or fails, when the microbial load is enormous (more cells take longer to clear), when the microbes are inherently resistant, when a protective biofilm shields them, and — crucially at the bedside — when organic matter such as blood, pus, feces, or biofilm is present, because it physically blocks the agent and chemically inactivates many of them. Microbes themselves also fall in a predictable resistance ranking, from the nearly indestructible prions and bacterial endospores down through mycobacteria (their waxy wall makes tuberculosis hard to kill) and non-enveloped viruses, to the easiest targets of all: ordinary vegetative bacteria and fragile enveloped viruses like influenza and HIV.

FactorEffect on killingWhy
Time (contact time)More time → more killEach interval removes another fraction (log)
Temperature / concentrationHigher → faster killSpeeds denaturation and reaction rate
Number of microbesMore cells → slower to clearSame % killed each interval — larger start, longer to finish
Microbe typeSpores & mycobacteria resistEndospore coats and waxy walls block agents
Organic matterPresent → killing failsBlood/pus shield and inactivate the agent
BiofilmPresent → strong resistanceMatrix blocks penetration; cells shielded
Clean before you disinfect — every time. Because blood, pus, and biofilm both physically shield microbes and chemically neutralize disinfectants, wiping a bloody surface with disinfectant may do almost nothing. The rule that a soiled surface must be cleaned first and only then disinfected is not busywork; it is the death curve applied to real life. The same logic explains the wet contact time: the surface must stay wet for the full labeled time or the kill never reaches the target log reduction.

Interactive Slide through exposure time and flip between log and linear scales to see microbes die by a constant fraction. Open full ↑
Key points
  • Microbes die logarithmically — a constant fraction per unit time — so absolute zero is never guaranteed and sterility is a probability (a 10−6 assurance level).
  • The D-value is the time to kill 90% (one log) of a population and sets how long a process must run.
  • Killing speeds up with more time, heat, and concentration; it slows with a big microbial load, resistant organisms, biofilm, and organic matter.
  • Resistance ranks prions & endospores (toughest) → mycobacteria → non-enveloped viruses → vegetative bacteria & enveloped viruses (easiest).

Section 5Asepsis at the bedside — medical vs surgical

Two kinds of asepsis

Asepsis simply means the absence of contamination by pathogens, and at the bedside it is practiced at two different levels. Medical asepsis — also called clean technique — aims to reduce the number and spread of microbes and to break the chain of transmission. It is the everyday level: hand hygiene, gloves, gowns, cleaning shared equipment, and the standard precautions you use with every patient regardless of diagnosis. It does not require sterility; it requires cleanliness and consistency. Surgical asepsis — sterile technique — aims for the total absence of microbes and is used whenever you breach the body's natural barriers: inserting a urinary or central catheter, starting a sterile dressing change on a deep wound, or assisting in the operating room. The mental shift from "clean" to "sterile" is exactly the ladder from Section 1, now applied with gloved hands.

Clean technique: the everyday defense

Medical asepsis rests on a small number of habits that, done reliably, prevent an enormous share of infections. Hand hygiene is first among them — performed before and after every patient contact, before a clean task, after body-fluid exposure, and after touching the patient's surroundings (the familiar moments of hand hygiene). Alcohol-based hand rub is the default because it is fast and effective; soap and water are reserved for visibly soiled hands and for spore-formers like C. diff. Around this core sit gloves and gowns to protect against splashes, safe handling of linens and waste, disinfection of shared devices between patients, and the transmission-based precautions layered on top of standard precautions when a patient carries a specific organism. None of this is glamorous, and all of it works.

Sterile technique: the rules of the field

Surgical asepsis is governed by a set of blunt, non-negotiable rules that exist because a sterile field is only sterile until proven otherwise. Once a sterile package is opened, a field is established, and from that moment: only sterile items may touch sterile items; a 1-inch border around the drape is considered contaminated; anything held below waist level or out of your line of sight is considered contaminated, because you cannot vouch for it; you never reach across or turn your back on the field; and any moisture that wicks through the drape (strike-through) contaminates it, because microbes travel through the wet path. Sterile gloves and gowns extend the field to your hands and front. The underlying philosophy is honest humility: when in doubt about whether something is still sterile, you treat it as contaminated and start over — a re-drape is cheap, a bloodstream infection is not.

FeatureMedical asepsis (clean)Surgical asepsis (sterile)
GoalReduce number & spread of microbesEliminate all microbes
StandardCleanSterile
Typical tasksHand hygiene, gloves/gowns, cleaningCatheter insertion, sterile dressings, OR
Hand practiceHandwash or alcohol rubSurgical hand scrub, sterile gloves
If contaminatedRe-clean and continueDiscard field and restart
When in doubt, it's contaminated. The most useful sentence in sterile technique is also the humblest. If you cannot say for certain that a glove, drape edge, or instrument stayed sterile — it turned below your waist, drifted out of sight, or got a splash of moisture — treat it as contaminated and replace it. Nurses who internalize this early keep central-line and catheter infection rates down, because the field is protected by a habit of doubt, not a hope of luck.

Interactive Toggle clean vs sterile technique, then tap bedside tasks to see which asepsis each one needs Open full ↑
Key points
  • Medical asepsis (clean technique) reduces and contains microbes — hand hygiene, gloves, gowns, cleaning — and is used with every patient.
  • Surgical asepsis (sterile technique) eliminates all microbes and is used whenever a natural barrier is breached.
  • A sterile field is contaminated by anything nonsterile, a 1-inch border, items below the waist or out of sight, and moisture strike-through.
  • The governing rule is "when in doubt, it's contaminated" — discard and restart rather than gamble.

The chapter in one breath

  • Microbial control is a ladder: sterilization (all life, including spores) → disinfection (surfaces) → antisepsis (living tissue) → sanitization (safe reduction).
  • -cidal agents kill; -static agents only stop multiplication and rely on the host's immune system.
  • Physical methods: moist heat beats dry heat; the autoclave (121°C, 15 psi) sterilizes and is verified by a Geobacillus spore indicator; radiation and 0.2 µm filtration handle heat-sensitive items.
  • Chemical methods denature proteins or disrupt membranes; alcohols and chlorhexidine prep skin, bleach kills surface spores, and the Spaulding classification matches killing level to how an item is used.
  • Microbes die logarithmically; the D-value is the time to kill 90%, and sterility is a statistical assurance level, never an absolute.
  • Killing is slowed by big loads, resistant organisms, biofilm, and especially organic matter — so always clean before you disinfect.
  • At the bedside, medical asepsis (clean) is the everyday default and surgical asepsis (sterile) is used whenever a barrier is breached — and when in doubt, it's contaminated.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Antisepsis
The reduction or removal of microbes from living tissue, using an antiseptic.
Antiseptic
A chemical gentle enough to apply to living skin or tissue, e.g., alcohol or chlorhexidine.
Asepsis
The absence of contamination by pathogens; practiced as medical (clean) or surgical (sterile) technique.
Autoclave
A device that sterilizes with pressurized saturated steam, typically 121°C at ~15 psi for ~15 minutes.
Bactericidal / bacteriostatic
Agents that respectively kill bacteria (-cidal) or merely stop them multiplying (-static).
Biological indicator
A vial of heat-resistant spores run through a cycle to confirm true sterilization.
D-value (decimal reduction time)
The time needed to kill 90% (one log) of a microbial population under set conditions.
Degerming
The mechanical removal of microbes from skin, e.g., scrubbing before an injection.
Disinfection
Destruction of most pathogens on an inanimate surface; does not reliably kill endospores.
Endospore
A dormant, extremely resistant bacterial structure that is the toughest target of microbial control.
Filtration
Physical removal of microbes by straining fluid or air through pores too small for cells (e.g., 0.2 µm, HEPA).
Medical asepsis
"Clean technique" that reduces the number and spread of microbes; the everyday level of care.
Microbial death curve
The logarithmic (straight-line, on a log scale) decline of a population under a lethal agent.
Pasteurization
Brief mild heating that lowers pathogen and spoilage counts in liquids without sterilizing.
Sanitization
Reduction of microbial counts to a level judged safe for public health, e.g., dishes and food surfaces.
Spaulding classification
A scheme rating items critical, semicritical, or noncritical to set the required level of processing.
Sterility assurance level (SAL)
The statistical probability of a survivor after sterilization, conventionally one in a million (10−6).
Sterilization
The complete destruction or removal of all microbial life, including endospores.
Surgical asepsis
"Sterile technique" aiming for the total absence of microbes; used when barriers are breached.
Use-dilution
The working concentration at which a chemical agent is effective (more is not always better).

Check yourself

1. A colleague says a bedrail has been "sterilized" with a disinfectant wipe. Why is that word wrong, and what actually happened?
The rail was disinfected, not sterilized. Sterilization removes all microbial life including endospores and is not achieved by a surface wipe; disinfection destroys most pathogens on an inanimate surface but does not reliably kill spores.
2. Distinguish an antiseptic from a disinfectant, and explain why you should never treat the two as interchangeable.
An antiseptic is used on living tissue and must be gentle; a disinfectant is used on inanimate objects and can be much harsher. Putting a surface disinfectant on skin or in a wound injures tissue, so the target — living vs nonliving — determines which you may use.
3. A drug is labeled "bacteriostatic." What does that mean, and why does it matter more in an immunocompromised patient?
Bacteriostatic means it stops bacteria from multiplying but does not kill them. The living, halted bacteria still depend on the patient's immune system to clear them — so in someone immunocompromised, a static agent may fail where the host defenses cannot finish the job.
4. Why does ordinary boiling at 100°C fail to sterilize, and how does the autoclave overcome that limit?
Boiling kills vegetative cells and most viruses but not endospores. The autoclave raises the pressure (~15 psi), which raises steam's boiling point to 121°C — hot enough moist heat to destroy endospores in about 15 minutes.
5. Autoclave tape on a pack has turned dark. Does that prove the contents are sterile? Explain.
No — the tape only shows the pack was exposed to heat, not that the cycle held long enough to sterilize. True verification comes from a biological indicator: a vial of Geobacillus stearothermophilus spores that must be dead after the run.
6. A hospital needs to sterilize prepackaged plastic syringes and IV tubing that would melt in an autoclave. Name two suitable methods.
Ionizing radiation (gamma, X-ray, or electron beam) and ethylene oxide gas both sterilize prepackaged, heat-sensitive plastics in the cold. These sterilize without the high temperatures that would damage plastic. (Membrane filtration, by contrast, sterilizes heat-labile fluids, not solid items like syringes and tubing.)
7. Why is 70% alcohol a better antiseptic than 100% alcohol?
Alcohol needs water to denature proteins effectively. 70% alcohol contains the water that lets it coagulate microbial proteins and penetrate cells, so it kills better than pure (100%) alcohol, which dehydrates the surface too fast.
8. You are caring for a patient with Clostridioides difficile. Why must you wash with soap and water instead of using alcohol hand rub?
Alcohol does not kill C. diff spores. Only the mechanical action of soap and water physically flushes the spores off your hands, so alcohol-based rub is inadequate for this organism.
9. Using the Spaulding classification, explain why a surgical scalpel and a blood-pressure cuff are processed differently.
The scalpel is a critical item (it enters sterile tissue) and must be sterilized; the cuff is a noncritical item (it touches only intact skin) and needs only low- or intermediate-level disinfection. The required level of killing matches how the item contacts the patient.
10. Explain what the microbial death curve tells us and why "sterile" is defined as a probability.
Microbes die logarithmically — a constant fraction per unit time — so the count keeps dropping by tenfold intervals but never mathematically hits zero. Because absolute zero can't be guaranteed, sterility is defined statistically as a sterility assurance level, conventionally one survivor in a million (10−6).
11. Define the D-value and describe one way it is used in practice.
The D-value (decimal reduction time) is the time needed to kill 90% — one log — of a population under set conditions. Knowing the starting count and D-value lets you calculate how long a sterilization or canning process must run to reach the target survival level.
12. During a sterile catheter insertion, the sterile field drifts below your waist for a moment. What is the correct action and the principle behind it?
Treat it as contaminated and re-establish the field. In surgical asepsis, anything below waist level or out of your line of sight is considered contaminated — the rule is "when in doubt, it's contaminated," so you discard and restart rather than gamble on a bloodstream infection.
Microbiology · Chapter 9

Antimicrobial Drugs

Few discoveries have saved more lives than the antibiotic. In the span of a single lifetime, infections that once emptied hospital wards — pneumonia, sepsis, a wound gone bad — became, for the most part, curable. That victory rests on one elegant idea: a bacterium is built differently from you, and a well-chosen drug can exploit that difference to kill the microbe while leaving your patient unharmed. This chapter unpacks how antimicrobials work, how we pick the right one, and why bacteria are fighting back. Along the way you will see that the nurse is not a bystander to antibiotic therapy but one of its most important stewards — deciding when cultures are drawn, whether a dose lands on time, and whether the whole enterprise keeps working for the next patient.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Explain selective toxicity and why it makes antimicrobial therapy possible.
  • Define the therapeutic index and connect a narrow index to the need for drug-level monitoring.
  • Name the five bacterial targets antibacterial drugs attack and give a drug class for each.
  • Distinguish bactericidal from bacteriostatic and narrow- from broad-spectrum drugs.
  • Describe how susceptibility testing (MIC, Kirby–Bauer, Etest) guides drug choice.
  • Summarize the four main resistance mechanisms and how resistance genes spread.
  • Explain what MRSA is and why it matters at the bedside.
  • State the goals of antibiotic stewardship and the nurse's concrete role in it.

Section 1What makes a good antimicrobial

Selective toxicity: hurt the microbe, spare the patient

The reason we can pour a poison into a sick person and call it medicine is a principle named selective toxicity: a good antimicrobial harms the microbe far more than it harms the host. This is possible because a bacterium is a prokaryote — it is built from parts your own cells simply do not have. It wraps itself in a rigid cell wall made of peptidoglycan; your cells have no wall. Its ribosomes (the machines that build proteins) are a different size and shape from yours. It makes its own folic acid from scratch, while you get folate from your diet. Each of those differences is a handle a drug can grab. Aim at a structure the microbe has and the patient lacks, and you get a cure without collateral damage.

Selective toxicity is a matter of degree, not perfection. The drugs with the widest safety margin tend to be those that attack the cell wall — the penicillins and cephalosporins — precisely because human cells have no equivalent target to injure. Drugs that hit something bacteria and humans share, even a little, are more likely to cause the toxicities nurses watch for: an aminoglycoside can damage the kidney and the inner ear, and an antifungal that targets the fungal cell membrane can also disturb the patient's own membranes because both are built from sterols. The closer a microbe is to us biologically — a fungus, a protozoan, a human cell hijacked by a virus — the harder selective toxicity becomes, which is exactly why antibacterials are gentler than most antifungals and antivirals.

The therapeutic index: how much room for error

Every drug has a dose that helps and a higher dose that harms. The therapeutic index puts a number on the gap between them — roughly, the toxic dose divided by the effective dose. A large index means a comfortable margin: you would have to overshoot badly before the patient got hurt, which is why penicillin can be given in generous doses with little worry. A small or narrow therapeutic index means the helpful dose and the harmful dose sit uncomfortably close together, so the drug must be dosed carefully and, often, measured directly in the blood.

This is where the graph on the right becomes a bedside tool. The goal is to keep the drug's concentration inside the therapeutic window — high enough to kill bacteria, low enough to spare the patient. For narrow-index antimicrobials such as vancomycin and the aminoglycosides (gentamicin, tobramycin), we do not guess. We draw blood levels: a trough just before the next dose to confirm the drug has not built up to toxic levels, and sometimes a peak to confirm it climbed high enough to work.

DrugTherapeutic indexWhat that means for nursing
PenicillinsWideLarge safety margin; routine levels not needed
CephalosporinsWideWell tolerated; watch for allergy, not toxicity
VancomycinNarrowMonitor trough levels; watch kidney function
AminoglycosidesNarrowMonitor peak & trough; risk of kidney & ear toxicity
A drug level is only as good as its timing. A vancomycin trough must be drawn right before the next dose — not an hour early, not after it hangs. Draw it at the wrong moment and the number is meaningless, the pharmacist doses off a bad value, and the patient is either under-treated or pushed toward kidney injury. For narrow-index drugs, the nurse's clock is part of the therapy.
Interactive Slide the antimicrobial dose between subtherapeutic, effective, and toxic — and see how selective toxicity sets the window's width Open full ↑
Key points
  • Selective toxicity — hitting a target the microbe has and the host lacks — is what makes antimicrobial therapy possible.
  • Cell-wall drugs are the safest because human cells have no wall to injure.
  • The therapeutic index is the gap between the helpful dose and the harmful one.
  • Narrow-index drugs (vancomycin, aminoglycosides) need blood-level monitoring; timing of the draw is everything.

Section 2How the drugs work

Five ways to kill a bacterium

For all their variety, antibacterial drugs attack a bacterium in only a handful of places. Learn these five targets and you can slot almost any antibiotic you meet into a mental map — and, more usefully, predict its selective toxicity and its side effects.

1. Cell wall synthesis. The peptidoglycan wall is the bacterium's pressure vessel; without it the cell bursts. Penicillins, cephalosporins, and vancomycin block wall construction, so growing bacteria lyse. Because human cells have no wall, this is the most selectively toxic target of all. Drugs in the penicillin and cephalosporin families share a chemical ring called a beta-lactam, which is why they are grouped as beta-lactam antibiotics.

2. Protein synthesis. Bacterial ribosomes (built from 30S and 50S subunits) differ from the human 40S/60S version, so a drug can jam the bacterial machine while sparing yours. Aminoglycosides and tetracyclines hit the 30S subunit; macrolides (azithromycin, erythromycin) and clindamycin hit the 50S. The match is imperfect, however — your mitochondria carry bacteria-like ribosomes, which helps explain some of these drugs' toxicities.

3. Nucleic acid synthesis. Some drugs block the enzymes bacteria use to copy and unwind DNA. The fluoroquinolones (ciprofloxacin, levofloxacin) inhibit bacterial DNA gyrase and topoisomerase, enzymes shaped differently from the human versions.

4. Metabolic (folate) pathways. Bacteria must synthesize folic acid themselves; you eat yours. Sulfonamides and trimethoprim starve the bacterium of folate by blocking two steps of that pathway — a target you simply do not possess.

5. Cell membrane. A few drugs punch holes in the bacterial membrane, causing the cell's contents to leak out. These tend to be more toxic, because your cells have membranes too; they are reserved for stubborn infections.

Kill it or just stop it: -cidal vs -static

Drugs also differ in how thoroughly they act. A bactericidal drug kills the bacterium outright (penicillins, aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones). A bacteriostatic drug merely halts its growth (tetracyclines, macrolides, sulfonamides), then leaves the finishing blow to the patient's own immune system. In a healthy person, static is often enough. In a patient whose immune defenses are down — a neutropenic chemotherapy patient, someone with endocarditis or meningitis — the team usually reaches for a bactericidal drug, because there is no reliable immune backup to mop up survivors.

TargetExample drug classesWhy it is selective
Cell wall synthesisPenicillins, cephalosporins, vancomycinHuman cells have no cell wall
Protein synthesis (ribosome)Aminoglycosides, tetracyclines, macrolidesBacterial ribosome (70S) differs from human (80S)
Nucleic acid synthesisFluoroquinolonesBacterial DNA enzymes differ from human ones
Folate metabolismSulfonamides, trimethoprimHumans get folate from diet; bacteria make it
Cell membranePolymyxins, daptomycinLeast selective — reserved for resistant cases
“Penicillin allergy” is a target clue, not just a chart flag. Because penicillins and cephalosporins share the beta-lactam ring, a patient with a true, severe penicillin allergy may react to some cephalosporins too. Always verify what actually happened with a reported allergy — a childhood rash is very different from throat-closing anaphylaxis — because mislabeling drives patients toward broader, more toxic, more resistance-prone alternatives.
Interactive Tap the five structures on a bacterial cell that antibacterial drugs attack Open full ↑
Key points
  • Antibacterials hit five targets: cell wall, ribosome, nucleic acids, folate metabolism, and cell membrane.
  • Beta-lactams (penicillins, cephalosporins) attack the cell wall — the safest, most selective target.
  • Bactericidal drugs kill; bacteriostatic drugs stall growth and rely on the immune system to finish.
  • Immunocompromised patients usually need a bactericidal drug.

Section 3Spectrum and choosing a drug

Narrow versus broad spectrum

A drug's spectrum of activity is simply the range of bacteria it works against. A narrow-spectrum drug hits only a few kinds of bacteria; a broad-spectrum drug hits many. Broad sounds better, and in a crisis it can be lifesaving — but breadth has a cost. A broad-spectrum antibiotic clear-cuts the patient's normal flora along with the pathogen, opening the door to a superinfection such as Clostridioides difficile colitis or a yeast overgrowth, and every unnecessary exposure nudges the whole bacterial population toward resistance. The goal, whenever the culprit is known, is the narrowest drug that will do the job.

Empiric first, then definitive

Cultures take time, and a septic patient cannot wait a day or two for results. So therapy usually happens in two stages. Empiric therapy is the educated first guess: based on the likely source, the local resistance patterns, and how sick the patient is, the team starts a broad-spectrum drug immediately — but only after cultures are drawn. Once the lab reports what grew and what it is susceptible to, the team switches to definitive therapy, ideally narrowing (or de-escalating) to a focused drug. This is why the order in which things happen at the bedside matters so much: cultures first, then antibiotics.

Susceptibility testing: proving the drug will work

How does the lab know which drug to recommend? By testing the patient's own isolate against a panel of antibiotics. The key number is the minimum inhibitory concentration (MIC) — the lowest drug concentration that stops the bacterium from growing. Several methods get at it. In the classic Kirby–Bauer (disk-diffusion) test, paper disks soaked in different antibiotics sit on a lawn of bacteria; the larger the clear zone of inhibition around a disk, the more susceptible the organism — though disk diffusion sorts the isolate into susceptible or resistant rather than reading out an exact MIC. The Etest strip in the figure refines this into an actual MIC value, and automated broth microdilution systems do the same in tiny wells. The result comes back to the chart as S (susceptible), I (intermediate), or R (resistant) — a report the nurse reads alongside the team when a patient is not improving on the current drug.

 Narrow-spectrumBroad-spectrum
Bacteria coveredFew, targetedMany
Effect on normal floraSpares most of itWipes out much of it
Superinfection riskLowerHigher (C. diff, yeast)
Resistance pressureLowerHigher
Best used whenThe pathogen is knownEmpirically, while awaiting cultures
Cultures before the first dose. Once antibiotics are on board, they can sterilize a blood or wound sample within hours, and a falsely negative culture leaves the team flying blind for the rest of the admission. If a patient is about to start antibiotics, the nurse's job is to make sure the ordered cultures are collected first — it is one of the highest-value habits in infection care.
Interactive Pick a drug from narrow to very broad and watch what its spectrum kills — the infection versus the patient's protective normal flora Open full ↑
Key points
  • Spectrum is the range of bacteria a drug covers; narrow is preferred once the pathogen is known.
  • Broad-spectrum drugs invite superinfection and resistance — use them empirically, then narrow.
  • Empiric therapy is the first guess; definitive therapy follows the culture and de-escalates.
  • The MIC is the lowest concentration that inhibits growth; results are reported as S / I / R.

Section 4How bacteria fight back

Four ways to survive the drug

Resistance is not the drug “wearing off.” It is evolution in fast-forward: a drug kills the susceptible bacteria and leaves the rare survivor that happened to carry a defense, and that survivor's descendants inherit the trick. There are four main defenses, and the figure lays them out.

Destroy the drug. The most famous example is beta-lactamase, an enzyme that snips open the beta-lactam ring of penicillins before it can work. This is why some penicillins are combined with a beta-lactamase inhibitor (as in amoxicillin–clavulanate) to protect the drug.

Change the target. If the drug can no longer recognize its binding site, it cannot act. This is the trick behind MRSA: the bacterium acquires an altered penicillin-binding protein that beta-lactams cannot grip.

Pump the drug out. Efflux pumps in the membrane actively bail the antibiotic back out before it reaches a lethal concentration inside the cell.

Keep the drug out. By altering the porins or the permeability of its outer membrane, a bacterium can simply reduce how much drug gets in at all.

How resistance spreads — and why it spreads so fast

Resistance can arise by spontaneous mutation, but its terrifying speed comes from horizontal gene transfer: bacteria pass resistance genes to one another, even across species, on mobile loops of DNA called plasmids. A single plasmid can carry resistance to several drug classes at once, so one exposure can select for a strain resistant to antibiotics it never even met. Every unnecessary antibiotic anywhere — in a clinic, a farm, a fish tank — adds to the selective pressure that favors these survivors.

The named threats: MRSA and friends

MRSA — methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus — is resistant to essentially all beta-lactam antibiotics, which knocks out the safest, most familiar drugs and forces the team toward vancomycin and its relatives. It spreads readily in hospitals and communities, so patients known to carry it are often placed on contact precautions. It is not alone: VRE (vancomycin-resistant enterococci), ESBL-producing and carbapenem-resistant gram-negatives, and multidrug-resistant tuberculosis all crowd the same worrying list. The common thread is that our shelf of reliable drugs is shrinking faster than we are refilling it.

MechanismWhat the bacterium doesExample
Drug inactivationMakes an enzyme that destroys the drugBeta-lactamase vs penicillins
Target modificationAlters the drug's binding siteMRSA's altered binding protein
Efflux pumpPumps the drug back outTetracycline resistance
Reduced uptakeKeeps the drug from enteringAltered porins in gram-negatives
Contact precautions are chemotherapy for the whole unit. A gown and gloves for a MRSA-colonized patient can feel like a nuisance for a wound that looks fine — until you remember the organism travels on hands and bedrails to the next patient, who may be far more vulnerable. Consistent precautions and hand hygiene are how a nurse keeps one patient's resistant organism from becoming the whole floor's problem.
Interactive Tap the four ways bacteria defeat an antibiotic and watch the drug get stopped Open full ↑
Key points
  • Bacteria resist drugs four ways: destroy the drug, change the target, pump it out, or keep it out.
  • Resistance genes spread fast by horizontal gene transfer on plasmids, driven by selective pressure.
  • MRSA resists all beta-lactams by altering the drug's binding site, forcing a switch to vancomycin.
  • Contact precautions and hand hygiene contain resistant organisms on the unit.

Section 5Stewardship and the nurse

What stewardship means

Antibiotic stewardship is the coordinated effort to use these drugs only when they help, and to use them well. It is often summarized as the five rights of antimicrobials: the right drug, at the right dose, by the right route, for the right duration, in the right patient. The purpose is twofold — better outcomes for the patient in front of you (fewer side effects, less C. diff, shorter stays) and protection of the drugs themselves for the patients who come after. Because resistance is a shared resource being spent, stewardship is genuinely a public-health duty, not just a cost-control exercise.

The nurse is a steward — here is how

Stewardship is often described as a physician-and-pharmacist activity, but the nurse touches nearly every point of it. You are the one who draws cultures before the first dose, who gives antibiotics on time so blood levels stay effective, and who monitors the patient for both improvement and toxicity. You are positioned to ask the questions that trim unnecessary drug use: Can this IV antibiotic switch to an oral one now that the patient is eating and improving? Is this still needed on day 5, or is it just still ordered? Are two drugs covering the same bug? And you are the last check on the allergy history — clarifying a vague “penicillin allergy” so the patient is not pushed onto a broader, more toxic alternative for no reason.

Adherence and what you teach the patient

Stewardship reaches beyond the hospital, and here patient teaching is the tool. Explain that antibiotics do nothing for colds, flu, and most sore throats — and that demanding them “just in case” carries real risk with no benefit. Teach patients to take the drug exactly as prescribed, to never save or share leftover antibiotics, and to never use someone else's. The old blanket rule to “always finish every pill” is being refined — some infections do better with shorter courses — so the honest, durable message is simpler and stronger: take it the way it was prescribed, and call before stopping early or doubling up.

Stewardship goalWhat the nurse does
Right diagnosisCollect cultures before the first antibiotic dose
Right timingGive doses on schedule to keep levels effective
Right durationPrompt the team to review or de-escalate ongoing orders
Right routeFlag when a stable, eating patient could switch IV → oral
Right patientVerify and clarify the allergy history
PreventionHand hygiene, timely device removal, patient teaching
“Is this antibiotic still needed?” is a nursing question. An order placed on day one has a way of quietly continuing for a week because no one revisited it. A nurse who raises the day-of-therapy at rounds — or simply asks whether the IV can go oral — prevents excess exposure, superinfection, and resistance without any new technology. Curiosity, voiced out loud, is a stewardship intervention.
Interactive Build an antibiotic plan by setting the right drug, dose, duration, and adherence support, and watch the infection clear or breed resistance Open full ↑
Key points
  • Stewardship = right drug, dose, route, duration, and patient — for the patient now and for future patients.
  • The nurse steward draws cultures first, gives doses on time, and watches for toxicity.
  • Advocate to de-escalate, switch IV to oral, and clarify allergies.
  • Teach adherence: take as prescribed, never share or save antibiotics, and don't expect them for viral illness.

The chapter in one breath

  • Selective toxicity — attacking a microbe-only target — is why antimicrobials cure without poisoning the patient.
  • The therapeutic index is the safety margin; narrow-index drugs like vancomycin and aminoglycosides need blood-level monitoring.
  • Antibacterials hit five targets: cell wall, ribosome, nucleic acids, folate metabolism, and cell membrane.
  • Drugs are bactericidal or bacteriostatic, and narrow- or broad-spectrum; narrow and cidal have their place, and so does empiric breadth.
  • Susceptibility testing (MIC via Kirby–Bauer, Etest, or microdilution) reports S / I / R to guide definitive therapy — after cultures are drawn.
  • Bacteria resist by destroying, dodging, ejecting, or excluding the drug, and spread resistance fast via plasmids; MRSA is the headline example.
  • Stewardship protects both the patient and the drugs, and the nurse is a frontline steward — cultures first, doses on time, de-escalation advocated, adherence taught.
