Susceptibility testing
Laboratory testing of a patient's isolate against antibiotics, reported as S, I, or R.
Therapeutic index
The safety margin between a drug's effective dose and its toxic dose.
Trough level
The drug concentration measured just before the next dose, used to avoid toxic accumulation.

Check yourself

1. Explain the principle of selective toxicity and give one bacterial structure that makes it possible.
Selective toxicity means harming the microbe far more than the host by targeting something the microbe has but the patient does not. The peptidoglycan cell wall is the classic example: human cells have no wall, so cell-wall drugs kill bacteria without injuring the patient.
2. What is the therapeutic index, and why do vancomycin and aminoglycosides require blood-level monitoring?
The therapeutic index is the gap between the effective dose and the toxic dose. Vancomycin and aminoglycosides have a narrow index — the helpful and harmful doses are close — so levels (like a trough) are drawn to keep the drug inside the therapeutic window and off the kidney and inner ear.
3. A vancomycin trough is ordered. When should it be drawn, and why does the timing matter?
Right before the next scheduled dose. A trough drawn early or after the dose gives a false value; the pharmacist then doses off bad data, risking under-treatment or kidney injury. For narrow-index drugs the nurse's timing is part of the therapy.
4. Name the five bacterial targets antibacterial drugs attack, with one drug class for each.
Cell wall (penicillins/cephalosporins/vancomycin), protein synthesis/ribosome (aminoglycosides, tetracyclines, macrolides), nucleic acid synthesis (fluoroquinolones), folate metabolism (sulfonamides, trimethoprim), and cell membrane (polymyxins, daptomycin). Cell-wall drugs are the most selectively toxic because humans have no wall.
5. Distinguish bactericidal from bacteriostatic, and say which is generally preferred in an immunocompromised patient.
Bactericidal drugs kill bacteria; bacteriostatic drugs only stop their growth and rely on the immune system to finish. An immunocompromised patient (e.g., neutropenic, or with meningitis/endocarditis) usually needs a bactericidal drug because there is no dependable immune backup.
6. Why can a patient with a severe penicillin allergy sometimes react to cephalosporins?
Penicillins and cephalosporins share the beta-lactam ring, so an immune response to one can cross-react with the other. This is why the nurse verifies what the reaction actually was — a mild rash is very different from anaphylaxis — before a broader drug is chosen.
7. Compare narrow- and broad-spectrum antibiotics. Name two downsides of broad-spectrum use.
Narrow-spectrum drugs hit few bacteria; broad-spectrum drugs hit many. Broad-spectrum downsides: they disrupt the normal flora and invite superinfection (like C. diff or yeast), and they apply more selective pressure for resistance. Broad is best used empirically, then narrowed.
8. Distinguish empiric from definitive therapy, and state what must happen before the first dose.
Empiric therapy is the educated first guess started before results; definitive therapy is the focused drug chosen after cultures identify the pathogen and its susceptibilities. Cultures must be drawn before the first antibiotic dose, or the drug can sterilize the sample and hide the culprit.
9. What does the MIC tell you, and what do the letters S, I, and R mean on a susceptibility report?
The MIC is the lowest drug concentration that stops the organism from growing. On the report, S = susceptible (the drug should work), I = intermediate, and R = resistant (the drug is not a reliable choice).
10. List the four main ways bacteria resist antibiotics, and give the mechanism MRSA uses.
Destroy the drug (e.g., beta-lactamase), change the target, pump the drug out (efflux), or keep the drug out (reduced uptake). MRSA uses target modification — an altered penicillin-binding protein that beta-lactams can no longer grip — which is why it resists all beta-lactams.
11. Why does antibiotic resistance spread so quickly between bacteria?
Because of horizontal gene transfer: bacteria share resistance genes — even across species — on mobile plasmids, and one plasmid can carry resistance to several drugs at once. Every unnecessary antibiotic adds selective pressure favoring the resistant survivors.
12. Give three concrete things a bedside nurse does that qualify as antibiotic stewardship.
Any three: draw cultures before the first dose; give doses on time to keep levels effective; advocate to de-escalate or switch IV to oral; clarify the allergy history; teach patients to take antibiotics as prescribed and not to share or save them. Each trims unnecessary exposure and protects both the patient and the drugs.
Microbiology · Chapter 10

How Microbes Cause Disease

A pathogen does not make your patient sick by magic. It follows a script — get in, hold on, multiply, do damage, and dodge the immune system long enough to spread to the next host. Every step of that script is powered by a specific molecular tool, and every tool is a target that nursing care, drugs, and vaccines are built to block. This chapter takes apart the machinery of disease: what makes one microbe a killer and another a harmless bystander, how germs breach the body's borders and anchor themselves, the toxins and enzymes they use to wreck tissue, the disguises they wear to stay hidden, and the predictable stages an infection passes through from the moment of exposure to recovery. Understand the script, and you understand why we do almost everything we do at the bedside.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Distinguish pathogenicity from virulence, and explain what virulence factors are.
  • Interpret ID₅₀ and LD₅₀ as measures of how infectious or deadly a microbe is.
  • Name the major portals of entry and explain why the same microbe can be harmless at one portal and lethal at another.
  • Describe how adhesins let pathogens attach, and why attachment is a drug and vaccine target.
  • Compare exotoxins and endotoxin in source, chemistry, potency, and clinical effect.
  • Explain how capsules, antigenic variation, and intracellular hiding let microbes evade host defenses.
  • List the five stages of infection and identify when a patient is most contagious.
  • Connect each step of the disease process to a specific nursing or public-health intervention.

Section 1Pathogenicity and virulence

Two words that are not the same

In everyday speech "pathogenic" and "virulent" get used interchangeably, but microbiology draws a sharp line between them, and the distinction is worth keeping straight. Pathogenicity is the ability of a microbe to cause disease at all — a yes-or-no property. Virulence is the degree of that ability — how much harm, how fast, at how small a dose. Two organisms can both be pathogens while sitting at opposite ends of the virulence scale: Clostridium botulinum is staggeringly virulent (on the order of a nanogram of its toxin per kilogram of body weight can kill), while a common cold rhinovirus is a genuine pathogen that rarely does more than make you miserable for a week.

It also matters whose pathogen we mean. A primary pathogen can cause disease in an otherwise healthy person with intact defenses — influenza, Salmonella, Mycobacterium tuberculosis. An opportunistic pathogen only causes disease when it gets an opening: a breached barrier, wiped-out normal flora, or a weakened host. Much of what a nurse guards against — catheter infections, ventilator pneumonia, Candida overgrowth — is opportunistic disease caused by organisms that would be harmless in a healthy person.

Virulence factors: the toolkit of harm

What makes one strain more virulent than another comes down to virulence factors — the specific genes and molecules that let a microbe establish itself and cause damage. These are not vague qualities; they are concrete structures and secreted products, and the whole rest of this chapter is essentially a tour of them. They fall into a few functional buckets: tools for attaching (adhesins), tools for spreading through tissue (invasins and enzymes), tools for damaging the host (toxins), and tools for evading defenses (capsules, antigenic variation). Because each factor is a distinct molecule, each one is a potential target — an antibody a vaccine can raise, an enzyme a drug can inhibit, a toxin an antitoxin can neutralize.

Measuring virulence: ID₅₀ and LD₅₀

Virulence can be quantified, which is how we compare organisms objectively. The ID₅₀ (median infectious dose) is the number of microbes needed to establish infection in half of an exposed population; the LD₅₀ (median lethal dose) is the amount of a microbe or toxin needed to kill half of them. A lower number means a more virulent agent, because it takes fewer organisms to do the damage. Shigella has an ID₅₀ of only 10 to 100 cells — which is exactly why it spreads so easily person-to-person and why hand hygiene is decisive — whereas Vibrio cholerae needs roughly a hundred million organisms, so it usually takes contaminated water to deliver a big enough dose.

TermWhat it measuresLow number means
PathogenicityWhether a microbe can cause disease (yes/no)—
VirulenceHow severe the disease it causes—
ID₅₀Dose that infects 50% of a populationVery infectious (spreads at low dose)
LD₅₀Dose that kills 50% of a populationVery deadly (lethal at low dose)
A low ID₅₀ is a hand-hygiene emergency. Organisms like Shigella, norovirus, and C. difficile take only a handful of particles to infect. That is why a single missed hand wash can start an outbreak on a unit, and why these bugs drive contact-precaution and dedicated-equipment policies. The dose is so small that "a little exposure" is all it takes.
Interactive Pick a pathogen and drag the exposure dose to compare avirulent vs highly virulent microbes by their ID₅₀ Open full ↑
Key points
  • Pathogenicity is the ability to cause disease; virulence is how much harm it does.
  • Primary pathogens sicken healthy hosts; opportunistic pathogens need a breach, wiped-out flora, or a weakened host.
  • Virulence factors are the specific molecules for attaching, spreading, damaging, and evading — each one a drug or vaccine target.
  • ID₅₀ and LD₅₀ quantify virulence; a lower value means a more dangerous organism.

Section 2Getting in and attaching

Portals of entry

A microbe cannot cause infection until it reaches tissue it can colonize, and it gets there through a portal of entry — a specific route into the body. The great majority of pathogens enter through the mucous membranes of the respiratory tract (by far the most common — influenza, tuberculosis, the common cold), the gastrointestinal tract (Salmonella, hepatitis A, norovirus), the genitourinary tract (gonorrhea, chlamydia), or the conjunctiva of the eye. Others enter through the skin, which is normally an excellent barrier but is breachable through cuts, bites, burns, and surgical wounds. A special case is the parenteral route, where an organism is deposited directly into deeper tissue or the bloodstream — a needlestick, an IV line, a puncture wound — bypassing the surface defenses entirely.

The clinical punchline is that the same organism can be harmless at one portal and dangerous at another. Escherichia coli is a peaceful resident of the colon, but delivered to the bladder it causes a UTI, and reaching the bloodstream it can cause fatal sepsis. Every invasive device a nurse manages — a urinary catheter, a central line, an endotracheal tube — is essentially an artificial portal of entry that hands a microbe a shortcut past the body's borders. That is why device insertion is done sterile and why prompt removal is one of the most reliable infection-prevention moves there is.

Portal of entryRouteExample diseases
Respiratory tractInhaled droplets/aerosolsInfluenza, TB, COVID-19, colds
GI tractIngested (fecal–oral, food, water)Salmonellosis, hepatitis A, norovirus
Genitourinary tractSexual contact, ascendingGonorrhea, chlamydia, UTIs
Skin / mucous breaksCuts, bites, burns, woundsCellulitis, tetanus, rabies
ParenteralDirectly into tissue/bloodLine infections, hepatitis B, HIV

Adhesion: sticking around

Getting in is not enough — a microbe that cannot anchor itself is swept away by mucus, urine flow, peristalsis, or the tide of tears and saliva. So the next step is attachment, accomplished by adhesins: surface molecules (often on fimbriae, pili, or in the capsule and glycocalyx) that bind like a key to specific receptor molecules on host cells. This binding is precise and often explains a pathogen's tropism — why a given microbe infects one tissue and not another. When many organisms adhere and then encase themselves in a slimy matrix, they form a biofilm, a community that is dramatically harder for antibiotics and immune cells to penetrate. Biofilms on catheters, prosthetic joints, and heart valves are a major reason device-associated infections are so stubborn.

Because attachment is a mandatory first move, it is a favorite target for prevention. Some vaccines work by raising antibodies against a pathogen's adhesins so the microbe can never gain a foothold, and part of what hand hygiene, catheter care, and oral care in ventilated patients accomplish is physically removing organisms before they establish a biofilm.

Infectious dose

How many organisms does it actually take? That is the infectious dose, captured numerically by the ID₅₀ from Section 1. It depends on both the microbe's virulence factors and the host's defenses, which is why an exposure that does nothing to a healthy adult can seed a serious infection in an immunocompromised or very young patient. A pathogen armed with strong adhesins and a good evasion toolkit needs far fewer organisms to succeed, which loops back to why low-dose organisms demand such strict precautions.

Every line and tube is a portal you created. Central lines, Foleys, and ET tubes each punch straight through a natural barrier. The bundle habits — sterile insertion, daily "does this still need to be in?" review, scrupulous site care — exist because the fastest way to close an artificial portal of entry is to remove the device.
Interactive Tap a portal on the body diagram to see which pathogens enter there and the adhesins they grip host cells with Open full ↑
Key points
  • Pathogens enter through portals of entry: respiratory, GI, GU, conjunctiva, skin breaks, and the parenteral route.
  • The same microbe can be harmless at one portal and lethal at another (E. coli: colon vs. bladder vs. blood).
  • Adhesins anchor microbes to specific host receptors; clumped, matrix-encased communities become drug-resistant biofilms.
  • Invasive devices are artificial portals of entry — sterile insertion and early removal are core nursing defenses.

Section 3Causing damage: exotoxins and endotoxin

Exotoxins: precision weapons

Much of the harm a pathogen does comes from toxins, and the more dramatic of the two families is the exotoxin. Exotoxins are proteins that bacteria manufacture and secrete, produced by both gram-positive and gram-negative organisms. Being proteins, they are exquisitely specific and often astonishingly potent — the toxins of tetanus, botulism, and diphtheria are among the most lethal substances known. They come in three broad styles. A-B toxins have two parts: a B (binding) subunit that latches onto a target cell and an A (active) subunit that enters and sabotages a specific internal process — diphtheria toxin halts protein synthesis, cholera toxin drives the massive fluid loss of rice-water diarrhea, and botulinum and tetanus toxins block neurotransmitter release to paralyze muscle. Membrane-disrupting toxins punch holes in host-cell membranes; these include the hemolysins that lyse red cells and the leukocidins that kill white cells. Superantigens short-circuit the immune system, triggering a huge, indiscriminate release of cytokines that produces the shock of toxic shock syndrome.

Two clinically useful facts follow from exotoxins being proteins. First, they are usually heat-labile — destroyed by cooking — which is why proper food handling prevents many toxin-mediated illnesses. Second, a protein toxin can be chemically inactivated into a toxoid that no longer harms but still provokes protective antibodies; the tetanus and diphtheria components of routine vaccines are toxoids, and pre-formed antitoxin antibodies can be given to neutralize circulating toxin in an acute poisoning.

Endotoxin: the gram-negative alarm

The second toxin is not secreted and not a protein. Endotoxin is the lipid A portion of lipopolysaccharide (LPS), a fixed component of the outer membrane of gram-negative bacteria. It is only released when the bacterial cell is damaged or dies — including when antibiotics lyse large numbers of organisms. Any single molecule of endotoxin is far weaker than a potent exotoxin, but the body's reaction to it is powerful and generic: fever, vasodilation with falling blood pressure, and widespread activation of clotting. When a bloodstream infection floods the body with endotoxin, that reaction becomes septic shock — dangerously low blood pressure and the microclotting of disseminated intravascular coagulation (DIC). Unlike a protein exotoxin, endotoxin is heat-stable and cannot be made into a toxoid, so there is no vaccine against it.

FeatureExotoxinEndotoxin
ChemistryProtein, secretedLipid A of LPS (outer membrane)
SourceGram-positive & gram-negativeGram-negative only
ReleasedActively secreted by living cellOnly when the cell is damaged/lysed
PotencyVery high; specific effectLower per molecule; general effect
Heat stabilityUsually heat-labileHeat-stable
Toxoid / vaccine?Yes (tetanus, diphtheria)No
Typical effectSpecific (paralysis, diarrhea, etc.)Fever, hypotension, shock, DIC
Killing the bug can briefly make the patient worse. When antibiotics rupture a large load of gram-negative bacteria, they dump a surge of endotoxin into the blood — a spike of fever, chills, and dropping pressure right after the first dose (a Jarisch–Herxheimer-type reaction). Anticipate it, monitor vitals closely after starting therapy in sepsis, and don't mistake it for treatment failure.
Interactive Toggle exotoxin vs endotoxin and tap features to compare them side by side Open full ↑
Key points
  • Exotoxins are secreted proteins — potent, specific, often heat-labile, and convertible to protective toxoids.
  • Exotoxin styles: A-B toxins, membrane-disrupting hemolysins/leukocidins, and superantigens.
  • Endotoxin is lipid A of LPS from gram-negative cells, released on lysis; it is heat-stable and has no toxoid.
  • A flood of endotoxin drives fever, hypotension, septic shock, and DIC.

Section 4Evading the body's defenses

Capsules: the invisibility cloak

Getting in, holding on, and doing damage still isn't enough if the immune system mops the microbe up. So successful pathogens carry tools for immune evasion, and the classic one is the capsule — a thick, slippery polysaccharide layer around the cell. A capsule works two ways: it is physically hard for a phagocyte to grip and engulf, and it hides the surface molecules that antibodies and complement would otherwise latch onto. The capsule is the master virulence factor of several of the most feared pathogens — Streptococcus pneumoniae, Neisseria meningitidis, Haemophilus influenzae type b, and Klebsiella pneumoniae. It is no accident that the vaccines against these organisms are aimed squarely at the capsule: teach the immune system to recognize that coat and the invisibility cloak stops working.

Antigenic variation: the costume change

Another strategy is to keep changing the disguise. In antigenic variation, a microbe alters the surface molecules the immune system has learned to recognize, so that yesterday's antibodies no longer fit. Influenza does this every year through small mutations (antigenic drift) and occasionally through big reassortments (antigenic shift) — which is why the vaccine is reformulated annually and why a truly novel shift can cause a pandemic. Neisseria gonorrhoeae constantly reshuffles the proteins on its pili, one reason gonorrhea produces no lasting immunity and can reinfect the same person repeatedly. The trypanosomes of African sleeping sickness and the Borrelia of relapsing fever run the same trick, producing waves of illness as each new variant outruns the antibody response.

Hiding and sabotage

Pathogens have still more moves. Some become intracellular, living inside host cells where circulating antibodies cannot reach them — Mycobacterium tuberculosis famously survives inside the very macrophages sent to destroy it, and Listeria and Salmonella do the same. Others secrete IgA proteases that chop up the antibodies guarding mucous membranes, or coagulase that walls the organism off inside a clot of the host's own fibrin (a Staphylococcus aureus specialty). Some coat themselves in host proteins to look like "self," a trick called molecular mimicry. Each of these explains a clinical reality: TB requires months of therapy because the bug is hidden inside cells; staph abscesses often need drainage because antibiotics cannot penetrate the walled-off pocket.

Evasion strategyHow it worksExample
CapsuleBlocks phagocytosis; hides surface antigensS. pneumoniae, N. meningitidis
Antigenic variationChanges surface molecules to dodge antibodiesInfluenza, N. gonorrhoeae
Intracellular hidingLives inside host cells, out of antibody reachM. tuberculosis, Listeria
IgA proteaseDestroys mucosal antibodiesN. gonorrhoeae, H. influenzae
CoagulaseWalls the microbe inside a fibrin clotStaphylococcus aureus
Why some infections need drainage, not just drugs. When S. aureus uses coagulase to seal itself inside an abscess, antibiotics can't get in at useful concentrations. "Source control" — draining the pocket, removing the infected line or hardware — is often what actually cures the patient, with antibiotics as backup. No drug beats a scalpel at an abscess.
Interactive Tap capsule, antigenic variation, or intracellular hiding, then toggle the immune attack against the microbe's escape Open full ↑
Key points
  • Capsules block phagocytosis and hide antigens; capsular vaccines target exactly this.
  • Antigenic variation changes surface molecules so antibodies no longer fit (flu, gonorrhea).
  • Intracellular hiding keeps microbes out of antibody reach and forces long therapy (TB).
  • Coagulase and abscess formation are why "source control" — drainage or device removal — is often essential.

Section 5The stages of an infection

Five predictable stages

Most acute infections unfold along the same timeline, and knowing the stages tells you what to expect and when a patient is most dangerous to others. The incubation period runs from the moment of exposure to the first symptoms; the microbe is multiplying but the patient feels fine, and this interval can be hours (food poisoning) or years (HIV, TB). Next comes the prodromal period, a short phase of vague, non-specific complaints — mild fever, fatigue, general achiness — as the microbial load and the immune response both build. The period of illness is the peak: the microbe is most numerous, the signs and symptoms specific to the disease are at their worst, and this is when the patient is sickest and, untreated, most at risk. Then the period of decline arrives as the immune system (helped by treatment) gains the upper hand and symptoms recede, though the patient is weakened and vulnerable to secondary infections. Finally the convalescence period is recovery, when the body repairs itself and strength returns.

Why the stages matter at the bedside

The practical payoff is understanding communicability — when a patient can spread the infection — because it does not line up neatly with how sick they look. For many illnesses, shedding begins in the prodromal stage or even late incubation, meaning a person is contagious before they feel truly ill and before anyone suspects infection. Measles and influenza spread most in exactly this window, which is why a patient can seed an outbreak in a waiting room while feeling only "a little off." Just as important, some patients keep shedding organisms into the decline and convalescence stages, and a few become long-term carriers who feel completely well yet remain a source — the reason isolation precautions are timed to the organism's shedding pattern, not merely to how bad the patient's symptoms are right now.

StageWhat is happeningSymptoms
IncubationMicrobe multiplying after exposureNone yet
ProdromalLoad and immune response buildingMild, vague (fatigue, low fever)
IllnessMicrobe at peak; disease fully expressedMost severe, disease-specific
DeclineImmune system gaining controlSubsiding; patient weakened
ConvalescenceRecovery and tissue repairResolving; strength returns
Contagious before they look sick. The most infectious moment for measles, flu, and many others falls in the prodromal stage — the patient feels only mildly unwell and hasn't been diagnosed. That gap is why standard precautions apply to everyone, not just patients with a known infection, and why prompt isolation the moment a communicable illness is suspected matters so much.
Interactive Tap each of the five stages on the infection curve to watch pathogen numbers rise and fall over time Open full ↑
Key points
  • Acute infections move through incubation → prodromal → illness → decline → convalescence.
  • Symptoms peak during the period of illness, when the microbe is most numerous.
  • Communicability often begins in the prodromal stage — patients spread infection before they seem sick.
  • Some patients shed during decline/convalescence or become long-term carriers, so isolation is timed to shedding, not just symptoms.

The chapter in one breath

  • Pathogenicity is whether a microbe causes disease; virulence is how much harm it does, measured by ID₅₀ and LD₅₀ (lower = more dangerous).
  • Virulence factors — molecules for attaching, spreading, damaging, and evading — are the concrete tools of disease and the targets of drugs and vaccines.
  • Microbes enter through portals of entry (respiratory, GI, GU, skin, parenteral) and anchor with adhesins; invasive devices are artificial portals nurses control.
  • Exotoxins are potent secreted proteins (A-B toxins, hemolysins, superantigens) that can be turned into protective toxoids.
  • Endotoxin is lipid A of LPS from gram-negative cells, released on lysis, driving fever, hypotension, septic shock, and DIC.
  • Pathogens evade defenses with capsules, antigenic variation, and intracellular hiding — explaining vaccines, annual flu shots, and the need for drainage or long therapy.
  • Infections pass through five stages, and patients are often most contagious in the prodromal phase, before they look sick.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Adhesin
A surface molecule (often on fimbriae or pili) that binds a pathogen to specific receptors on host cells so it is not washed away.
Antigenic variation
A pathogen's ability to change its surface molecules so previously formed antibodies no longer recognize it.
Biofilm
A matrix-encased community of adherent microbes that resists antibiotics and immune cells; common on catheters and implants.
Capsule
A thick polysaccharide layer around a cell that blocks phagocytosis and hides surface antigens.
Carrier
A person who harbors and can transmit a pathogen while showing no symptoms.
Convalescence period
The final stage of infection, during which the body recovers and repairs.
Endotoxin
The lipid A portion of LPS in the gram-negative outer membrane; released on cell lysis, causing fever, hypotension, and shock.
Exotoxin
A secreted protein toxin, often potent and specific; producible by gram-positive and gram-negative bacteria.
ID₅₀ (median infectious dose)
The number of microbes needed to infect 50% of an exposed population; a lower value means a more infectious organism.
Incubation period
The interval from exposure to the first appearance of symptoms.
LD₅₀ (median lethal dose)
The dose of microbe or toxin that kills 50% of a population; a lower value means a deadlier agent.
Lipopolysaccharide (LPS)
A molecule of the gram-negative outer membrane whose lipid A component is endotoxin.
Opportunistic pathogen
A microbe that causes disease only when defenses are breached, normal flora are lost, or the host is weakened.
Pathogenicity
The ability of a microbe to cause disease.
Portal of entry
The route by which a pathogen enters the body (respiratory, GI, GU, skin, or parenteral).
Primary pathogen
A microbe that can cause disease in a healthy host with intact defenses.
Prodromal period
An early stage of mild, non-specific symptoms as microbial load and immune response build.
Superantigen
An exotoxin that triggers a massive, non-specific cytokine release, producing shock.
Toxoid
A chemically inactivated exotoxin that no longer harms but still induces protective antibodies; used in vaccines.
Virulence
The degree of harm a pathogen is capable of causing.
Virulence factor
A specific molecule or structure that helps a microbe attach, spread, damage tissue, or evade defenses.

Check yourself

1. A microbe is described as "highly pathogenic but low in virulence." Explain what that means, and give the general definition of each term.
Pathogenicity is the ability to cause disease at all; virulence is the degree of harm. "Highly pathogenic, low virulence" means it reliably causes disease, but the disease it causes is mild — like a common-cold rhinovirus, a true pathogen that rarely does serious damage.
2. Two organisms are compared: one has an ID₅₀ of 20 cells, the other 100 million. Which spreads more easily person-to-person, and why does that matter for nursing precautions?
The one with the ID₅₀ of 20 (like Shigella) spreads far more easily, because it takes only a handful of organisms to infect. Low-dose organisms (also norovirus, C. difficile) demand strict hand hygiene and contact precautions — a single lapse can start an outbreak.
3. Distinguish a primary pathogen from an opportunistic pathogen, with an example of each.
A primary pathogen causes disease in a healthy host (e.g., influenza, M. tuberculosis); an opportunistic pathogen needs a breach, lost flora, or a weakened host (e.g., Candida, coagulase-negative staph on a line). Most healthcare-associated infections are opportunistic.
4. E. coli lives peacefully in the colon. Explain, using the concept of portals of entry, how it can cause both a UTI and fatal sepsis.
The same organism is harmless at its normal site but dangerous at a new portal: reaching the bladder it causes a UTI; reaching the bloodstream it can cause sepsis. Devices like Foleys and central lines are artificial portals of entry that deliver it there.
5. What are adhesins, and why is attachment a good target for vaccines and infection prevention?
Adhesins are surface molecules that bind pathogens to specific host-cell receptors so they aren't swept away. Attachment is a mandatory first step, so antibodies against adhesins (or physically removing microbes with hygiene) can stop infection before it starts — and clumped adherent microbes form drug-resistant biofilms.
6. Describe the structure and action of an A-B exotoxin.
The B (binding) subunit attaches to a host cell; the A (active) subunit then enters and disrupts a specific internal process. Examples: diphtheria toxin stops protein synthesis; cholera toxin drives massive fluid loss; botulinum and tetanus toxins block neurotransmitter release.
7. Compare exotoxin and endotoxin on four features: chemistry, source, heat stability, and whether a toxoid vaccine is possible.
Exotoxin: secreted protein, from gram-positive and gram-negative bacteria, usually heat-labile, can be made into a toxoid. Endotoxin: lipid A of LPS, gram-negative only, heat-stable, no toxoid. This is why tetanus/diphtheria have vaccines but there is no vaccine against endotoxin.
8. Why might a septic patient briefly worsen — fever, chills, dropping blood pressure — right after the first dose of an effective antibiotic?
The antibiotic lyses large numbers of gram-negative bacteria, releasing a surge of endotoxin that triggers fever, vasodilation, and hypotension. Anticipate it, monitor vitals closely, and don't mistake this reaction for treatment failure.
9. How does a bacterial capsule help a microbe evade the immune system, and how do we exploit this weakness?
The capsule is slippery and hard for phagocytes to grip, and it hides the surface antigens antibodies and complement would target. Vaccines against S. pneumoniae, N. meningitidis, and Hib teach the immune system to recognize the capsule, defeating the disguise.
10. Explain antigenic variation, and use it to explain why the influenza vaccine is reformulated every year.
Antigenic variation is a pathogen changing its surface molecules so old antibodies no longer fit. Influenza drifts through small mutations each year (and occasionally shifts dramatically), so last year's antibodies and vaccine no longer match — hence a new formulation annually.
11. A patient has a walled-off Staphylococcus aureus abscess. Why are antibiotics alone often not enough, and what is usually needed?
S. aureus coagulase walls the microbe inside a fibrin clot that antibiotics cannot penetrate at useful concentrations. "Source control" — incision and drainage, or removing infected hardware/lines — is usually what cures it, with antibiotics as backup.
12. List the five stages of an acute infection in order, and state during which stage patients are often most contagious — and why that is a problem.
Incubation → prodromal → illness → decline → convalescence. Many illnesses (measles, flu) are most contagious in the prodromal stage. That is a problem because the patient is shedding before they look or feel truly sick, which is why standard precautions apply to everyone and suspected cases are isolated promptly.
Microbiology · Chapter 11

Infection & Epidemiology

A single sick patient is a story about one person. A ward full of them is a story about a system — how a microbe found a home, slipped out of one body, traveled, and settled into the next. This chapter zooms out from the individual infection to the pattern: the six-link chain of infection that every outbreak follows, the reservoirs microbes hide in, the routes they travel, and the language epidemiologists use to count and track disease across a population. Then it zooms back in to the bedside, where the most dangerous infections a patient can catch are often the ones we hand them — and where breaking a single link in that chain is the most powerful thing a nurse does all shift.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Name the six links in the chain of infection and give a nursing action that breaks each one.
  • Distinguish the major types of reservoir — human, animal, and environmental — and explain what a carrier is.
  • Compare the five modes of transmission: contact, droplet, airborne, vehicle, and vector.
  • Define incidence and prevalence and explain why they answer different questions.
  • Distinguish endemic, epidemic (outbreak), and pandemic disease.
  • Identify the four major healthcare-associated infections (CAUTI, CLABSI, SSI, VAP) and the bundle that prevents each.
  • Explain standard precautions and the three categories of transmission-based precautions, and choose the right one for a given organism.

Section 1The chain of infection

Why think in a chain

Infection is never a single event. For a microbe to make a new person sick, a whole sequence of things has to line up: the microbe must exist, it must be living somewhere, it must get out of that somewhere, travel by some route, get into a new person, and find that person vulnerable enough to take hold. Epidemiologists picture this sequence as the chain of infection — six links that must all connect for transmission to succeed. The reason this model matters so much to nurses is its most hopeful feature: break any one link and the whole chain fails. You do not have to sterilize the world; you just have to interrupt the microbe at the weakest, most reachable point.

The six links

Read the chain in order and you can see where a nurse stands in it. The infectious agent is the microbe itself. Its reservoir is the place it normally lives and multiplies — a person, an animal, the soil, a sink drain. The portal of exit is how it leaves the reservoir (respiratory droplets, stool, blood, wound drainage). The mode of transmission is the route it travels (touch, droplets, air, a shared object, an insect). The portal of entry is how it gets into the next person — often the very same routes running in reverse, plus the artificial doorways we create with catheters, IV lines, and surgical incisions. Finally, the susceptible host is a person whose defenses are low enough to be colonized and infected. Notice how many links live on the nurse's side of the bed.

LinkWhat it isNursing action that breaks it
Infectious agentThe pathogen (bacterium, virus, fungus, parasite)Appropriate antimicrobials; treat & identify the organism
ReservoirWhere the microbe lives & multipliesClean the environment; treat carriers; remove standing water & soiled linen
Portal of exitHow it leaves the reservoirCover coughs; contain drainage & body fluids; dressings over wounds
Mode of transmissionThe route to a new hostHand hygiene; PPE; isolation; disinfect shared equipment
Portal of entryHow it enters the next personAseptic technique; catheter & line care; keep skin intact; remove devices early
Susceptible hostA person with lowered defensesVaccinate; nutrition; glycemic control; treat underlying illness
Hand hygiene attacks the easiest link. Of the six links, the mode of transmission is the one most under a nurse's direct control — and on a hospital ward, the dominant mode is a pair of hands moving from one patient to the next. That is why hand hygiene isn't one intervention among many; it is the single most reachable break point in the entire chain.

Interactive Tap each of the six links to explore it and cut the chain to see how nurses break transmission Open full ↑
Key points
  • Transmission requires six links to connect: agent → reservoir → portal of exit → mode of transmission → portal of entry → susceptible host.
  • Breaking any single link stops the infection — you don't have to break them all.
  • The mode of transmission link is the one nurses control most directly, and hand hygiene is its master key.
  • Devices (catheters, lines, tubes) create new portals of entry that don't exist in nature.

Section 2Reservoirs & transmission

Where microbes hide: reservoirs

A reservoir is the habitat where a pathogen normally lives, grows, and multiplies, waiting for a chance to spread. Reservoirs come in three kinds. Human reservoirs include people who are visibly sick, but also — and more dangerously — carriers, people who harbor and shed a microbe without symptoms. "Typhoid Mary" spread Salmonella Typhi to dozens of families while feeling perfectly well; today, an asymptomatic staff member colonized with MRSA can do something similar. Animal reservoirs give us zoonoses — diseases that jump from animals to humans, such as rabies, Lyme disease, and salmonellosis. Environmental reservoirs are non-living: soil harbors Clostridium tetani and fungal spores; water harbors Legionella, which famously breeds in cooling towers and warm hospital plumbing.

How microbes travel: the five modes

Once a microbe exits its reservoir it needs a route to the next host, and there are five to know. Contact is the most common: direct contact means skin-to-skin or body-fluid transfer (a handshake, a kiss, sex), while indirect contact runs through a contaminated object called a fomite — a bed rail, a stethoscope, a shared glucometer. Droplet transmission carries large respiratory droplets a short distance (classically within about 1–2 meters, roughly 3–6 feet) when someone coughs, sneezes, or talks; the droplets are too heavy to float and fall quickly. Airborne transmission is the dangerous cousin: tiny droplet nuclei stay suspended in the air for long periods and travel on air currents, so tuberculosis, measles, and varicella can infect someone who was never near the patient. Vehicle transmission uses a shared medium — food, water, blood, or IV fluids — to reach many people at once (a single contaminated batch causes an outbreak). Vector transmission uses a living carrier, usually an arthropod: mechanically (a fly tracking microbes on its feet) or biologically (a mosquito in which the pathogen actually multiplies, as with malaria).

ModeHow it spreadsClassic examplePrecaution
Contact (direct)Skin-to-skin or fluid-to-fluidMRSA, scabies, herpesGloves & gown; hand hygiene
Contact (indirect)Via a contaminated fomiteC. difficile, norovirus, RSVDisinfect shared equipment
DropletLarge droplets, short range (<2 m)Influenza, pertussis, meningococcusSurgical mask; ~2 m spacing
AirborneTiny nuclei suspended & driftingTuberculosis, measles, varicellaN95; negative-pressure room
VehicleShared food, water, blood, fluidsSalmonella, hepatitis A, HIVFood/water safety; blood screening
VectorLiving carrier (usually an arthropod)Malaria, Lyme, West NileRepellent; nets; vector control
Droplet and airborne are not the same — and the mask you grab depends on which. Droplets are heavy and fall within a couple of meters, so a surgical mask and a little distance protect you. Airborne nuclei float and drift, so only a fitted N95 respirator and a negative-pressure room will do. Confusing the two — wearing a surgical mask into a TB room — is a real and preventable exposure.

Interactive Tap the five transmission routes and watch how a pathogen crosses from reservoir to new host Open full ↑
Key points
  • A reservoir is where a microbe lives; reservoirs are human (including symptom-free carriers), animal (zoonoses), or environmental (soil, water).
  • The five modes are contact (direct/indirect via a fomite), droplet, airborne, vehicle, and vector.
  • Droplets fall within ~2 m (mask); airborne nuclei drift (N95 + negative-pressure room).
  • Vehicle transmission can infect many people from one contaminated source — the signature of a point-source outbreak.

Section 3Epidemiology basics

Counting disease: incidence and prevalence

Epidemiology is the study of how disease is distributed in populations and what drives that distribution — the science behind every outbreak investigation and every public-health decision. Its two most fundamental numbers are easy to mix up. Incidence is the number of new cases that appear in a population during a set period of time; it measures how fast a disease is arriving. Prevalence is the total number of cases — new and old — present at a given moment; it measures how much disease is out there right now. A brief, intense illness like influenza has high incidence during flu season but low prevalence at any snapshot, because people recover quickly. A chronic condition like HIV has low incidence but high and climbing prevalence, because cases accumulate and persist. Two more terms round out the vocabulary: morbidity is the amount of illness in a population, and mortality is the amount of death.

Patterns over place and time: endemic, epidemic, pandemic

Epidemiologists also describe the pattern a disease shows. A disease is endemic when it is constantly present in a region at a fairly steady, expected level — the common cold is endemic everywhere; malaria is endemic in parts of the tropics. An epidemic (often called an outbreak when it is smaller or more local) is a rise in cases above the expected baseline for that place and time. When an epidemic crosses countries and continents to affect a large part of the world, it is a pandemic — COVID-19 being the defining example of this generation. A disease that appears only occasionally and irregularly, with no steady pattern, is called sporadic. These are not just labels: the moment case counts climb above the endemic baseline, public-health systems switch from routine surveillance into outbreak response.

TermMeaningExample
IncidenceNew cases in a period1,200 new flu cases this week
PrevalenceAll current cases at a moment38 million people living with HIV
SporadicOccasional, irregular casesTetanus in a vaccinated country
EndemicSteady, expected background levelMalaria in tropical regions
Epidemic / outbreakCases above the expected baselineA measles outbreak in a school
PandemicEpidemic spanning countries/continentsCOVID-19, 1918 influenza
John Snow removed a pump handle before anyone had seen the germ. In 1854 London, Snow mapped cholera deaths, traced them to one contaminated water pump, and had its handle removed — ending the outbreak decades before Vibrio cholerae was ever identified. The lesson still holds: you can break the chain by finding the pattern, even before you know the microbe.

Interactive Tap sporadic, endemic, epidemic, or pandemic to watch the case curve rise above the baseline and spread across the map Open full ↑
Key points
  • Incidence counts new cases over time; prevalence counts all existing cases at a moment.
  • Short, curable illnesses have high incidence but low prevalence; chronic ones have low incidence but high prevalence.
  • Endemic = steady baseline; epidemic/outbreak = above baseline; pandemic = epidemic across many countries.
  • Morbidity is illness; mortality is death — both are core public-health measures.

Section 4Healthcare-associated infections

The infections we hand to patients

A healthcare-associated infection (HAI) is an infection a patient acquires while receiving care — one they did not have, and were not incubating, on admission. HAIs are common: on any given day, roughly 1 in 31 hospitalized patients in the United States has at least one. They are also, for the most part, preventable, which is exactly why they are tracked, reported, and taken so seriously. Most of the big HAIs share a villain: the biofilm. When we thread a catheter into a bladder or a line into a vein, microbes colonize the plastic and build a slimy, drug-resistant biofilm along its surface — a fortress antibiotics and immune cells struggle to breach. Every indwelling device is both a portal of entry and a biofilm scaffold, which is why the guiding rule is blunt: if the device isn't needed, it comes out.

The big four — and the bundles that stop them

Four HAIs dominate. CAUTI (catheter-associated urinary tract infection) follows the indwelling urinary catheter — the single most-used, and most over-used, device in the hospital. CLABSI (central-line–associated bloodstream infection) is less common but far deadlier, because it delivers microbes straight into the bloodstream. SSI (surgical-site infection) develops in a wound after an operation. VAP (ventilator-associated pneumonia) strikes patients whose breathing tube bypasses the natural filters of the upper airway. For each, prevention isn't one trick but a bundle — a small set of evidence-based steps done together, every time, with nothing skipped. Bundles work because HAIs are chains too, and a bundle breaks several links at once.

HAIDevice / causePrevention bundle (nursing core)
CAUTIIndwelling urinary catheterInsert only if truly needed; sterile insertion; keep bag below bladder; remove ASAP
CLABSICentral venous lineFull-barrier insertion; chlorhexidine skin prep; scrub the hub; daily line-necessity review
SSISurgical incisionTimely pre-op antibiotics; clippers (not razors); glucose & normothermia; sterile dressing care
VAPEndotracheal tube / ventilatorHead of bed 30–45°; daily sedation pause & readiness-to-extubate; oral care with chlorhexidine
The best line is the one that's already out. A urinary catheter or central line left in "just in case" is a biofilm growing a little larger every hour. Study after study shows the most effective CAUTI and CLABSI prevention is simply asking, on every round, "Does this patient still need this device today?" — and pulling it the moment the answer is no.

Interactive Tap each device infection (CAUTI, CLABSI, SSI, VAP), then complete its prevention bundle to block the microbe's route in Open full ↑
Key points
  • An HAI is acquired during care; about 1 in 31 inpatients has one on any given day, and most are preventable.
  • The big four are CAUTI, CLABSI, SSI, and VAP — three of the four are device-associated.
  • Biofilms on indwelling devices shelter microbes from drugs and immune defenses.
  • Bundles — and early device removal — are the proven way to drive HAI rates down.

Section 5Breaking the chain

Standard precautions: the floor for everyone

Infection control rests on a two-tier system. The base tier is standard precautions, and its founding assumption is deliberately paranoid: treat the blood and body fluids of every patient as potentially infectious, regardless of diagnosis, because you cannot tell by looking who is carrying what. Standard precautions apply to all patients, all the time, and include hand hygiene before and after every contact, PPE (gloves, gown, mask, or eye protection) whenever you might contact body fluids, respiratory hygiene / cough etiquette, safe injection practices (one needle, one syringe, one time), and safe handling of soiled equipment and linens. This tier alone, done faithfully, breaks the transmission link for the majority of hospital microbes.

Transmission-based precautions: adding a tier for known threats

When a patient is known or suspected to carry a highly transmissible organism, you add a second tier layered on top of standard precautions. There are three categories, matched to the three high-risk modes of transmission. Contact precautions (gown and gloves; dedicated equipment) counter organisms spread by touch and fomites — MRSA, VRE, C. difficile, RSV. Droplet precautions (surgical mask within ~2 m) counter influenza, pertussis, and meningococcal disease. Airborne precautions (an N95 respirator and a negative-pressure room with the door closed) counter the drifting-nuclei diseases — tuberculosis, measles, varicella. A useful memory hook for the airborne set is "My Chicken Hez TB": Measles, Chickenpox (varicella), Herpes zoster (disseminated), TB. Choosing the wrong tier — or none — leaves the chain intact.

PrecautionPPE / roomExample organisms
Standard (all patients)Hand hygiene; PPE as risk dictatesAssume all blood/body fluids infectious
ContactGown + gloves; dedicated equipmentMRSA, VRE, C. difficile, RSV, scabies
DropletSurgical mask within ~2 m; private roomInfluenza, pertussis, N. meningitidis
AirborneN95 + negative-pressure room, door closedTuberculosis, measles, varicella
C. difficile needs soap and water, not gel. Alcohol-based hand rub is the default for hand hygiene — fast and effective against most microbes — but it does not kill C. difficile spores. For a patient on contact precautions for C. diff, you must wash with soap and water to physically scrub the spores away, and clean the room with a sporicidal (bleach) agent. Knowing the exception is the difference between breaking the chain and spreading it.

Interactive Pick a precaution level, then don the correct PPE on the nurse and get live feedback Open full ↑
Key points
  • Standard precautions apply to every patient and treat all body fluids as infectious — hand hygiene is their core.
  • Transmission-based precautions add a tier: Contact (gown/gloves), Droplet (mask), Airborne (N95 + negative-pressure room).
  • Match the precaution to the mode: airborne threats need an N95, not a surgical mask.
  • Spore formers like C. difficile require soap-and-water handwashing and bleach cleaning — alcohol gel won't do.

The chapter in one breath

  • The chain of infection has six links — agent, reservoir, portal of exit, transmission, portal of entry, susceptible host — and breaking any one stops transmission.
  • Reservoirs are human (including symptom-free carriers), animal (zoonoses), or environmental (soil, water).
  • The five modes of transmission are contact (direct/indirect via a fomite), droplet, airborne, vehicle, and vector; droplet and airborne need different masks.
  • Incidence counts new cases; prevalence counts all current cases — they answer different questions.
  • Disease patterns run endemic (steady) → epidemic/outbreak (above baseline) → pandemic (across continents).
  • HAIs — CAUTI, CLABSI, SSI, VAP — affect ~1 in 31 inpatients, ride on biofilm-coated devices, and fall with bundles and early device removal.
  • Standard precautions protect against every patient; transmission-based precautions (Contact, Droplet, Airborne) add protection against known threats.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Check yourself

1. List the six links of the chain of infection in order, and explain why the model is encouraging for infection control.
Infectious agent → reservoir → portal of exit → mode of transmission → portal of entry → susceptible host. It is encouraging because breaking any single link — not all of them — stops transmission, and several links (especially mode of transmission) are directly under a nurse's control.
2. Which link in the chain is most directly under a bedside nurse's control, and what single action targets it best?
The mode of transmission — and hand hygiene is the master action. On a ward, the dominant route is hands moving between patients, so cleaning them before and after each contact severs the chain more often than any other intervention.
3. What is a carrier, and why is a carrier often more dangerous to a population than a visibly sick person?
A carrier harbors and sheds a pathogen without symptoms. Because they look and feel well, no one isolates them or takes precautions, so they can keep spreading the microbe unchecked — the "Typhoid Mary" problem.
4. Name the three types of reservoir and give an example of each.
Human (a symptomatic patient or a carrier), animal (zoonoses like rabies or Lyme), and environmental (soil harboring C. tetani; water harboring Legionella). Knowing the reservoir tells you where to aim source control.
5. A nurse enters a tuberculosis patient's room wearing only a surgical mask. What is wrong, and what is required instead?
TB is airborne, so a surgical mask is inadequate — it requires a fitted N95 respirator and a negative-pressure room with the door closed. Droplet diseases fall within ~2 m (mask is fine); airborne nuclei drift, so only an N95 protects.
6. Distinguish a fomite from a vector, and give one example of each.
A fomite is an inanimate contaminated object (a bed rail, stethoscope); a vector is a living carrier, usually an arthropod (a mosquito, a tick). Fomites drive indirect-contact spread; vectors drive vector-borne disease.
7. Define incidence and prevalence, and explain why influenza has high incidence but low prevalence while HIV shows the reverse.
Incidence is new cases over a period; prevalence is all existing cases at a moment. Flu arrives fast (high incidence) but resolves quickly, so few cases exist at any snapshot (low prevalence). HIV arises slowly (low incidence) but persists for life, so cases accumulate (high prevalence).
8. Arrange these in order of increasing spread and define each: endemic, pandemic, epidemic.
Endemic (steady, expected background level) → epidemic/outbreak (cases above the expected baseline) → pandemic (an epidemic spanning countries and continents). The jump from endemic to epidemic is the trigger for outbreak response.
9. Name the four major healthcare-associated infections and note which one is not device-associated.
CAUTI, CLABSI, VAP, and SSI — the surgical-site infection (SSI) is the one not tied to an indwelling device. The other three ride on a urinary catheter, a central line, and a ventilator/endotracheal tube respectively.
10. What is a biofilm, and why does it make device-associated infections so hard to treat?
A biofilm is a slime-encased community of microbes anchored to a surface such as a catheter or line. The matrix shields the microbes from antibiotics and immune cells, so the infection resists treatment — which is why removing the device is often the real cure.
11. A patient has a urinary catheter that is no longer clinically needed. What is the single most effective CAUTI-prevention step, and why?
Remove the catheter. Every hour an unnecessary catheter stays in, its biofilm grows and the portal of entry remains open; daily review of device necessity and prompt removal is the best-proven way to prevent CAUTI.
12. A patient is on contact precautions for C. difficile. Why is alcohol hand rub not enough, and what must you use instead?
Alcohol gel does not kill C. difficile spores — you must wash with soap and water to physically remove them, and clean the room with a bleach (sporicidal) agent. Knowing this spore-former exception prevents you from unintentionally carrying spores to the next patient.
Microbiology · Chapter 12

Innate Immunity

Long before your body knows the name of an invader, it is already fighting back. Innate immunity is the defense you were born with — a set of barriers, cells, and chemical alarms that respond within seconds to minutes, the same way every time, to almost anything that doesn't belong. It doesn't need to have met the microbe before, and it doesn't remember it afterward. From the skin that keeps microbes out, to the neutrophils that swarm a wound, to the fever that makes your patient miserable at 3 a.m., innate immunity is the fast, blunt, always-on first responder of the immune system. This chapter walks through its layers in the order the body meets an invader — and shows you why so much of nursing is really the art of protecting, and reading, these defenses.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Contrast innate and adaptive immunity by speed, specificity, and memory.
  • Describe the first-line defenses — skin, mucous membranes, and normal flora — and how each keeps microbes out.
  • Identify the major phagocytic cells and walk through the steps of phagocytosis.
  • Explain the inflammatory response and relate the four cardinal signs to what is happening in the tissue.
  • Describe how pyrogens reset the hypothalamic thermostat to produce a fever, and weigh its benefits and dangers.
  • Summarize the outcomes of the complement cascade — opsonization, inflammation, and cytolysis.
  • Explain how interferons protect cells from viral infection.
  • Connect each of these defenses to concrete nursing assessments at the bedside.

Section 1First line: barriers

Two immune systems, one goal

Your body defends itself with two overlapping systems. Innate immunity is present from birth, responds within seconds to minutes, and treats all invaders with the same non-specific weapons — it has no memory, so the tenth exposure gets the same response as the first. Adaptive immunity (the subject of the next chapter) is slower to start, exquisitely specific to one particular microbe, and it remembers, which is why a second exposure is met faster and harder. Innate immunity buys the days that adaptive immunity needs to gear up. In practice they work as one team, but learning them separately makes each far easier to understand.

Innate immunity is often taught as three lines of defense. The first line is the set of physical, chemical, and biological barriers that stop microbes from ever entering — the focus of this section. The second line is the internal response that kicks in once something breaches a barrier: phagocytes, inflammation, fever, and antimicrobial proteins (Sections 2–5). Adaptive immunity is the third line.

Skin and mucous membranes

The skin is the single largest organ of the body and the most important physical barrier there is. Its outer layer, the epidermis, is built of tightly packed cells stuffed with keratin and stacked into dead, shingled sheets that are constantly shed — taking any attached microbes with them. Most bacteria simply cannot penetrate intact skin. This is exactly why every break in the skin matters clinically: an IV site, a surgical incision, a pressure injury, or a simple scratch is a door in the wall, and the nurse's job of keeping that door clean and dressed is frontline infection control.

Where the body opens to the outside — the respiratory, digestive, urinary, and reproductive tracts — the barrier is not dry skin but a mucous membrane. These surfaces are kept moist and coated with mucus, a sticky gel that traps microbes before they can reach the cells beneath. In the airway, cells carrying tiny hair-like cilia beat in waves to sweep the trapped debris up and out of the lungs — the so-called mucociliary escalator. Tears, saliva, and the flushing action of urine physically wash microbes away. Understanding this tells you why an intubated patient, whose tube bypasses the mucociliary escalator, is at such high risk for pneumonia, and why we encourage coughing, deep breathing, and adequate hydration.

Chemical and microbial reinforcements

Barriers are not just walls; they are hostile chemical environments. The skin secretes sebum (oily, acidic) and sweat, keeping the surface at a low, unfriendly pH. Lysozyme, an enzyme found in tears, saliva, and mucus, chews apart bacterial cell walls. Stomach acid is brutally low in pH, and the vagina is kept acidic by resident lactobacilli. On top of all this sits the normal flora — the harmless microbes that already occupy the skin and mucosal surfaces. By taking up space and consuming nutrients, they crowd out would-be pathogens, a protection called competitive exclusion. Wipe out that flora — with broad-spectrum antibiotics, for instance — and opportunists like Candida or Clostridioides difficile move into the vacancy.

First-line defenseTypeHow it stops microbes
Skin (epidermis)PhysicalKeratinized, dry, shedding cell layers block entry
Mucous membranesPhysicalTrap microbes in mucus; cilia and flushing remove them
Sebum & sweatChemicalLow pH and fatty acids inhibit growth
LysozymeChemicalEnzyme that digests bacterial cell walls
Stomach acid / vaginal acidityChemicalLow pH kills or inhibits many microbes
Normal floraBiologicalCompetitive exclusion — crowd out pathogens
Every skin break is a breach in the wall. When you insert an IV, place a catheter, or find a stage-2 pressure injury, you are looking at a defeated first-line barrier. Aseptic technique, clean dressings, and removing devices as soon as they are no longer needed are how nurses substitute for the skin the patient no longer has intact.
Interactive Tap body sites to explore the skin, mucous membrane, secretion, and normal-flora barriers Open full ↑
Key points
  • Innate immunity is fast, non-specific, and has no memory; adaptive is slow, specific, and remembers.
  • The first line is physical, chemical, and microbial barriers that keep invaders out.
  • Skin and mucous membranes are the physical walls; lysozyme, acid, and sebum are the chemical defenses.
  • Normal flora protect by competitive exclusion; any break in the skin is a breach that nursing care must cover.

Section 2Second line: cells

The white blood cell responders

Once a microbe slips past the barriers, it meets the cellular second line — the white blood cells, or leukocytes. These are made in the bone marrow and patrol the blood and tissues. Two of them do most of the eating, and they are the cells you must know. Neutrophils are the most numerous white cell and the first to arrive at an infection — fast, aggressive, short-lived front-line soldiers. Macrophages (from circulating monocytes that mature after leaving the blood) are the larger, longer-lived cleanup crew; they patrol the tissues, engulf microbes and debris, and help kick off other immune responses. A microbe-eating cell of either kind is called a phagocyte, and the act of eating is phagocytosis (Greek for "cell eating").

These cells don't reach a wound by accident. Chemical signals released at the site of infection — from damaged cells, from the microbes themselves, and from complement (Section 5) — draw phagocytes toward the trouble, a directed migration called chemotaxis. To get out of the bloodstream, circulating white cells first stick to the vessel wall (margination) and then squeeze between the cells of the vessel lining into the tissue, a maneuver called diapedesis (or extravasation).

How a phagocyte recognizes an enemy

How does a phagocyte tell "self" from "invader"? It carries pattern-recognition receptors (PRRs) — sensors that latch onto molecular signatures shared by whole classes of microbes but absent from human cells, called pathogen-associated molecular patterns (PAMPs). A classic PAMP is the lipopolysaccharide (LPS) in the outer membrane of Gram-negative bacteria. Because these patterns are broad, one receptor recognizes many microbes — the hallmark of innate, non-specific immunity. Recognition is made even easier when a microbe is coated with tags called opsonins (antibodies or the complement protein C3b); this coating, opsonization, is like painting a target "eat me" and dramatically speeds engulfment.

Phagocytosis, step by step

The killing sequence is worth memorizing because it recurs throughout immunology. (1) Chemotaxis — the phagocyte migrates toward the microbe. (2) Adherence — it binds the microbe, helped by opsonins. (3) Ingestion — the cell membrane wraps around the microbe and pinches off an internal bubble called a phagosome. (4) Fusion — the phagosome merges with a lysosome (full of digestive enzymes) to form a phagolysosome. (5) Killing and digestion — enzymes, reactive oxygen (the "respiratory burst"), and low pH destroy the microbe. (6) Exocytosis — the indigestible remains are dumped back out. The spent neutrophils, dead microbes, and tissue fluid left behind are what we recognize as pus.

CellComes fromRole
NeutrophilBone marrow (granulocyte)First responder; most abundant WBC; fast, short-lived phagocyte
Monocyte → macrophageBone marrow; matures in tissueLarge, long-lived phagocyte; cleanup and immune signaling
Dendritic cellBone marrowPhagocyte that alerts adaptive immunity
Natural killer (NK) cellLymphoid lineKills virus-infected and tumor cells (not classic phagocytosis)
EosinophilBone marrow (granulocyte)Attacks parasites; role in allergy
Read the differential before you read the fever. A rising neutrophil count (neutrophilia), often with immature "bands," points toward a bacterial infection — the marrow is shipping out first responders. A very low neutrophil count (neutropenia), common in chemotherapy patients, strips away the front-line phagocytes: such a patient can be gravely infected yet show little pus, redness, or swelling, because there are too few neutrophils left to produce those local signs. A new fever may be the only clue — which is why fever in a neutropenic patient is a medical emergency, and why neutropenic precautions exist.
Interactive Step through how a phagocyte hunts, engulfs, and destroys a microbe Open full ↑
Key points
  • Neutrophils are the first, most numerous phagocytes; macrophages (from monocytes) are the long-lived cleanup phagocytes.
  • Phagocytes reach infection by chemotaxis, margination, and diapedesis.
  • They recognize microbes by PRRs binding PAMPs, and engulf opsonized microbes fastest.
  • Phagocytosis: chemotaxis → adherence → ingestion (phagosome) → fusion (phagolysosome) → digestion → exocytosis; leftover debris is pus.

Section 3Inflammation

The four cardinal signs

Inflammation is the tissue's coordinated reaction to injury or infection, and it produces four classic signs that have been recognized since antiquity: redness, heat, swelling, and pain (in Latin, rubor, calor, tumor, dolor). A fifth is often added: loss of function (functio laesa). Far from being the enemy, these signs are the visible proof that the second line has arrived and is working. When you assess a wound, an IV site, or a joint, you are reading the four cardinal signs directly — they are the everyday language of infection assessment.

What is actually happening in the tissue

Each sign has a mechanism. When cells are injured or microbes are detected, resident mast cells and damaged tissue release chemical mediators — above all histamine, along with prostaglandins, bradykinin, and cytokines. These do two things. First, they cause vasodilation, widening the blood vessels so more blood floods the area — that extra warm blood is the redness and the heat. Second, they increase vascular permeability, making the vessel walls leaky so fluid, proteins, and cells pour out into the tissue — that accumulation of fluid (edema) is the swelling. The pressure of that swelling on nerve endings, along with mediators like prostaglandins and bradykinin that sensitize those nerves, produces the pain. The whole point is to deliver phagocytes and clotting proteins to the site and to wall the infection off with a mesh of fibrin so it cannot spread.

Cardinal signLatinWhat causes it
RednessruborVasodilation — more blood flowing in
HeatcalorWarmth of that increased blood flow
SwellingtumorFluid leaking from leaky vessels (edema)
PaindolorPressure on nerves plus pain-sensitizing mediators
Loss of functionfunctio laesaSwelling and pain limit use of the part

Acute, chronic, and when inflammation turns dangerous

Acute inflammation is short-lived and resolves as the threat is cleared. When a threat can't be cleared, inflammation becomes chronic: macrophages persist and may wall off the invader in a ball of immune cells called a granuloma (classically in tuberculosis). Localized inflammation is protective — but when the same mediators spill into the bloodstream system-wide, the result can be catastrophic. In sepsis, a body-wide inflammatory response to infection causes widespread vasodilation and leaky vessels, dropping the blood pressure and starving organs of oxygen. The very tools that heal a local wound, unleashed everywhere at once, can kill.

The signs you are trained to fear are the immune system working. Warmth, redness, and swelling at a wound mean inflammation is active — useful locally, but a red flag when it spreads. Learn to escalate the pattern: a hot, swollen, red, painful limb with fever and a climbing heart rate may be moving from local inflammation toward systemic infection. Early recognition of sepsis is one of the highest-value skills a nurse has.
Interactive Step through inflammation and watch redness, heat, swelling, and pain appear Open full ↑
Key points
  • The four cardinal signs are redness, heat, swelling, and pain (plus loss of function).
  • Mast cells and injured tissue release histamine and other mediators that cause vasodilation and increased vascular permeability.
  • Inflammation delivers phagocytes and walls off infection with fibrin.
  • Localized inflammation heals; systemic inflammation (sepsis) is life-threatening.

Section 4Fever

Resetting the thermostat

Fever (pyrexia) is an abnormally high core body temperature, and it is not the infection getting the upper hand — it is a deliberate, regulated defense. Your temperature is controlled by a "thermostat" in the hypothalamus, which normally holds the body near 37°C (98.6°F). Fever happens when substances called pyrogens raise the hypothalamic set-point. There are two kinds. Exogenous pyrogens come from outside the body — most famously the LPS of Gram-negative bacteria (also called endotoxin). Endogenous pyrogens are made by the patient's own immune cells: the cytokines interleukin-1 (IL-1), IL-6, and tumor necrosis factor (TNF), released by macrophages when they meet a microbe. These pyrogens drive the production of prostaglandin E₂ in the hypothalamus, which is what actually pushes the set-point up.

Here is the key idea: once the set-point is raised, the body isn't "overheating" — it now believes 39°C is the correct temperature and works to reach it. That is why the onset of a fever brings shivering and a feeling of cold: muscles contract to generate heat and blood vessels constrict to conserve it, all to climb toward the new, higher target.

The three phases of a fever

A fever runs a predictable course, and recognizing the phase changes what you do. In the onset (chill phase), the set-point jumps; the patient feels cold, shivers, and has cool, pale skin as the body drives its temperature up. In the plateau (stadium), the body has reached the new set-point — the patient feels warm, flushed, and dry, and the temperature holds steady at its elevated level. In defervescence (the flush or crisis), pyrogens fall and the set-point drops back to normal; now the body is "too hot," so it sweats and flushes to shed the excess heat. Reading these phases is practical: a shivering patient is climbing a fever, and piling on blankets to stop the shivering only drives the temperature higher.

PhaseSet-pointWhat you seeNursing note
Onset (chill)RisingShivering, cold, pale, "goosebumps"Temp is climbing; keep comfortable, don't overload blankets
Plateau (stadium)Reached (high)Warm, flushed, dry skinWatch hydration; temp is holding high
Defervescence (flush)FallingSweating, flushed, feels hotReplace fluids lost in sweat

Friend or foe?

A moderate fever helps: higher temperatures slow the growth of many pathogens, speed up the enzymes of the immune response, and enhance the activity of phagocytes and other defenders. That is why routinely erasing every low fever with antipyretics is not always wise — the fever is doing work. But fever also has costs and limits. It raises metabolic demand, the heart rate, and fluid loss; in young children a rapid rise can trigger a febrile seizure; and a very high temperature — roughly above 40–41°C (104–106°F) — begins to denature the body's own proteins and becomes a medical emergency. Antipyretics such as acetaminophen and NSAIDs work by blocking prostaglandin production, lowering the set-point back down.

In some patients, the absence of fever is the alarm. Very old, very young, and immunocompromised patients — and those on steroids — may mount little or no fever even with serious infection. Never let a normal temperature falsely reassure you if the rest of the picture (confusion, low blood pressure, rising heart rate, low urine output) says sepsis. And treat a fever above 40°C, or any fever with altered mental status, as urgent.
Interactive Drag through one fever episode and watch a pyrogen raise the hypothalamic set point while body temperature chases it with chills, then breaks in a sweat Open full ↑
Key points
  • Fever is a regulated rise in the hypothalamic set-point, not loss of control.
  • Exogenous pyrogens (e.g., LPS) and endogenous pyrogens (cytokines IL-1, IL-6, TNF) act via prostaglandin E₂.
  • Phases: onset (chills, climbing) → plateau (steady, high) → defervescence (sweating, falling).
  • Moderate fever aids defense; very high fever (>40°C) is dangerous, and some patients can't mount one at all.

Section 5Complement & interferons

Complement: a cascade of ready-made proteins

Complement is a set of about 30 blood proteins, made mostly by the liver, that circulate in an inactive form and wait. When triggered, they activate one another in sequence — a cascade, where each activated protein cuts and activates the next, amplifying the signal at every step. Three pathways can set it off: the classical pathway (triggered by antibodies bound to a microbe), the alternative pathway (triggered directly by microbial surfaces), and the lectin pathway (triggered by carbohydrates on microbes). All three converge on the central protein, C3, which is split into C3a and C3b — the hinge of the whole system.

From there, complement produces three life-saving outcomes. Opsonization: C3b coats the microbe as an opsonin, tagging it so phagocytes grab it far more easily (linking this section straight back to phagocytosis). Inflammation: the fragments C3a and C5a are anaphylatoxins and chemotactic factors — they trigger mast cells to release histamine and they call phagocytes to the scene. Cytolysis: the terminal proteins assemble into a ring called the membrane attack complex (MAC), which punches a hole in the microbe's membrane so its contents leak out and it bursts. Together, these are sometimes remembered as the three jobs of complement: tag, recruit, and destroy.

Complement outcomeKey playersWhat it does
OpsonizationC3bCoats the microbe so phagocytes engulf it faster
InflammationC3a, C5aAnaphylatoxins — trigger histamine release and attract phagocytes
CytolysisMAC (C5b–C9)Forms pores that lyse the microbe

Interferons: the antiviral alarm

Barriers, phagocytes, and complement are formidable against bacteria — but a virus hides inside a host cell, where those defenses can't reach. For viruses, innate immunity has a different tool: interferons (IFNs), a family of cytokines named because they "interfere" with viral replication. When a cell is infected by a virus, it releases type I interferons (IFN-α and IFN-β). These don't save the dying cell that made them; instead they act as a warning to neighboring cells. A neighbor that receives the interferon signal switches on an antiviral state — it produces enzymes that degrade viral genetic material and shut down protein synthesis if a virus enters — so the virus finds no factory to hijack. A separate interferon, IFN-γ, mainly activates macrophages and bridges to adaptive immunity.

This matters clinically in two directions. The malaise, muscle aches, and fever of a viral illness like influenza are largely the effects of your own interferons working, not the virus directly. And because interferons are natural antivirals and immune modulators, purified versions are used as medications — for certain viral hepatitis infections, some cancers, and multiple sclerosis.

Complement deficiency has a signature. Patients who can't build a functioning MAC (terminal complement deficiency) are strikingly prone to Neisseria infections — recurrent meningococcal disease in particular. It's a neat illustration of how one missing innate protein leaves a specific hole in the defenses, and why complement isn't just textbook trivia.
Interactive Tap complement's three outcomes — lysis, opsonization, and inflammation — to see how each one attacks a microbe Open full ↑
Key points
  • Complement is a cascade of ~30 proteins; three pathways (classical, alternative, lectin) converge on C3.
  • Its three outcomes are opsonization (C3b), inflammation (C3a/C5a), and cytolysis (the MAC).
  • Interferons are antiviral cytokines: a virus-infected cell releases type I IFN to put neighboring cells into an antiviral state.
  • Interferons cause much of the "sick" feeling of viral illness and are used as drugs.

The chapter in one breath

  • Innate immunity is the fast, non-specific, memory-free defense you are born with; it buys time for adaptive immunity.
  • The first line keeps microbes out: skin and mucous membranes, chemical defenses (lysozyme, acid, sebum), and normal flora.
  • When barriers fail, phagocytes — neutrophils first, then macrophages — find microbes by chemotaxis and destroy them by phagocytosis.
  • Inflammation (redness, heat, swelling, pain) comes from histamine-driven vasodilation and leaky vessels; systemic, it becomes sepsis.
  • Fever is a raised hypothalamic set-point driven by pyrogens (LPS; cytokines IL-1/IL-6/TNF) via prostaglandin E₂ — helpful in moderation, dangerous when very high.
  • Complement tags (opsonization), recruits (inflammation), and destroys (MAC); interferons warn neighboring cells to resist viruses.
