  • Nearly every innate defense maps onto a bedside assessment — skin integrity, the WBC differential, wound signs, temperature phase, and sepsis recognition.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Antipyretic
A drug (e.g., acetaminophen, an NSAID) that lowers fever by blocking prostaglandin production and resetting the hypothalamic set-point down.
Chemotaxis
The directed movement of a cell, such as a phagocyte, toward a chemical signal released at a site of infection.
Complement
A cascade of about 30 blood proteins that, when activated, cause opsonization, inflammation, and cell lysis.
Cytokine
A signaling protein released by cells to communicate; includes interleukins, interferons, and TNF.
Diapedesis
The squeezing of white blood cells between the cells of a vessel wall to enter infected tissue; also called extravasation.
Fever (pyrexia)
A regulated rise in core body temperature caused by pyrogens raising the hypothalamic set-point.
Inflammation
The tissue response to injury or infection, marked by redness, heat, swelling, and pain.
Innate immunity
The non-specific defenses present from birth that respond rapidly and without memory.
Interferon (IFN)
An antiviral cytokine; a virus-infected cell releases type I IFN to put neighboring cells into an antiviral state.
Lysozyme
An enzyme in tears, saliva, and mucus that digests bacterial cell walls.
Macrophage
A large, long-lived phagocyte derived from a monocyte; cleans up microbes and debris and signals other immune cells.
Membrane attack complex (MAC)
The ring of terminal complement proteins (C5b–C9) that forms pores and lyses a target cell.
Neutrophil
The most abundant white blood cell and the first phagocyte to arrive at an infection.
Opsonization
The coating of a microbe with tags (antibody or C3b) that make phagocytosis faster and easier.
PAMP
Pathogen-associated molecular pattern — a molecular signature (e.g., LPS) shared by many microbes and recognized by innate receptors.
Pattern-recognition receptor (PRR)
A receptor on innate immune cells that binds PAMPs to detect microbes.
Phagocytosis
The engulfment and destruction of a microbe by a phagocyte inside a phagolysosome.
Pyrogen
A fever-inducing substance; exogenous (e.g., LPS) or endogenous (cytokines such as IL-1, IL-6, TNF).
Sepsis
A dysregulated, body-wide inflammatory response to infection that can cause dangerously low blood pressure and organ failure.
Vasodilation
Widening of blood vessels; in inflammation it increases blood flow, causing redness and heat.

Check yourself

1. In three words — speed, specificity, and memory — how does innate immunity differ from adaptive immunity?
Innate is fast, non-specific, and has no memory; adaptive is slower, highly specific, and remembers. Innate immunity responds the same way to almost any invader within minutes, buying the days that adaptive immunity needs to gear up a targeted response.
2. Name the three types of first-line barrier and give one example of each.
Physical (skin, mucous membranes), chemical (lysozyme, stomach acid, sebum), and biological (normal flora). All three keep microbes from ever entering; any break in the skin defeats the physical barrier.
3. What is the mucociliary escalator, and why is an intubated patient at higher risk of pneumonia?
The mucociliary escalator is the coordinated beating of airway cilia that sweeps mucus-trapped microbes up and out of the lungs. An endotracheal tube bypasses this defense, so trapped microbes are not cleared — raising the risk of ventilator-associated pneumonia.
4. Which phagocyte arrives first at an infection and is the most abundant white blood cell? What does a rising count of it suggest?
The neutrophil. A rising neutrophil count (neutrophilia), often with immature "bands," suggests a bacterial infection, because the marrow is releasing first responders.
5. Put the steps of phagocytosis in order and name the two internal vesicles formed.
Chemotaxis → adherence → ingestion → fusion → digestion → exocytosis; the microbe is enclosed in a phagosome, which fuses with a lysosome to form a phagolysosome. Enzymes and reactive oxygen inside the phagolysosome kill the microbe.
6. Define opsonization and name two opsonins. Why does it matter to a phagocyte?
Opsonization is coating a microbe with tags — antibody or complement C3b — that make it much easier to engulf. The tags act like an "eat me" label, so phagocytes adhere to and ingest the microbe far faster.
7. List the four cardinal signs of inflammation and match each to its mechanism.
Redness and heat = vasodilation (more warm blood); swelling = fluid leaking from permeable vessels (edema); pain = pressure on nerves plus sensitizing mediators. Histamine from mast cells is the central driver of the vascular changes.
8. A patient's local wound inflammation progresses to fever, low blood pressure, and a fast heart rate. What dangerous process should you suspect, and why is it so deadly?
Sepsis — a body-wide inflammatory response to infection. The same vasodilation and vascular leak that heal a local wound, unleashed system-wide, drop the blood pressure and starve organs of oxygen. Early recognition is critical.
9. Distinguish exogenous from endogenous pyrogens, and name the hypothalamic mediator through which they act.
Exogenous pyrogens come from outside the body (classically LPS/endotoxin of Gram-negative bacteria); endogenous pyrogens are the patient's own cytokines (IL-1, IL-6, TNF). Both raise the hypothalamic set-point via prostaglandin E₂.
10. A patient is shivering and feels cold at the start of a fever. What is happening to the set-point, and why is loading on blankets counterproductive?
The set-point has jumped up, so the body shivers and constricts vessels to climb toward the new, higher target (the onset/chill phase). The temperature is already rising; adding blankets to stop the shivering only helps it climb higher.
11. State the three outcomes of complement activation and the key player in each.
Opsonization (C3b coats the microbe), inflammation (C3a and C5a are anaphylatoxins/chemotactic), and cytolysis (the membrane attack complex, C5b–C9, lyses the microbe). All three pathways converge on C3. "Tag, recruit, destroy."
12. How do interferons protect the body against a virus, and why do they make you feel sick?
A virus-infected cell releases type I interferon that warns neighboring cells to enter an antiviral state — degrading viral genetic material and halting protein synthesis so the virus finds no factory to hijack. Interferons themselves cause much of the fever, aches, and malaise of a viral illness.
Microbiology · Chapter 13

Adaptive Immunity & Immunization

Your innate defenses are fast, but they are blunt — they treat every microbe the same and they never learn. Adaptive immunity is the opposite: slow to start, exquisitely precise, and blessed with memory. It is the arm of the immune system that recognizes one specific microbe out of millions, mounts a tailored attack, and then remembers that microbe for years or a lifetime. This chapter follows the two great cell lineages that make it work — T cells and B cells — explains how antibodies are built and what they do, and shows why a second exposure is so much faster than the first. That last fact is the whole reason vaccines work, so we finish where nursing practice lives: the immunizations you will draw up, document, and defend to families.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Contrast innate and adaptive immunity, and name the three hallmarks of the adaptive response: specificity, memory, and self-tolerance.
  • Define antigen and epitope, and explain what it means to distinguish self from non-self.
  • Distinguish helper (CD4) from cytotoxic (CD8) T cells and describe the role of MHC molecules in showing antigen to them.
  • Describe how a B cell becomes a plasma cell, and diagram the basic antibody structure.
  • List the five antibody classes and the main jobs antibodies do — neutralization, opsonization, agglutination, and complement activation.
  • Compare the primary and secondary antibody responses and explain immunologic memory.
  • Classify immunity as active or passive and natural or artificial, and match each to a clinical example.
  • Describe the major vaccine types and the nursing implications of live versus non-live vaccines.

Section 1The adaptive response

Two systems, one defense

Your immune system runs on two teams that work together. Innate immunity — skin, mucus, stomach acid, fever, phagocytes, and inflammation — is present from birth, responds within minutes to hours, and reacts the same way to almost any invader. It buys time. Adaptive immunity is the specialist team called in behind it. It takes days to gear up the first time, but it recognizes one particular microbe with extraordinary precision, and once the fight is over it keeps a record so that the next encounter is faster and stronger. Nearly everything protective about a vaccine, a childhood illness you never catch twice, and a positive antibody titer comes from this second system.

Adaptive immunity itself splits into two branches. The humoral (antibody-mediated) response is run by B lymphocytes, which secrete antibodies into blood and body fluids to attack microbes outside of cells — bacteria in the bloodstream, toxins, viruses in transit. The cell-mediated response is run by T lymphocytes, which handle threats inside cells, especially virus-infected cells and cancer cells. Both branches begin when the body detects something foreign.

Antigens, epitopes, and the meaning of “foreign”

An antigen is any molecule the immune system can recognize and respond to — usually a protein or polysaccharide on the surface of a microbe. Adaptive cells do not read the whole antigen at once; each one recognizes a small, specific patch called an epitope. A single bacterium carries many different epitopes, which is why one infection can trigger many different lymphocytes at the same time. The precision here is staggering: your body pre-builds millions of different lymphocytes, each tuned to a different epitope, before it has ever met the microbe. When a matching antigen shows up, the rare lymphocyte that fits it is selected and told to multiply — a process called clonal selection.

Specificity, memory, and tolerance

Three properties define adaptive immunity. Specificity means each response is aimed at one antigen and not its neighbors — measles immunity does nothing for mumps. Memory means the system remembers a past antigen and reacts faster and harder on re-exposure; this is the property vaccines exploit. Self-tolerance means the system learns, during development, to ignore the body's own molecules (self) while attacking foreign ones (non-self). Lymphocytes that would attack self are normally deleted or silenced early on. When that quality-control step fails, the immune system turns on the body — the basis of autoimmune disease such as type 1 diabetes, rheumatoid arthritis, and lupus.

FeatureInnate immunityAdaptive immunity
Present when?From birth, always readyDevelops after exposure to an antigen
SpeedMinutes to hoursDays on first exposure
SpecificityBroad — same response to many microbesExquisite — one antigen at a time
Memory?No — reacts the same every timeYes — faster & stronger on re-exposure
Key cellsPhagocytes, NK cells, mast cellsT lymphocytes and B lymphocytes
Why the second time is easier. A patient who shrugs off a cold that flattened them last year, or a child who never catches chickenpox twice, is showing you adaptive memory in action. Vaccines are simply a way to buy that memory without the dangerous first illness — you teach the immune system the microbe's face using a harmless stand-in.

Interactive Tap the three hallmarks of adaptive immunity — specificity, memory, and self vs non-self Open full ↑
Key points
  • Innate immunity is fast and non-specific; adaptive immunity is slower, highly specific, and remembers.
  • Adaptive immunity has two arms: humoral (B cells, antibodies, extracellular threats) and cell-mediated (T cells, intracellular threats).
  • An antigen is recognized at a small patch called an epitope; clonal selection multiplies the lymphocyte that fits.
  • The three hallmarks are specificity, memory, and self-tolerance; loss of tolerance causes autoimmunity.

Section 2T cells

Where T cells come from and how they “see”

T lymphocytes are born in the bone marrow but finish their training in the thymus (the T is for thymus), where the dangerous ones — those that would attack self — are weeded out. A mature T cell cannot recognize a free-floating antigen the way an antibody can. It only reacts to antigen that has been chopped up and displayed on a special surface molecule called MHC (major histocompatibility complex). Think of MHC as a serving tray: a cell holds up a fragment of protein on its MHC, and a passing T cell inspects it. This is called antigen presentation, and it is the reason T cells can tell an infected cell from a healthy one.

There are two classes of tray. MHC class I is found on almost every nucleated cell in the body and displays fragments of whatever is being made inside — so a virus-infected cell involuntarily advertises the virus. MHC class II is found only on professional antigen-presenting cells (dendritic cells, macrophages, and B cells), which deliberately swallow microbes and display their pieces to recruit help.

Helper T cells — the conductors (CD4)

Helper T cells carry a surface marker called CD4 and read antigen presented on MHC class II. They do not kill anything directly; they orchestrate. When a helper T cell recognizes its antigen, it releases chemical messengers (cytokines) that switch on the rest of the response: they push B cells to make antibody, amplify cytotoxic T cells, and rev up macrophages. Because they sit at the center of the network, losing them is catastrophic — which is exactly what makes HIV so devastating.

Cytotoxic T cells — the assassins (CD8)

Cytotoxic T cells carry CD8 and read antigen on MHC class I. When one recognizes a cell displaying foreign (viral or tumor) protein on its class I tray, it delivers the kill: it punches holes in the target and triggers it to self-destruct (apoptosis), taking the virus factory down with it. This is the front line against viral infections and abnormal cells. A handy memory aid is the “rule of eight”: CD4 × MHC II = 8, and CD8 × MHC I = 8 — each T-cell type pairs with the MHC class whose number multiplies to eight.

FeatureHelper T cellCytotoxic T cell
Surface markerCD4CD8
Reads antigen onMHC class IIMHC class I
Main jobCoordinate & amplify the response via cytokinesDirectly kill infected or abnormal cells
Best againstDirecting all responses (works with B cells too)Virus-infected cells, tumor cells
Clinical noteTarget of HIV; count tracks immune statusKey defense in viral illness
HIV attacks the conductor. HIV infects and destroys CD4 helper T cells. As the CD4 count falls, the whole adaptive network loses its coordinator, and the patient becomes vulnerable to opportunistic infections that a healthy immune system would never allow. That is why the CD4 count is the headline lab value in HIV care, and why a count below 200 defines AIDS — the number tells you how much of the orchestra is left.

Interactive Switch between CD4 helper and CD8 cytotoxic T cells and tap each feature to compare their roles Open full ↑
Key points
  • T cells mature in the thymus and recognize antigen only when it is presented on MHC.
  • Helper T cells (CD4) read MHC II and coordinate the response with cytokines; they do not kill directly.
  • Cytotoxic T cells (CD8) read MHC I and destroy infected or abnormal cells.
  • MHC I is on nearly all cells; MHC II is only on antigen-presenting cells. HIV depletes CD4 cells, crippling adaptive immunity.

Section 3B cells & antibodies

From B cell to antibody factory

B lymphocytes mature in the bone marrow (a helpful coincidence: B for bone marrow). Each B cell carries a unique receptor on its surface, and when that receptor binds its matching antigen — usually with a push from a helper T cell — the B cell activates and divides. Most of its offspring become plasma cells, short-lived factories that pump out thousands of identical antibodies per second. A smaller share become memory B cells that linger for years, ready for a repeat encounter (the subject of the next section). Antibodies are also called immunoglobulins (Ig), and they are the working product of the humoral response.

Antibody structure: the Y

Every antibody has the same basic shape: a Y built from four protein chains — two identical long heavy chains and two identical short light chains, held together by disulfide bonds. The two tips of the Y are the variable regions: their amino-acid sequence differs from antibody to antibody, giving each its unique antigen-binding site. The stem of the Y is the constant region, which is the same within a class and acts as a handle that other immune components — phagocytes and complement proteins — can grab. So one end of the molecule does the recognizing and the other end does the recruiting.

What antibodies actually do

Antibodies rarely destroy a microbe by themselves. Instead they tag and disable it in four main ways. In neutralization, antibodies coat a virus or toxin so it can no longer attach to host cells — this is how antitoxins and many vaccine antibodies protect. In opsonization, antibodies coat a microbe so phagocytes grab it more easily (the constant stem is the “handle”). In agglutination, antibodies cross-link many microbes into clumps that are easier to clear and can no longer spread. And in complement activation, clustered antibodies trigger the complement cascade, which can punch holes in bacterial membranes. The five classes below differ in their constant stems, and therefore in where they go and what they do.

ClassWhere / roleClinical pearl
IgGMost abundant in blood; long-term protection; crosses the placentaSignals a past or resolved infection / established immunity; protects the newborn
IgMFirst antibody made in a new infection; large, stays in bloodA positive IgM suggests a current or recent infection
IgAGuards mucous membranes & secretions; found in breast milkPasses protection to the breastfeeding infant's gut
IgEBinds mast cells; drives allergy & parasite defenseThe antibody behind hay fever and anaphylaxis
IgDOn the surface of naive B cellsHelps activate the B cell; least understood clinically
IgM says “now,” IgG says “before.” Serology often reports both. Detecting IgM against a microbe points to an acute or recent infection, because IgM shows up first and fades. Detecting IgG points to a past infection or established immunity, because IgG persists. When you order a rubella or hepatitis titer, this is the logic you are reading — and it is why IgG, not IgM, is what crosses the placenta to protect a newborn for its first few months.

Interactive Tap each part of the Y-shaped antibody to see its structure and how the paratope grips an antigen's epitope Open full ↑
Key points
  • B cells mature in bone marrow; activated B cells become antibody-secreting plasma cells and long-lived memory B cells.
  • An antibody is a Y: variable tips recognize antigen; the constant stem recruits phagocytes and complement.
  • Antibodies work by neutralization, opsonization, agglutination, and complement activation.
  • The five classes are IgG, IgM, IgA, IgE, IgD; remember IgM = new infection, IgG = past/lasting, IgA = mucosa/milk, IgE = allergy.

Section 4Primary vs secondary response

The primary response: slow and costly

The first time the body meets a new antigen, it must find the one rare lymphocyte that fits, activate it, and let it multiply before any meaningful defense appears. This primary response has a lag of roughly one to two weeks, produces a modest amount of antibody, and is led by IgM, the first-responder class, before IgG builds up. During that lag the patient is often sick — the immune system is fighting from behind. This delay is exactly why a brand-new infection can be dangerous and why an unvaccinated person has no head start.

Memory cells: the immune system's notebook

As the primary response resolves, it does not simply pack up. It leaves behind memory B cells and memory T cells — long-lived cells, sometimes surviving for decades, that carry the receptor for that one antigen. They circulate quietly, primed. Memory is the physical basis of immunity: it is what “having had” a disease or a vaccine actually means at the cellular level.

The secondary response: fast and fierce

When the same antigen returns, memory cells are already waiting, so the body skips most of the search-and-activate delay. The secondary response begins in hours to a few days, produces far more antibody, and is dominated by high-affinity IgG. Often the microbe is cleared before symptoms even appear — which is why you may never know you were re-exposed. Every booster shot deliberately triggers a secondary response to push antibody levels back up and refresh the memory pool.

FeaturePrimary responseSecondary response
TriggerFirst exposure to the antigenRe-exposure to the same antigen
Lag before antibody~1–2 weeksHours to a few days
Amount of antibodyLowMuch higher
Dominant classIgM first, then IgGMostly high-affinity IgG
Driven byNaive lymphocytesMemory B and T cells
Titers and boosters are memory checks. When a health system checks a nurse's hepatitis B titer, or confirms rubella immunity before pregnancy, it is measuring the antibody that immunologic memory left behind. If the titer is low, a booster reawakens the memory cells and drives a rapid secondary response — far easier than starting the whole series over. This same principle is why tetanus prophylaxis after a dirty wound is a booster, not a fresh primary series, in someone previously vaccinated.

Interactive Drag the slider across the timeline to watch antibody titer rise, fall, and surge 10-100x higher on re-exposure Open full ↑
Key points
  • The primary response is slow (1–2 week lag), small, and IgM-first.
  • Memory B and T cells persist for years and are the physical basis of lasting immunity.
  • The secondary response is faster, larger, and IgG-dominant — often clearing the microbe before symptoms appear.
  • Boosters and titers both work through this memory: one refreshes it, the other measures it.

Section 5Immunization & vaccines

Four kinds of immunity

Clinical immunity is sorted along two axes. The first is active versus passive: in active immunity the person's own immune system makes the antibody and builds memory (long-lasting); in passive immunity ready-made antibody is given to the person, protecting them immediately but only until that borrowed antibody breaks down (no memory). The second axis is natural versus artificial: natural means it happened through ordinary life, artificial means it was delivered deliberately by a clinician. Crossing the two axes gives four categories, and every immunization you handle fits one of them.

TypeHow it happensExampleLasting?
Natural activeYou catch the illness and recoverImmunity after chickenpoxYes — memory forms
Artificial activeA vaccine teaches your immune systemMMR, hepatitis B, tetanus vaccineYes — memory forms
Natural passiveAntibody given by natureMother's IgG across placenta; IgA in breast milkNo — wears off in months
Artificial passivePre-made antibody injectedAntivenom, rabies/tetanus immune globulin, RhoGAMNo — temporary

How vaccines are built

All vaccines do the same job — show the immune system an antigen so it builds memory — but they differ in how they present it, and that difference drives the nursing precautions. Live attenuated vaccines (MMR, varicella, oral polio, intranasal flu) use a weakened but living microbe; they give strong, long-lasting immunity but are generally contraindicated in pregnancy and in immunocompromised patients because even a weakened live microbe can be risky. Inactivated (killed) vaccines (hepatitis A, injectable polio, most flu shots) use a dead microbe — safer, but often needing boosters. Subunit, conjugate, and toxoid vaccines (hepatitis B, Hib, pertussis, tetanus and diphtheria toxoids) use only a purified piece or an inactivated toxin, so they cannot cause the disease at all. mRNA and viral-vector vaccines deliver the genetic instructions for a single antigen so the body makes it briefly and learns to recognize it. None of the non-live types can give the patient the actual infection.

Herd immunity and the nurse's role

When a high enough share of a community is immune, a microbe can no longer find enough susceptible hosts to spread — this is herd immunity, and it shelters the few who cannot be vaccinated (newborns, the immunocompromised, those with true contraindications). Nurses are central to keeping that number high: you administer vaccines by the correct route, observe for the rare immediate reaction, document the lot number and site, and — often most important — you answer families' questions with accurate, calm information. Vaccine hesitancy is met at the bedside far more effectively than in a headline.

Live vaccines have a guest list. Before giving a live vaccine (MMR, varicella, live intranasal flu), confirm the patient is not pregnant and not significantly immunocompromised — these are the classic contraindications, because a weakened-but-living microbe can still cause trouble in a vulnerable host. Inactivated, subunit, toxoid, and mRNA vaccines carry no such risk of infection and are the safer choice in those patients. When in doubt, live-virus vaccine plus pregnancy or immunosuppression is the pairing to stop and check.

Interactive Tap the four boxes of the active/passive × natural/artificial grid to compare vaccines, maternal antibodies, and immune globulin Open full ↑
Key points
  • Active immunity = the body makes its own antibody & memory (lasting); passive = borrowed antibody, immediate but temporary.
  • Natural vs artificial is simply whether it happened through life or was delivered by a clinician — giving four categories.
  • Vaccine types: live attenuated, inactivated, subunit/conjugate/toxoid, and mRNA/vector; only live types carry an infection risk in vulnerable hosts.
  • Live vaccines are generally contraindicated in pregnancy and immunocompromise; herd immunity protects those who cannot be vaccinated.

The chapter in one breath

  • Adaptive immunity is slow but specific and remembers; its arms are humoral (B cells/antibodies) and cell-mediated (T cells).
  • Its hallmarks are specificity, memory, and self-tolerance; lost tolerance causes autoimmunity.
  • T cells read antigen on MHC: helper (CD4) cells coordinate via cytokines; cytotoxic (CD8) cells kill infected cells. HIV destroys CD4 cells.
  • B cells become plasma cells that secrete antibodies — Y-shaped, with variable tips that recognize and a constant stem that recruits.
  • Antibodies work by neutralization, opsonization, agglutination, and complement; classes are IgG, IgM, IgA, IgE, IgD.
  • The secondary response is faster and stronger than the primary thanks to memory cells — the basis of boosters and titers.
  • Immunity is active/passive and natural/artificial; vaccines create artificial active immunity, and live vaccines are avoided in pregnancy and immunocompromise.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Active immunity
Protection produced by the person's own immune system making antibody and memory; long-lasting.
Adaptive immunity
The specific, memory-forming arm of the immune system, run by T and B lymphocytes.
Antibody (immunoglobulin)
A Y-shaped protein made by plasma cells that binds a specific antigen to tag or neutralize it.
Antigen
Any molecule the immune system can recognize and respond to; recognized at a small patch called an epitope.
Antigen-presenting cell (APC)
A cell (dendritic cell, macrophage, or B cell) that displays antigen on MHC class II to activate helper T cells.
Attenuated vaccine
A vaccine using a living but weakened microbe; strong immunity but avoided in pregnancy and immunocompromise.
CD4 (helper) T cell
A T cell that reads antigen on MHC II and coordinates the immune response with cytokines; target of HIV.
CD8 (cytotoxic) T cell
A T cell that reads antigen on MHC I and kills infected or abnormal cells.
Clonal selection
The process by which the one lymphocyte matching an antigen is chosen and multiplied.
Epitope
The small, specific portion of an antigen that a lymphocyte or antibody actually binds.
Herd immunity
Protection of a whole community, including the non-immune, when enough members are immune to stop spread.
Humoral immunity
The antibody-mediated branch of adaptive immunity, driven by B cells; fights extracellular threats.
Immunologic memory
The persistence of memory cells that make a repeat response faster and stronger.
Memory cell
A long-lived B or T lymphocyte that persists after infection and enables the secondary response.
MHC (major histocompatibility complex)
Surface molecules that display antigen fragments to T cells; class I on most cells, class II on APCs.
Opsonization
Coating a microbe with antibody so phagocytes engulf it more readily.
Passive immunity
Protection from ready-made antibody given to the person; immediate but temporary, with no memory.
Plasma cell
An activated B cell that secretes large amounts of antibody.
Primary response
The slow, modest, IgM-first reaction to a first exposure to an antigen.
Secondary response
The fast, large, IgG-dominant reaction to re-exposure, driven by memory cells.
Self-tolerance
The immune system's learned ability to ignore the body's own molecules; its failure causes autoimmunity.
Toxoid vaccine
A vaccine made from an inactivated bacterial toxin, e.g., tetanus and diphtheria toxoids.

Check yourself

1. A patient asks why the flu “hit them so hard” the first year but barely registered the next. Using the hallmarks of adaptive immunity, explain.
Immunologic memory. The first exposure built memory B and T cells, so the second time the immune system mounted a fast, strong secondary response and cleared the virus before it caused much illness. Specificity means that protection applies only to that strain.
2. Distinguish the humoral and cell-mediated branches of adaptive immunity, including which cells and which threats each handles.
Humoral = B cells and antibodies, fighting extracellular threats (bacteria, toxins, viruses in transit); cell-mediated = T cells, fighting intracellular threats (virus-infected and abnormal cells). Both are specific and form memory.
3. What is the difference between an antigen and an epitope, and why does one microbe activate many different lymphocytes?
An antigen is the whole recognizable molecule; an epitope is the small specific patch a lymphocyte actually binds. Because a microbe carries many different epitopes, many differently-tuned lymphocytes can each recognize a different patch at once.
4. A T cell cannot respond to a free-floating antigen. What must happen first, and what molecule makes it possible?
The antigen must be processed and displayed on an MHC molecule — antigen presentation. The T-cell receptor only recognizes antigen shown on MHC, which is how T cells tell an infected cell from a healthy one.
5. Compare CD4 and CD8 T cells: which MHC class does each read, and what does each do?
CD4 helper T cells read MHC class II and coordinate the response with cytokines; CD8 cytotoxic T cells read MHC class I and directly kill infected or abnormal cells. Memory aid: CD4×MHC II = 8 and CD8×MHC I = 8.
6. Why does HIV cause such profound immune failure, and which lab value tracks it?
HIV destroys CD4 helper T cells, the coordinators of the entire adaptive response, so both antibody and cytotoxic responses collapse. The CD4 count tracks the damage; below 200 defines AIDS, and opportunistic infections appear.
7. Describe the basic structure of an antibody and say which part recognizes antigen and which part recruits other defenses.
A Y made of two heavy and two light chains; the variable tips form the antigen-binding sites (recognition), and the constant stem recruits phagocytes and complement. One end recognizes, the other end recruits.
8. Name the four main ways antibodies disable microbes.
Neutralization, opsonization, agglutination, and complement activation. Antibodies mostly tag and disable rather than kill directly — coating toxins/viruses, flagging microbes for phagocytes, clumping them, and triggering complement.
9. A serology result shows IgM positive and IgG negative for a virus. What does this suggest, and why?
A current or recent infection. IgM is the first antibody made and fades quickly, so its presence without IgG points to an early/acute infection; IgG rises later and persists, marking past infection or established immunity.
10. Why is the secondary response faster and stronger than the primary response?
Memory B and T cells from the first exposure are already present, so the body skips the search-and-activate lag. The result is antibody in hours to days, in far greater amount, dominated by high-affinity IgG — the principle behind booster shots.
11. A newborn is protected from some infections in its first months without ever being vaccinated. What type of immunity is this, and what is its source?
Natural passive immunity. Maternal IgG crossed the placenta (and IgA passes in breast milk), giving borrowed antibody that protects immediately but wears off in months, because the infant forms no memory of its own.
12. A pregnant patient is due for several vaccines. Which category of vaccine is generally contraindicated and why, and which types are safe?
Live attenuated vaccines (e.g., MMR, varicella) are generally contraindicated because a weakened but living microbe can pose a risk. Inactivated, subunit, toxoid, and mRNA vaccines carry no infection risk and are considered safe. The same caution applies to significantly immunocompromised patients.
Microbiology · Chapter 14

Immune Disorders

The immune system is your patient's greatest protector — and, when it misfires, one of their most dangerous threats. Sometimes it overreacts to something harmless, like pollen or a dose of penicillin, and the response itself does the damage. Sometimes it turns against the body's own tissues. Sometimes it is too weak to defend at all. And sometimes, when we deliberately introduce blood or an organ from another person, it attacks the very help we are trying to give. This chapter walks through these problems — hypersensitivity, allergy and anaphylaxis, autoimmunity, immunodeficiency, and transfusion and transplant reactions — and shows you what each looks like at the bedside and what nursing actions keep patients safe.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Describe what hypersensitivity is and distinguish the four types (I–IV) by mediator, timing, and mechanism.
  • Explain type I allergy from sensitization through IgE, mast-cell degranulation, and anaphylaxis.
  • State why epinephrine is the first-line treatment for anaphylaxis and recognize the emergency.
  • Explain how self-tolerance breaks down and name common autoimmune diseases and their targets.
  • Distinguish primary (congenital) from secondary (acquired) immunodeficiency.
  • Summarize the course of HIV/AIDS in terms of CD4 counts and opportunistic infections.
  • Explain ABO and Rh blood typing and the cause of a hemolytic transfusion reaction.
  • Distinguish the major kinds of graft and describe rejection and graft-versus-host disease.

Section 1Hypersensitivity: when defense becomes damage

An immune response that harms the host

A healthy immune response is protective — it clears an invader and then quiets down. A hypersensitivity reaction is an immune response that is exaggerated, misdirected, or inappropriate, so that the response itself injures the body's own tissues. The trigger may be genuinely dangerous (a microbe), harmless (pollen, a food, a drug), or even part of the patient (their own cells). What all hypersensitivities share is that the damage comes from the immune system's activity, not directly from the trigger.

Almost every hypersensitivity requires a first, silent exposure called sensitization. On that first encounter the immune system does not cause symptoms; it simply learns the antigen and builds a stockpile of antibodies or primed T cells against it. The dramatic reaction happens on a later exposure, when that pre-built machinery fires. This is why a patient can take a drug uneventfully once and react violently the second time — and why "no reaction last time" never guarantees safety.

The four types (Gell and Coombs)

In the 1960s Gell and Coombs sorted hypersensitivities into four types by the immune mechanism behind them. The first three are driven by antibodies; the fourth is driven by T cells. A classic memory aid is ACID: type I Anaphylactic/allergic, type II Cytotoxic, type III Immune-complex, type IV Delayed. Types I–III happen within minutes to hours because antibodies are already present; type IV takes a day or more because T cells must be recruited to the site.

TypeAlso calledMediatorTimingMechanism & example
IImmediate / allergic / anaphylacticIgE on mast cellsSeconds to minutesAllergen cross-links IgE → mast-cell degranulation. Hay fever, food allergy, anaphylaxis
IICytotoxicIgG / IgM vs. cell-surface antigenMinutes to hoursAntibody binds a cell, marking it for destruction (complement, phagocytes). Hemolytic transfusion reaction, HDN, autoimmune hemolytic anemia
IIIImmune-complexIgG in antigen–antibody complexesHoursSoluble complexes deposit in vessels, joints, kidneys and inflame them. Serum sickness, SLE, some glomerulonephritis
IVDelayed-type / cell-mediatedT cells (no antibody)1–3 daysSensitized T cells release cytokines, recruit macrophages. Contact dermatitis (poison ivy, nickel), TB skin test

Keep the antibody-versus-T-cell divide front of mind, because it explains the tempo you see clinically. If a patient breaks out in hives within minutes of a bee sting, you are watching type I. If a rash appears two days after a new topical cream, that slow onset points to type IV. Timing is a genuine diagnostic clue, not a trivia detail.

Why nurses care about all four

Each type maps to real bedside events. Type I is the drug allergy and the anaphylaxis you must be ready to treat in seconds. Type II is the mismatched blood transfusion you exist to prevent. Type III underlies flares of lupus and the joint and kidney damage of immune-complex disease. Type IV is the positive tuberculin skin test you read at 48–72 hours and the contact dermatitis a patient blames on their gloves or tape.

Timing tells you the type. Immediate (seconds–minutes) → think type I and be ready for anaphylaxis. Delayed by a day or more → think type IV. That single question — "how fast did it come on?" — steers your assessment and your urgency before any lab result returns.

Interactive Tap the four hypersensitivity types (I–IV) to compare their mediator, onset, and mechanism Open full ↑
Key points
  • Hypersensitivity is an immune response that damages the host's own tissues.
  • Most reactions need a silent sensitization exposure first; the reaction fires on a later exposure.
  • Types I–III are antibody-mediated (fast); type IV is T-cell-mediated (delayed 1–3 days).
  • Remember ACID: Anaphylactic, Cytotoxic, Immune-complex, Delayed.

Section 2Allergy and anaphylaxis

Sensitization first, reaction later

A type I reaction — what most people mean by "an allergy" — unfolds in two acts. During sensitization, a first exposure to an otherwise harmless allergen (pollen, peanut, penicillin, bee venom) is taken up and presented, and the immune system responds by making IgE antibody specific to it. That IgE then coats the surface of mast cells in the tissues and basophils in the blood, arming them. Nothing dramatic happens yet; the patient feels fine.

On the next exposure, the allergen bridges two adjacent IgE molecules on a mast cell — cross-linking them — and that is the trigger. Within seconds the mast cell undergoes degranulation, dumping preformed histamine and other mediators, and begins synthesizing leukotrienes and prostaglandins. Those mediators cause the familiar package: vasodilation (redness, warmth, dropping blood pressure), increased vascular permeability (swelling, hives), smooth-muscle contraction (bronchospasm, cramping), and increased secretions (runny nose, watery eyes).

From a local nuisance to a systemic emergency

Where the reaction happens decides how serious it is. A localized reaction stays near the point of contact: allergic rhinitis (hay fever), hives at a skin site, or asthma in the airways. A systemic reaction — anaphylaxis — occurs when mediators are released body-wide, usually because the allergen entered the bloodstream (an injected drug, an IV contrast agent, a sting, an ingested food). Anaphylaxis is a life-threatening emergency: widespread vasodilation causes blood pressure to crash (distributive shock), and airway swelling plus bronchospasm can choke off breathing within minutes.

FeatureLocalized allergyAnaphylaxis (systemic)
Where mediators actNear contact siteWhole body
Typical signsHives, itching, sneezing, wheezeHives plus hypotension, throat/airway swelling, stridor, wheeze, GI cramps, sense of doom
Main dangerDiscomfortAirway closure and shock — can kill in minutes
First-line treatmentAntihistamine; avoid triggerEpinephrine, IM, immediately

Recognizing and treating the emergency

The drug that reverses anaphylaxis is epinephrine, given intramuscularly into the outer thigh, and it must be given at the first sign — not after antihistamines, not after a wait-and-see. Epinephrine constricts blood vessels to restore blood pressure, relaxes airway smooth muscle to open breathing, and stabilizes mast cells. Antihistamines and corticosteroids are helpful adjuncts but are far too slow and too weak to be relied on alone. Alongside the drug, protect the airway, give oxygen, lay the patient flat with legs raised (unless breathing is easier sitting up), run IV fluids for the shock, and monitor closely — a biphasic reaction can return hours later even after the patient looks recovered.

Prevention is nursing work too. A documented allergy must be verified, banded, and honored on every medication pass; the single most common cause of a preventable drug reaction is a missed or ignored allergy history. For patients with severe allergies, teaching correct use of an epinephrine auto-injector — and to call emergency services and go to the hospital even after using it — is part of discharge.

Epinephrine first, and fast. In anaphylaxis, epinephrine is the only first-line drug — IM into the lateral thigh, repeated every 5–15 minutes as needed. Antihistamines treat the hives, not the killing parts (airway and blood pressure). When in doubt about whether a reaction is "bad enough," give the epinephrine; the risk of withholding it is far greater than the risk of giving it.

Interactive Step through the IgE and mast-cell cascade from first exposure to anaphylaxis Open full ↑
Key points
  • Type I allergy = sensitization (make IgE, arm mast cells) then a later reaction when allergen cross-links IgE.
  • Mast-cell degranulation releases histamine, causing vasodilation, swelling, bronchospasm, and secretions.
  • Anaphylaxis is systemic type I: airway swelling and distributive shock, potentially fatal in minutes.
  • Treat anaphylaxis with IM epinephrine immediately; watch for a biphasic return.

Section 3Autoimmunity: the body against itself

How self-tolerance breaks down

The immune system normally learns, early in life, to ignore the body's own molecules — a state called self-tolerance. Developing lymphocytes that react too strongly against self are deleted or disabled before they mature. Autoimmunity is the failure of that tolerance: self-reactive T cells or antibodies (autoantibodies) escape control and attack the body's own tissues, producing an autoimmune disease. The attack uses the same weapons as any hypersensitivity — often type II (autoantibodies against cell-surface targets), type III (immune complexes), or type IV (self-reactive T cells).

Why tolerance fails is usually a mix of factors. A genetic predisposition (many autoimmune diseases run in families and link to particular HLA/MHC types) sets the stage, and something in the environment — often an infection — pulls the trigger. One well-studied mechanism is molecular mimicry: a microbial antigen resembles a human protein closely enough that antibodies raised against the microbe also attack the body. The classic example is rheumatic fever, where antibodies against Streptococcus pyogenes cross-react with heart tissue. Autoimmune diseases also fall more often on women, and many wax and wane in flares and remissions.

Organ-specific versus systemic disease

It helps to sort autoimmune diseases by how much of the body they target. Organ-specific diseases aim autoantibodies or T cells at a single tissue — the thyroid, the pancreatic islets, the neuromuscular junction — so the damage and the symptoms are focused. Systemic diseases, like systemic lupus erythematosus, generate autoantibodies against widespread components (such as the cell nucleus), so they injure many organs at once: skin, joints, kidneys, blood, and heart.

DiseaseTargetEffectScope
Graves’ diseaseTSH receptor (stimulating antibody)Overactive thyroid (hyperthyroidism)Organ-specific
Hashimoto thyroiditisThyroid tissueUnderactive thyroid (hypothyroidism)Organ-specific
Type 1 diabetes mellitusPancreatic islet β-cellsNo insulin productionOrgan-specific
Myasthenia gravisAcetylcholine receptorMuscle weakness, ptosisOrgan-specific
Multiple sclerosisCNS myelin sheathNerve conduction failureOrgan-specific (CNS)
Rheumatoid arthritisJoint synoviumSymmetric joint inflammation, deformitySystemic
Systemic lupus erythematosusNuclear antigens (anti-nuclear antibodies)Skin, joints, kidney, blood, heartSystemic

Living with an autoimmune disease

There is no cure for most autoimmune diseases, so care aims to control the immune attack and manage what it has damaged. Treatment leans on immunosuppression — corticosteroids and other drugs that quiet the immune response — which brings its own hazard: a patient who is deliberately immunosuppressed is also more vulnerable to infection, blurring the line between an autoimmune problem and an immunodeficiency one. Some organ-specific diseases are managed by replacing what was lost rather than suppressing anything, as with insulin in type 1 diabetes or thyroid hormone in Hashimoto disease.

Immunosuppression is a double-edged sword. The drugs that calm an autoimmune flare also lower the wall against infection. A patient on high-dose steroids or a biologic can have a serious infection with only muted fever or few classic signs — so a small change in how they look or feel deserves a close second look, and infection precautions matter more, not less.

Interactive Tap six classic autoimmune diseases to see which self-tissue lost tolerance and how it is attacked Open full ↑
Key points
  • Autoimmunity is a loss of self-tolerance: autoantibodies or self-reactive T cells attack the body.
  • Causes combine genetic risk and an environmental trigger; molecular mimicry (e.g., rheumatic fever) is one route.
  • Diseases are organ-specific (Graves, Hashimoto, type 1 diabetes, myasthenia gravis, MS) or systemic (RA, SLE).
  • Care centers on immunosuppression or hormone replacement; immunosuppression raises infection risk.

Section 4Immunodeficiency and HIV/AIDS

When defenses are too weak

If hypersensitivity and autoimmunity are the immune system doing too much, immunodeficiency is it doing too little. A part of the immune system is missing or not working, so the patient cannot clear infections that a healthy person would shrug off — and they become prey to opportunistic organisms, microbes that rarely trouble the immunocompetent. Immunodeficiencies split into two groups by origin.

Primary immunodeficiencies are congenital — the person is born with a genetic defect in some component of immunity. They range from mild (selective IgA deficiency, often silent) to catastrophic. The most severe is severe combined immunodeficiency (SCID), in which both B cells and T cells are absent or nonfunctional; affected infants have almost no defense and, without treatment such as a bone-marrow transplant, do not survive infancy — the origin of the "bubble" isolation once used to protect them.

Secondary (acquired) immunodeficiencies are far more common and develop after birth from some external cause: malnutrition (the leading cause worldwide), certain cancers (especially leukemias and lymphomas), chemotherapy and other immunosuppressive drugs, radiation, aging, and viral infection — most importantly HIV. Many of your patients are secondarily immunodeficient by treatment: anyone on chemotherapy, high-dose steroids, or anti-rejection drugs.

FeaturePrimary immunodeficiencySecondary immunodeficiency
OriginCongenital (genetic)Acquired after birth
OnsetUsually infancy/childhoodAny age
ExamplesSCID, selective IgA deficiency, DiGeorge syndromeHIV/AIDS, malnutrition, chemotherapy, leukemia, steroids
FrequencyRareCommon — many hospitalized patients

HIV and AIDS

The human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) is a retrovirus that targets the very cell that coordinates the whole immune response: the CD4 helper T cell. Over years, HIV steadily infects and destroys these cells. As the CD4 count falls, the immune system loses its conductor, and the patient becomes progressively unable to fight off infection. HIV is transmitted through blood, sexual contact, and from mother to child in pregnancy, birth, or breastfeeding — not through casual contact.

AIDS (acquired immunodeficiency syndrome) is the advanced stage of HIV infection, defined by a CD4 count that falls below 200 cells/µL (normal is roughly 500–1,500) or by the appearance of an AIDS-defining opportunistic illness — infections and cancers that a healthy immune system holds at bay, such as Pneumocystis jirovecii pneumonia, Candida of the esophagus, disseminated tuberculosis, cytomegalovirus, and Kaposi sarcoma. The lower the CD4 count, the wider the range of opportunists that can take hold.

The story is far more hopeful than it once was. Antiretroviral therapy (ART) — combinations of drugs that block different steps of the viral life cycle — can suppress HIV to undetectable levels, preserve the CD4 count, prevent progression to AIDS, and render the virus untransmissible sexually. HIV has become, for many, a chronic manageable condition. Nursing priorities include supporting adherence to a lifelong regimen, watching for opportunistic infections, and applying standard precautions consistently — the same barrier practices that protect against all bloodborne pathogens.

The immunocompromised patient hides infection. Without a full immune response, the classic signs — high fever, pus, a hot red wound — may be blunted or absent, so a serious infection can look deceptively mild. In a neutropenic or HIV patient, treat even a low-grade fever or subtle change as potentially serious, and guard them fiercely: meticulous hand hygiene and protective precautions are lifesaving because their own defenses cannot fill the gap.

Interactive Compare primary vs secondary immunodeficiency, then drag the HIV timeline to watch CD4 T cells fall and opportunistic infections take hold Open full ↑
Key points
  • Immunodeficiency = too little immune function, opening the door to opportunistic infections.
  • Primary = congenital (e.g., SCID); secondary = acquired (HIV, malnutrition, chemotherapy, steroids) and far more common.
  • HIV destroys CD4 helper T cells; AIDS = CD4 < 200 cells/µL or an AIDS-defining opportunistic illness.
  • ART suppresses HIV and preserves immunity; nurses support adherence and use standard precautions.

Section 5Transfusion and transplant reactions

ABO and Rh: the blood you can safely give

Red blood cells carry surface molecules that the immune system reads as antigens. The most important set is the ABO system: type A cells carry the A antigen, type B carry B, type AB carry both, and type O carry neither. Crucially, each person naturally makes antibodies against the ABO antigen they lack — a type A person has anti-B antibodies, a type O person has both anti-A and anti-B. This is why blood must be matched: transfusing the wrong ABO type sends antibody against donor red cells, and they are destroyed.

A second system, Rh, adds "positive" or "negative": Rh-positive cells carry the D antigen, Rh-negative cells do not. Unlike ABO, anti-Rh antibodies are not present from birth — an Rh-negative person makes them only after being exposed to Rh-positive blood. Because type O-negative blood carries none of these antigens, it is the emergency universal donor for red cells; type AB-positive, carrying no anti-A/anti-B antibodies, is the universal recipient.

Blood typeAntigens on red cellsAntibodies in plasmaCan receive red cells from
AAanti-BA, O
BBanti-AB, O
ABA and BnoneA, B, AB, O (universal recipient)
Ononeanti-A and anti-BO only (universal donor)

When a transfusion goes wrong

An acute hemolytic transfusion reaction is a type II hypersensitivity and a medical emergency, almost always caused by an ABO mismatch — usually a clerical or identification error at the bedside. The recipient's antibodies bind the transfused donor cells and destroy (hemolyze) them, triggering fever, chills, flank or back pain, dark urine, hypotension, and potentially kidney failure and death. The nurse's response is immediate: stop the transfusion, keep the IV line open with saline, and notify the provider and blood bank. Because these reactions come from misidentification, the two-person bedside verification of patient and unit is not paperwork — it is the actual safeguard against a fatal error.

A related problem crosses the placenta. Hemolytic disease of the newborn (HDN) occurs when an Rh-negative mother, sensitized to Rh-positive blood in an earlier pregnancy, makes anti-Rh antibodies that cross into a subsequent Rh-positive fetus and destroy its red cells. It is largely preventable: giving Rh immune globulin (RhoGAM) to Rh-negative mothers stops them from becoming sensitized in the first place.

Transplants and rejection

Transplanting tissue raises the same problem in a different form: the recipient's immune system recognizes donor cells as foreign (chiefly by their MHC/HLA molecules) and attacks the graft. How much it attacks depends on how closely donor and recipient are matched. An autograft (self to self, like a skin graft) and an isograft (between identical twins) are not rejected. An allograft (between two genetically different people — the usual organ transplant) is rejected unless the immune response is suppressed, and a xenograft (between species) faces the strongest rejection of all.

Rejection comes in three tempos: hyperacute (within minutes, from preexisting antibodies), acute (days to weeks, T-cell mediated), and chronic (months to years). To keep an allograft alive, recipients take immunosuppressant drugs for life — which, as ever, trades rejection risk for infection risk. One further danger runs the opposite direction: in a bone-marrow or stem-cell transplant, the graft's immune cells can attack the recipient's body — graft-versus-host disease (GVHD).

The bedside check is the last line of defense. Nearly every fatal hemolytic transfusion reaction traces back to giving the right blood to the wrong patient. The two-nurse verification of the patient's identity against the blood unit, done at the bedside right before hanging, is precisely the step that catches the error — never let it become a rushed formality, and stay with the patient during the first 15 minutes when a reaction is most likely to declare itself.

Interactive Tap any patient-and-donor square in the ABO/Rh grid to see if the transfusion is safe and why Open full ↑
Key points
  • ABO: you make antibodies against the antigen you lack; O-negative is the universal red-cell donor, AB-positive the universal recipient.
  • An acute hemolytic transfusion reaction is a type II reaction from ABO mismatch — stop the transfusion at once.
  • HDN comes from Rh incompatibility and is prevented with Rh immune globulin.
  • Grafts: autograft/isograft not rejected, allograft needs immunosuppression; GVHD is the graft attacking the host.

The chapter in one breath

  • Hypersensitivity is an immune response that harms the host; the four types differ by mediator and timing (I–III antibody, IV T-cell).
  • Type I allergy runs from sensitization and IgE to mast-cell degranulation; systemic release is anaphylaxis, treated with IM epinephrine immediately.
  • Autoimmunity is lost self-tolerance — organ-specific (Graves, type 1 diabetes, myasthenia gravis) or systemic (RA, SLE).
  • Immunodeficiency is too little immunity: primary (congenital, e.g., SCID) or secondary (acquired — HIV, chemotherapy, malnutrition).
  • HIV destroys CD4 T cells; AIDS is CD4 < 200/µL or an opportunistic illness; ART controls it.
  • ABO/Rh matching prevents the type II hemolytic transfusion reaction; identification errors are the cause, so the bedside check is the safeguard.
  • Grafts are rejected by degree of mismatch; allografts need lifelong immunosuppression, and GVHD is the graft attacking the host.

ReferenceWords worth knowing

A quick reference for the terms in this chapter — skim it now, or circle back whenever a word trips you up.

Allergen
An otherwise harmless antigen that provokes a type I (allergic) hypersensitivity response.
Allograft
A transplant between two genetically different individuals of the same species; the usual organ transplant, requiring immunosuppression.
Anaphylaxis
A severe, systemic type I reaction with airway swelling and distributive shock; a life-threatening emergency treated with epinephrine.
Autoantibody
An antibody directed against the body's own tissues.
Autoimmunity
A failure of self-tolerance in which the immune system attacks the body's own cells and tissues.
CD4 helper T cell
The coordinating lymphocyte of the immune response and the primary target of HIV.
Graft-versus-host disease (GVHD)
A reaction in which transplanted (graft) immune cells attack the recipient's tissues, seen with bone-marrow/stem-cell transplants.
Hemolytic disease of the newborn (HDN)
Destruction of a fetus's red cells by maternal anti-Rh antibodies; prevented with Rh immune globulin.
Hemolytic transfusion reaction
A type II reaction from an ABO-mismatched transfusion; the recipient's antibodies destroy donor red cells.
Hypersensitivity
An exaggerated or misdirected immune response that damages the host's own tissues.
IgE
The antibody class that arms mast cells and basophils and mediates type I hypersensitivity.
Immunodeficiency
A deficient immune response; primary (congenital) or secondary (acquired).
Mast cell
A tissue cell coated with IgE that releases histamine and other mediators when it degranulates.
Molecular mimicry
A microbial antigen resembling a self-protein, so anti-microbe antibodies also attack the body (e.g., rheumatic fever).
Opportunistic infection
An infection by a normally harmless microbe that takes hold when host defenses are weakened.
Primary immunodeficiency
A congenital, genetically based defect in immune function, such as SCID.
Rh factor
The D red-cell antigen; Rh-positive cells carry it, Rh-negative do not.
Secondary immunodeficiency
An acquired loss of immune function from an external cause (HIV, malnutrition, drugs, cancer).
Self-tolerance
The immune system's normal ability to recognize and not attack the body's own molecules.
Sensitization
The silent first exposure in which the immune system builds antibodies or primed cells against an antigen, setting up a later reaction.
Severe combined immunodeficiency (SCID)
A severe primary immunodeficiency lacking functional B cells and T cells.

Check yourself

1. A patient tolerated a drug the first time but reacted badly the second. Explain the concept that accounts for this.
Sensitization. The first exposure is silent — the immune system builds antibodies (or primed T cells) against the antigen. The reaction fires only on a later exposure, so "no reaction last time" never guarantees safety.
2. Which hypersensitivity types are antibody-mediated and which is cell-mediated, and how does that show up in timing?
Types I, II, and III are antibody-mediated (fast, minutes to hours); type IV is T-cell-mediated (delayed 1–3 days). Antibodies are already present so they act quickly; type IV must recruit T cells to the site, which takes a day or more — hence "delayed-type."
3. Walk through the steps of a type I reaction from first exposure to symptoms.
Sensitization: allergen → IgE made → IgE coats mast cells/basophils. Later exposure: allergen cross-links IgE → mast-cell degranulation → histamine and other mediators → vasodilation, swelling, bronchospasm, secretions. The symptoms come from the mediators, not the allergen itself.
4. A patient develops hives, throat tightness, wheezing, and falling blood pressure minutes after an IV antibiotic. What is happening and what is the first-line treatment?
Anaphylaxis (systemic type I). Give epinephrine intramuscularly into the outer thigh immediately. Also stop the trigger, protect the airway, give oxygen and IV fluids, and monitor for a biphasic reaction. Antihistamines are adjuncts, not the first-line drug.
5. Why are antihistamines not sufficient as the primary treatment for anaphylaxis?
They are too slow and too weak, and they treat the hives, not the killing components — airway swelling and shock. Only epinephrine restores blood pressure, opens the airway, and stabilizes mast cells fast enough to save the patient.
6. Define autoimmunity and explain molecular mimicry with an example.
Autoimmunity is a loss of self-tolerance in which the immune system attacks the body's own tissues. Molecular mimicry is when a microbial antigen resembles a self-protein, so antibodies against the microbe also attack the body. Classic example: rheumatic fever, where anti-Streptococcus pyogenes antibodies cross-react with heart tissue.
7. Contrast an organ-specific autoimmune disease with a systemic one, giving an example of each.
Organ-specific disease targets one tissue (e.g., type 1 diabetes attacks pancreatic β-cells; myasthenia gravis attacks the acetylcholine receptor). Systemic disease targets widespread components (e.g., SLE attacks nuclear antigens, injuring skin, joints, kidneys, and more).
8. Distinguish primary from secondary immunodeficiency and give an example of each.
Primary is congenital/genetic (e.g., SCID, present from birth). Secondary is acquired after birth from an external cause (e.g., HIV, malnutrition, chemotherapy, steroids). Secondary is far more common, and many hospitalized patients are secondarily immunodeficient by treatment.
9. What cell does HIV destroy, and how is AIDS defined?
HIV destroys CD4 helper T cells. AIDS is defined by a CD4 count below 200 cells/µL or the presence of an AIDS-defining opportunistic illness. Losing CD4 cells removes the coordinator of the immune response, leaving the patient open to opportunistic infections.
10. Why can a serious infection look deceptively mild in an immunocompromised patient, and what does that mean for nursing care?
Without a full immune response, classic signs (high fever, pus, redness) may be blunted or absent, so infection can hide. Treat even a low-grade fever or subtle change as potentially serious, and use meticulous hand hygiene and protective precautions because the patient's own defenses cannot fill the gap.
11. A patient begins a transfusion and quickly develops fever, chills, flank pain, and hypotension. What is the reaction, its immune type, and your first action?
An acute hemolytic transfusion reaction — a type II hypersensitivity, usually from an ABO mismatch. Stop the transfusion immediately, keep the line open with saline, and notify the provider and blood bank. These reactions come from patient-identification errors, which is why the bedside verification exists.
12. Rank an autograft, an allograft, and a xenograft by rejection risk, and explain why an allograft needs immunosuppression.
Autograft (self) is not rejected; allograft (different person, same species) is rejected without treatment; xenograft (different species) faces the strongest rejection. An allograft carries foreign MHC/HLA molecules the recipient's immune system attacks, so lifelong immunosuppressants are needed to keep it — at the cost of higher infection risk.
Microbiology · Chapter 15

Infectious Diseases by Body System

Microbes do not respect the neat chapter boundaries of a textbook — they land somewhere on a real patient and cause trouble that a nurse has to recognize, report, and help treat. This chapter reorganizes what you already know about pathogens around the place they strike: the skin, the lungs, the gut, the urinary and reproductive tracts, and the bloodstream and brain. For each region we will name the usual offenders, describe what the infection looks like at the bedside, and connect it to the assessments and precautions that keep one patient's infection from becoming the whole unit's problem. Think of it as a field guide — organized the way you actually meet infections on the floor.

By the end of this chapter you can

  • Recognize the common skin and wound infections — impetigo, cellulitis, and MRSA — and the assessment findings that flag them.
  • Distinguish the major respiratory infections (pneumonia, tuberculosis, influenza, strep throat) and the isolation precautions each requires.
  • Explain how gastrointestinal pathogens cause illness through infection versus toxin, and why C. difficile and norovirus demand soap-and-water hand hygiene.
  • Describe the pathogens and presentations of urinary tract infections and common sexually transmitted infections.
  • Recognize the warning signs of bacteremia, sepsis, and meningitis and the urgency each carries.
  • Match each body-system infection to its typical causative organism and route of transmission.
  • Connect bedside nursing actions — hand hygiene, device care, early recognition — to the prevention of healthcare-associated infection.

Section 1Skin & wound infections

The skin is a wall, until it isn't

Intact skin is one of the body's best defenses: a dry, slightly acidic, constantly shedding barrier that most microbes simply cannot cross. Skin and soft-tissue infections almost always begin where that wall is breached — a scratch, an insect bite, a surgical incision, a pressure injury, an IV site, or the cracked skin of eczema. Once organisms get past the surface, the two players you will meet again and again are Staphylococcus aureus and Streptococcus pyogenes (group A strep), both of which live harmlessly on skin and in noses until an opening lets them in.

What the infection looks like depends mostly on how deep it goes. Impetigo is a superficial infection of the outermost skin, common in children, producing fragile blisters that rupture into a golden, honey-colored crust. Cellulitis is deeper, involving the dermis and the fat beneath it: a spreading area of warmth, redness, swelling, and tenderness with poorly defined edges, often on a leg. Erysipelas is a brighter, sharply raised, well-demarcated cellulitis of the upper dermis, classically caused by group A strep. The deepest and most dangerous, necrotizing fasciitis, races along the tissue planes and produces pain out of proportion to what the skin shows — a surgical emergency, not an antibiotic-only problem.

MRSA and the infected wound

MRSA — methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus — is ordinary S. aureus that has acquired resistance to the entire penicillin family, so it shrugs off the drugs that would once have cured it. It is a leading cause of skin abscesses (patients often report a "spider bite" they never actually saw a spider make) and a notorious cause of surgical-site and device-related infections. MRSA is spread by contact — hands, shared equipment, contaminated surfaces — which is why colonized or infected patients are placed on contact precautions (gown and gloves) and why hand hygiene between patients is non-negotiable.

A wound infection announces itself with the classic signs of local inflammation — increasing redness, warmth, swelling, pain, and purulent (pus) drainage — sometimes with a spreading red border, foul odor, or fever. The single most useful nursing skill here is the ability to tell colonization from infection: nearly every open wound grows bacteria on a swab, but only a wound with the signs of tissue invasion needs antibiotics. A clean-looking chronic wound with a positive culture and no inflammation is usually colonized, and treating it does more harm than good.

InfectionDepthUsual causeHallmark finding
ImpetigoSuperficial epidermisS. aureus, group A strepHoney-colored crust
ErysipelasUpper dermisGroup A strepBright, raised, sharply bordered
CellulitisDermis & subcutaneous fatS. aureus, strepWarm, red, tender, ill-defined edges
Abscess / MRSALocalized pus pocketMRSA, S. aureusFluctuant lump; may need drainage
Necrotizing fasciitisFascia (surgical emergency)Group A strep, mixed floraPain out of proportion, rapid spread
Trace the edge with a pen. When you suspect cellulitis, mark the border of the redness with a skin marker and note the time. On the next assessment you (and the whole team) can see at a glance whether the infection is retreating on antibiotics or marching up the limb — an objective bedside measure that beats "looks about the same."
Interactive Tap four skin infections and watch how deep each one invades the skin Open full ↑
Key points
  • Skin infections start where the barrier is broken; S. aureus and group A strep are the usual causes.
  • Depth drives the picture: impetigo (superficial) → cellulitis (dermis) → necrotizing fasciitis (surgical emergency).
  • MRSA resists the penicillin family, spreads by contact, and warrants contact precautions.
  • A wound with spreading redness, warmth, pain, and pus is infected; a positive swab without those signs is usually just colonized.

Section 2Respiratory infections

Upper tract, lower tract

The respiratory system is split into an upper tract (nose, sinuses, throat, larynx) and a lower tract (trachea, bronchi, and the alveoli of the lungs), and the division matters because lower-tract infections are generally more serious. Upper-tract infections — the common cold, most sore throats, sinusitis — are usually viral and self-limited. The important exception is strep throat (streptococcal pharyngitis), caused by group A strep, which produces a sudden painful sore throat, fever, and swollen tonsils without the cough and runny nose of a cold. It matters because untreated it can lead to rheumatic fever, so it is one of the few sore throats worth culturing and treating with antibiotics.

The lungs are supposed to be nearly sterile, defended by the mucus blanket, the sweeping cilia, and the cough reflex. When those defenses fail — because a patient is intubated, sedated, weak, or aspirating — organisms reach the alveoli and cause pneumonia. Nurses classify pneumonia by where it was acquired: community-acquired pneumonia (CAP), most often from Streptococcus pneumoniae; hospital-acquired and ventilator-associated pneumonia (VAP), which involve tougher, more resistant organisms; and aspiration pneumonia, from inhaled mouth or stomach contents in patients with impaired swallowing or a depressed gag reflex.

Tuberculosis and influenza

Tuberculosis (TB), caused by the slow-growing acid-fast bacillus Mycobacterium tuberculosis, is the respiratory infection whose precautions you must never get wrong. TB spreads by tiny airborne droplet nuclei that hang in the air, so a suspected case goes into a negative-pressure airborne isolation room, and staff wear a fitted N95 respirator — not a surgical mask. Classic active TB brings a chronic productive cough (sometimes with blood), night sweats, fever, and weight loss. Treatment is long — months of multiple drugs — and the nurse's central role is supporting adherence, because stopping early is exactly how drug-resistant TB is bred.

Influenza is a viral lower-and-upper respiratory infection that arrives abruptly with fever, body aches, headache, and a dry cough. It spreads by droplets, so influenza patients are placed on droplet precautions (a surgical mask within a few feet), and because it is viral, antibiotics do nothing — antiviral drugs help only if started early. The most important nursing contribution against influenza happens before flu season: vaccination of patients and staff alike.

InfectionTypical causeTransmissionPrecaution
Strep throatGroup A strep (bacterial)Droplet/contactStandard (droplet if severe)
Community pneumoniaStreptococcus pneumoniaeDropletStandard/droplet
InfluenzaInfluenza virusDropletDroplet (surgical mask)
TuberculosisMycobacterium tuberculosisAirborneAirborne (N95 + negative pressure)
Droplet is not airborne — and the mask you grab proves you know it. Flu and bacterial pneumonia travel on large droplets that fall within a few feet: a surgical mask is enough. TB and measles travel on tiny nuclei that float: they demand a negative-pressure room and an N95. Reaching for the wrong mask is one of the most common — and most consequential — precaution errors on the floor.
Interactive Tap pneumonia, TB, influenza, or strep throat to see where each strikes in the airway and how it's caused and treated Open full ↑
Key points
  • Upper-tract infections are mostly viral; strep throat is the bacterial exception worth treating.
  • Pneumonia is classified by where it was acquired — community, hospital/ventilator, or aspiration — which predicts the likely organism.
  • TB is airborne: negative-pressure room and N95; its long therapy makes adherence a nursing priority.
  • Influenza is viral and droplet-spread; vaccination and droplet precautions matter more than antibiotics.

Section 3Gastrointestinal infections

Two ways the gut gets sick

Gastrointestinal (GI) pathogens almost all share one route of entry — the fecal-oral route, meaning microbes (or their toxins) from stool reach a new mouth by way of contaminated food, water, or hands. But they make you sick in two very different ways. In a true infection, the organism is swallowed, colonizes the gut, and invades or irritates the lining; symptoms take a day or more to appear because the microbe has to multiply first. In an intoxication, the microbe has already grown in the food and produced a toxin, and it is the pre-formed toxin you swallow — so symptoms hit fast, often within hours (the vomiting of Staphylococcus aureus food poisoning is the textbook example).

Foodborne illness is the umbrella term for all of this, and the usual suspects — Salmonella, Campylobacter, Shiga-toxin-producing E. coli, Norovirus — produce overlapping pictures of nausea, vomiting, crampy abdominal pain, and diarrhea that may be watery or bloody. For most healthy adults the illness is self-limited and the treatment is rehydration, not antibiotics. The nurse's clinical eye is on the vulnerable: infants, older adults, and immunocompromised patients, who can dehydrate dangerously, and on the warning signs of bloody diarrhea, which can signal the E. coli strain that damages the kidneys.

The two that spread on the unit

Clostridioides difficile (C. diff) is the infection you help cause if you are not careful with antibiotics. Broad-spectrum antibiotics flatten the normal gut flora, and this spore-forming organism blooms into severe, foul-smelling watery diarrhea and colitis. Two facts drive nursing practice: C. diff forms spores that alcohol hand rub does not kill, so you must wash with soap and water after caring for these patients; and the room needs a bleach-based (sporicidal) cleaner. Patients go on contact precautions, and new-onset diarrhea in anyone on antibiotics should raise the question immediately.

Norovirus is the explosive, highly contagious cause of "stomach flu" that shuts down cruise ships, long-term-care units, and hospital wards. It takes only a handful of viral particles to infect, it spreads by the fecal-oral route and by aerosolized vomit, and — like C. diff spores — it resists alcohol gel, so soap-and-water hand hygiene and bleach cleaning are again the rule. Sudden onset of vomiting and watery diarrhea sweeping through a group of people points straight at norovirus.

CauseMechanismOnsetKey nursing point
Salmonella/CampylobacterInfection (invasion)1–3 daysRehydrate; usually no antibiotics
S. aureus food poisoningPre-formed toxinHoursFast onset, short course
Shiga-toxin E. coliToxin + invasion2–4 daysBloody diarrhea; watch kidneys
C. difficileOvergrowth after antibioticsDays–weeksSoap & water; contact precautions
NorovirusInfection (very low dose)12–48 hoursSoap & water; extremely contagious
When it's C. diff or norovirus, the gel bottle lies to you. Alcohol hand rub feels clean and works beautifully against most bugs — but it does not touch C. diff spores or norovirus. After those patients, walk to the sink. The physical friction of soap and running water is what actually removes them.
Interactive Tap five common GI infections to trace what causes each one and how it spreads Open full ↑
Key points
  • GI pathogens spread by the fecal-oral route and cause illness by infection (slow onset) or pre-formed toxin (fast onset).
  • Most foodborne illness is self-limited; the mainstay is rehydration, and the risk is dehydration in the very young, old, and immunocompromised.
  • C. difficile follows antibiotic use, forms spores, and demands soap-and-water hand hygiene plus contact precautions.
  • Norovirus is intensely contagious, resists alcohol gel, and calls for the same soap-and-water and bleach-cleaning response.

Section 4Urogenital infections & STIs

Urinary tract infections

The urinary tract infection (UTI) is one of the most common infections nurses manage, and it is usually an inside job: the culprit is most often the patient's own Escherichia coli, harmless in the colon but troublesome once it climbs the short distance up the urethra into the bladder. Anatomy explains why women get far more UTIs than men — a shorter urethra sits closer to the anus. A lower UTI, or cystitis (bladder infection), brings dysuria (burning with urination), urinary frequency and urgency, and cloudy or foul-smelling urine. If the infection ascends to the kidneys — pyelonephritis — the patient turns systemically ill: fever, chills, and flank (back) pain over the kidney.

The version that belongs to nursing above all others is the catheter-associated UTI (CAUTI). An indwelling urinary catheter is a superhighway that lets bacteria bypass every normal defense and travel straight to the bladder, and the risk climbs with every day the catheter stays in. The single most effective prevention is simple and squarely a nursing responsibility: do not place a catheter unless it is truly indicated, and remove it the moment it is no longer needed. A subtle but crucial point — a positive urine culture in a patient with no symptoms (asymptomatic bacteriuria) is usually not treated, because treating it breeds resistance without helping the patient.

Sexually transmitted infections
